The Red Page
SONNETS OF CIVILISATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.
The old Earth lumbers on. Uncaring, she
Has Man's sloiv, staggering march to great-
ness known,
Has seen him —-from a grunting ape-thing
grown
To clouded knowledge of his destiny- —
liaise towered cities that musf ever be
By ruder tribes in triumph overthrown,
And seen on them, new civilisations sown
That effloresce and fade monotonously.
For Man the Earth has but a pitying scorn;
She knows that.after each experiment
lie will return to live with her again —
A. tribal farmer reaping scanty corn,
A hirsute hunter, happy in Ins den,
Who feasts and breeds and dies, and is con-
tent.
ii.
So many races lived, and left no trace
Save sonic strange skeleton —in strata deep
Close, to his Mother's heart he slept his sleep
Forgotten centimes in her embrace.
Of him we wonder vaguely for a space:
His (drawings on his cave-walls for us keep
Their eerie beauty, and with awe we peep
At the proud art of that long-vanished race.
The Neanderthal, that grim, gorilla-brute,
Who lorried, once the Earth ivith massive
• ! brain 1 -'— ■'
What slew this intellect as ours acute?
And the Crd-Magnon, man of mighty worth
Of mind and art —who closed his glorious
: reifjn?
Wh'at wiped this splendid giant from the
Earth?
if ■
id • III.
The endless struggle that Mankind has made
To, %ift itself a little from the ground!
TM ceaseless cycle, aye with failure crowned,
Fitym cave to city, till its glories fade,
TMt restless itch of Progress is allayed.
Aha Time smoothes down Man's temples. All
j I around
Lies lifeless-desert, and on it a mound —
Spoil for a later excavator's spade!
S6 kingdoms ripe and die; and Time goes on:
There is in some lone place a leaping birth.
On ruins' of a legendary past
A\young race rears stability at last!
domes swift catastrophe, and all is gone
Back to the comfort of old mother Earth!
, v iv.
Whuf is to Man this civilisation worth?
It is'a fever that affects Mankind,
An intermittent madness in his mind,
A festering scab upon the healthy Earth.
She looks upon it with a bitter mirth.
It is a torch that flares once in the wind,
Lighting a little space around, behind;
Then gutters out —yet in its death is birth.
i J
The Mother puts away the flimsy toys,
These broken empires that amused her boys;
Playtime is over, and they weary are.
She mothers them, and knows -when tired of
play
They come back to their cots at close of day;
Despite their dreams they will not wander
. far.
Y.
In angry-waves the hardy peoples dash
Upon the peaceful tribes; beneath the urge
Of crowded camps and hunger on they surge
■To deinquer, murder, rape, enslave and smash.
As predatory tribes with slothful clash
And fuse, a mighty kingdom will emerge;
The conquerors, a necessary scourge,
Uplift a civilisation ivith the lash!
The sated rulers to new luxuries turn,
Unguessed-at in their bitter hunting days;
flic armies of their slaves by duress stern
. or gettheir laziness, new labors learn;
1 On-bleeding backs they a new empire raise —•
And even they their barbarism spurn!
VI.
One moment Mankind rides the crested wave,
A moment glorious, beyond recall;
And then the wave, with slow and massive
fall,
Obliterates the beauty that it gave.
When discrowned king and manumitted slave
Are free and equal to be slaves of all,
Demdfiracies in their wide freedom brawl,
And go'down Shouting in a common grave.
ISo one by one the petals of the rose
Shrivel wnd fade, and all its splendor goes
Back to the Earth; and in her arms embraced
Through wintry centuries the dead seeds
sleep,
Till Spring comes troubling them, and they
nip-leap,
Once more their petals on the world to waste.
vii.
Now that this splendor that we took to build
So many toilsome centuries is torn
With' fratricidal hands, and lies forlorn
And desecrated, with mad faction filled.
Does not the widowed Earth, her fields un-
tilled.
Her budding lives from joy untimely shorn,
For these, her murdered children, gravely
mourn?
Is she unconscious of her millions killed?
Not o'er these hosts of broken dead she
grieves,
Nor mother-like for their stopped laughter
craves;
That rich manure she gratefully receives.
The autumn storm, that ruthlessly bereaves
The forests, lightly flings to unknown graves
A million million more of murdered leaves!
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
Spookism
Charles A. Merrier, author of Spiritualism
and Sir Oliver Lodge (Mental Culture Enter-
prise, London, per Sydney Book Club), is a
scientific man with a keenly logical mind and
a sense of humor. “When I read the drivel
that is put into the mouths of dead men who
when alive were of normal intelligence I
began to suspect that the foundation upon
which this structure was built was insecure.”
He claims that he is supported by high medi-
cal authority in his conviction that the pur-
suit of spiritualism leads to a morbid frame
of mind, and tends to render those who are at
all predisposed to insanity an easy prey to
the disease. You can find people who get
messages from spirits and who receive occult
manifestations every hour of the day—in a
mental hospital.. Merrier notes the waste of
time, the emotional impressibility produced,
the carelessness of observation, the atmo-
sphere of credulity, the encouragement of
imposture, as a few of the reasons why this
subject should be investigated and its
methods exposed. He characterises Oliver
Lodge’s strenuous propagandism as unfair
and dangerous.
Always the occult has exercised a fascina-
tion for the human mind, especially at
periods of war when there is universal be-
reavement. But we are called upon to believe
Lodge and his spirits, not on the ground of
faith but .on the ground of reason. All
Mercier has to do, therefore, is to examine
the evidence. Mercier puts the blame for
this recent outbreak of occultism upon
Lodge’s reputation as a scientific man.
Lodge’s honesty is above suspicion; but is he
in a position to know, and does he in fact
know, the things to which he testifies? There
is a wide difference between a man who
works at scientific subjects and a scientific
man. Mercier proves that Lodge does not
possess the scientific habit of mind ; and gives
good reasons why we should not accept the
specialist in electricity as a specialist in
spooks. Mercier suggests that an expert'
conjurer would be a better witness of the
authenticity of many alleged mediumistic
manifestations. And “as long as the per-
formances of mediums are not more inex-
plicable than the performances of conjurers,
the mediums have no title whatever to
demand our belief that their performances
are supernatural.”
Lodge gives evidence in favor of liis hypo-
thesis of telepathy, but no proof; for an
hypothesis is not proved until the evidence
has been shown to be inconsistent with any
and every other hypothesis. In fact, Mercier
shows that the evidence in favor of witch-
craft was of a much more cogent character
than the evidence in favor of telepathy.
Mercier makes out a much better case for
witchcraft than Lodge does for his spooks.
Mediums to-day are not executed, but are
paid, for professing their craft; yet witches,
knowing that they would bring upon them-
selves an agonising death, freely confessed.
The belief in witchcraft was even more
widely held in the past than the present
belief in spiritualism; and Mercier neatly
shows that every argument in favor of the
existence of spirits applies even more forcibly
to the existence of witches.
Merrier subjects Lodge’s written evidence
to a mercilessly destructive cross-examina-
tion that will be conclusive to the unbiased
reader. Yet it seems that there is something-
in telepathy, though no proof has yet been
adduced that telepathy has anything what-
ever to do with the existence of spooks. As
Maeterlinck argues, the potentialities of the
subconscious brain are so enormous and as
yet so inadequately explored, that there is no
need to seek for spirits when all the occult
manifestations may be explained by the
powers of our subconscious brain. Con-
sciously or unconsciously the believers in
communications from the dead credit to
“spirits” the manifestations of their human
subliminal intelligence.
J*
Incidentally, this statement from Current
Opinion is given for what it is worth: Dr.
Crawford, lecturer on mechanical engineer-
ing at the municipal technical institute of
Belfast, lias conducted some remarkable ex-
periments. I)r. Crawford does not worry
about spiritualism : all lie is concerned with
is certain “psychical” phenomena. He has
subjected a case of “levitation” —the raising
of a ponderable body in the presence of a
medium in a manner which cannot be
accounted for by the known laws of science—
to a prolonged and methodical quantitative
examination. Prof.Wliately-Smith in London
World summarises Dr. Crawford’s results:
(1) A wooden table is raised to a height of one or
more feet from the ground, without being in contact
with any person or any other material object. This
result can be produced almost always on demand.
(2) When the table is thus “levitated” the weight
of the medium is increased by an amount practically
equal to the weight of the table.
(3) Experiment has forced Dr. Crawford to infer
the existence of a rigid structure connecting the
medium to the table —a structure invisible and im-
palpable; A thin rod can be passed freely between the
medium and the table during “levitation”—or all
round the table —without effect.
(4) The forces concerned are under intelligent con-
trol of some kind.
Prof. Whately-Smith testifies that lie has
himself witnessed the phenomena, and that
they are undoubtedly genuine.
Well, there it is. All this page asks is for
the experiment to be repeated in the presence
of Dr. Merrier. Or is there any medium in
Australia who can repeat that experiment
under proper supervision?
Norman Lindsay
Even in these days the pen, provided that
it is the pen of Norman Lindsay, is mightier
than the sword. For in this time of war,
with economic pressure ever more heavily
descending upon Australia, a publisher can
be found to publish, and editors to edit, The
Ten Drawings of Norman Lindsay (Angus
and Robertson,’ Sydney, 12s. 6d.). This
special number of Art in Australia, ably
edited by Sydney Ure Smith and Bertram
Stevens, is a complete and final tribute to
the genius of Norman Lindsay. It is also
his justification, if, in the ease of genius,
justification is needed. Here are beauty and
brilliance, imagination in its most daring-
flights, paganism rioting in its heady youth,
and a marvellous technical mastery of a
difficult art. Here is the whole artistic and
social development of the artist, who is
characteristically as Australian as he is his
own self, from his boyhood efforts through
his various phases of experience and experi-
ment, up to a point beyond which in sheer
achievement it seems impossible for him to
go. But with such a record behind him
there must be other vistas stretching for-
ward. Julian Ashton’s essay in this volume
is an excellent appreciation of the man; his
brother Lionel adds his criticism and eulogy
of the artist. The prodigality of Lindsay’s
genius is aptly expressed by Ashton: “For
nearly 20 years Norman Lindsay has poured
into the world a flood of drawings, political,
social and imaginative; illustrations to stan-
dard authors, paintings in oil and water-
color, lithographs, posters and humorous pen-
and-ink sketches. In the leisure moments of
this strenuous life he has modelled life-sized
nymphs pursued by satyrs, built beautiful
miniature models of ships, from the Greek
trireme to the old English line-of-battle ship,
with every spar, rope, and block in place.
Incidentally, he has written on many artis-
tic questions, published two or three books:
illustrated by himself, and is still at work ’
upon the manuscript of others.” Of his work
Ashton notes that it has already begun to
show more restraint, a larger and more tol-
erant vision, “together with a dawning con-
ception of the great role woman plays in
keeping fresh the ideals of civilisation.”
Also, “his red tie and paganism are gone.”
The reproductions are perfect; and the
feast of art herein provided is bounteous and
full of variety. Here are the pictures that
aroused the old antagonism of the public:
“The Crucified Venus,” “Pollice Verso,”
“Dionysus” and the rest; the mar-
vellous illustrations to Rabelais; bacchanals
and processions and bathers, the Tom O’Bed-
lam grotesques, the Villon series, with its
tremendous feat, the great composition, “The
Apotheosis of Villon,” and, finally, a mas-
terly illustration to a Leon Gellert poem.
On this tender and beautiful note, this grace-
ful and unchallenging beauty, Norman Lind-
say exquisitely ends. If this be his depar-
ture-point for new worMs to conquer, the
Norman Lindsay of the future will not
annoy the Wowsers quite as much as he
managed to do in the past. But with this
picture, as with many of the others, “it is
difficult to understand how pen-work can be
pushed further: —but, if it can, Norman Lind-
say is the man to do it.”
A limited de luxe edition, with extra
plates and each plate separately mounted,
will be issued at £3 3s.
Anzalian
IV. A. Osborne, Professor of Psychology at
Melbourne University, drops a ton ot bombs
upon Catholic Ireland in the curiously titled
What TFe Owe to Ireland (Lothian, Melb.).
He doubts whether outside Ireland there is
adequate recognition of the wide racial dis-
parity, the gulf that separates the Ulsterman
Protestant and the Catholic Irish. lie finds
that in business- enterprise, statesmanship,
administration, military and naval command,
achievement in science, letters, law, art and
music, by a comparison of the great names-
in each class, that, “the Irish Catholics rank
very low among the white races for their
contribution to civilisation,” And he claims
that the wrongs suffered by Ulster from Eng-
land are even more real than those suffered
by the rest of Ireland. A further compari-
son of the achievements of the Scotch-Irish
and the Catholic-Irish immigrants in the
United States upholds the author’s thesis.
He contends that “the accumulated evidence
forces one to the conclusion that the Irish
are an inferior race” ; and he suggests that
they are incapable of self-government. The
cause for this alleged inferiority W. A.
Osborne guardedly suggests in his concluding
paragraph. It is extremely doubtful whether
such a bomb should be hurled at this moment
upon any class or section of the Empire’s
inhabitants. The publication of this book
might conceivably result in a racial and reli-
gious bitterness 'that Australia does not, at
this or any juncture, desire to foment.

Some Verse and Worse, by Ernest It.
Peate (Merredin ; Is.) ... . Well, we wouldn’t,
be rude enough to contradict the author.
Win. Martin, of Papatowai, Otago, M.L.,
apostrophises the uncomplaining ocean in A
Seaside Reverie. He gratuitously informs*
the ocean that:- ‘ - ’
Thy waters teemed with silent fish,
Incapable of thought, or wish....
Fish have no knowledge, thought, or share'
Of lands, so beautiful and near,
Or e’en suppose that life can be ...
Beyond the confines of the 5ea..../
Pathetic, isn’t it?
d* _
Chrystal Stirling has done an interesting
thing well. Taking “the ordinary eyeryday
letters” of a man who went to the war,' and
the letters of the wife, “watching and wait-
ing, and acting the brave soldier at home,”
the author of Soldiers Two (“Bookstall”
Series, Is.) has made of them a simple but
readable story. The letters of the somewhat
sentimental Billjim are full of quiet, graphic
touches; he sees the ordinary things 1 and
describes them in the ordinary way; but
the letters of his wife do not sound so real:
they are too literary.... and altogether too
much plot seems to happen in her home town.
As fiction, however, the little book is a
success, though similar letters must have;
passed between thousands of Billjims and
their wives. And the author, with a bee#
seise for a climax, does not shrink from the.
realistic touch of the final communication.
But why does the publisher allow on the.
novel’s cover a representation of Australian'
soldiers charging not in the Australian
slouch hats, which are the only wear over-
seas, but in the British field cap? There
are no Australian badges visible on the
uniforms, either.

Owing to the abnormal price of paper in
England, the London publishers are looking
out for shorter novels. Before the war
novels ran to distressing lengths: the idea
probably was that the purchaser- insisted on
getting his money’s worth of yarn ; but now
the cost of printing and of paper compels
the publisher to set forth his fiction _in
shorter gasps. Australian novelists trying
the overseas market should not exceed GOiOOO’
words.

copy on Red Page (14/3/’18) were epigrams, pro-
verbs, sentiments, phrases or aphorisms ; what’s -wrong;
with printing indigenous ones under the title of
Australianisms in a little booklet that would sell at:
6d. ? E. W. Cole arid Lothian have both issued sim-
ilar things of imported stuff which find a ready sale.
I understand Australian Nursery Rimes has been a

“Arthur V.”: There’s as much human nature in
youngsters as in grown-ups, and perhaps more. The
blase young “ ’ope and worry of our lives” (2}>
remains quite indifferent to “Tinkle and
“Old Bob’s Canter” in Australian Nursery Rimes.
He yearns for the exciting and the incongruous. Con-
sequently the shocking end of the “Five Little Rab-
bits,” and Mrs. Possum’s headache, caused by-
Johnny Jackass’s ill-timed mirth, attract him most;
while the distressing fulness after meals of Billy
Bandicoot, and the wallaby who always jumped with-
out ever bumping his head, are always sure to gain
his attention. His daddy thinks the latter should
have been included in the book, and is sure that
another omission from the book, “Ten Little
Rabbits,” will- be first favorite with the “’Ope” in a
year or two. -
“Umfaan” belatedly submits “the epitaph of one
of the best Anzacs and finest gentleman it has ever
been my good luck to meet. I refer to Frane Carse,
one-time barrister of Melbourne. When one of
Franc’s batterv passed out, he would remark “He
was a terrible good bloke.” Greater enitaph no
Australian ever had than that. Franc himself passed
out at Bullecourt, loved by his xrieh, his brother
officers and his superiors, and no finer epitaph could
be written of that good Australian than His own
words, “He was' a terrible good blbke.”
“Umnti”: “Furnley Maurice” says Dennis’s slang.is
a bit old-fashioned now. Obviously, therefore, it suits
The Sentimental Bloke. He loved one woman and
was true to her ; quite a mid-Victorian kind of thing
to do in a book, and out-of-date in this decadent
age. It is not surprising then that at least a third'
of the words Dennis puts into his glossary are to bp>
found in The Slany Dictionary (Chatto and Windus,.
New Edition, 1891, but originally written in 1858)..
A good many more of them will also be familiar ta
those who know the various slang words common in
London in the eighties and nineties of last century.

VORIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Tower of Vision.
The caravansary of futile days
Has windows opening on a dull grey sea,
Wrapped morn and evening in a sunless
haze,
Where no ship comes and never breeze is
free.
The twisted trees, set stiff by line and rule,
Lifce bearded ancients stand beside the gate,
Where the long roads agafnst the heart-
strings pull,
Grey, endless, straight as doom, and deso-
late.
In at dull doors the solemn people go,
Out from sad rooms the dreary people come
To watch the everlasting ebb and flow
Of landward dust and waters that are dumb.
Up the broad, faded stairs the people pass,
Down the brown corridors on listless feet;
And, like the arabesques on beaten brass,
The worms a pattern in the wainscot eat.
Three heavy flights to reach the upper floor,
And find the weary chambers of no rest —
But, curtained deep, there is a little door
In the wide wall that shuts away the west.
And I have found the key that fits the lock,
Cross-hilted like a dagger, and so old
It seems the wards of later days to mock
With some remembrance of an Age of Gold.
The door is all of yellow ivory;
And as it turns upon a noiseless hinge,
I find a twisted stair of wizardry
All dragon-wrought about its climbing fringe.
Mounting, the rust is crisp beneath my feet;
A bat wheels startled wings above my head;
And through the darkness creeps a murmur
sweet
Of God’s clean air that in the house lay
dead.
Pull back the brazen shutter —Ip, the sky!
The sea and sky and a wide, purple land !
A glory and a wonder and the cry
Of happy birds that soar and understand.
Oh, little turret, I will linger here
With all that was that I may truly see
Above the withered house of day and year
Truth echo back the thing that yet shall be.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WEIGHT.
A Garden.
A goldfinch from the new-leaved pear-tree
trills
A hymn of ecstasy; a brown bee goes
Droning his way along the honeyed rows,
Gold spangled with the pollen that he spills.
When all my being with this sweetness fills
I lift eyes sated with a flaming rose
To where Ben Lomond crowns with gleam-
ing snows
The purple panorama of the hills.
Then such a subtle gladness fills my soul —
Forgotten the grey spectre of regret,
The broken dreams, the grinning ghosts of
pain,
Those quests forlorn that failed to reach a
goal—
That for a fleeting moment I forget
That I shall never, never walk again.
TASMANIA.
H. W. STEWART.
Twenty-eight.
Twenty-eight! Twenty-eight! All the clocks
are out of date,
But I hear the chime of little things that I
have left top late.
Time’s a tyrant, Life’s a fool, things have
somehow gone amiss;
There’s another little glass to drink, another
girl to kiss;
I'm not ready for the audit when the debit
looks like this.
Twenty-eight! Twenty-eight!
Won’t you have a heart and wait
For a fellow who’s been drowsing till he’s
nearing twenty-eight?
Twenty-eight! Twenty-eight! There’s a
clamor that I hate
From the little things neglected, and I know
it’s getting late.
Little voices raised in chorus make a devil
of a row —
Have you heard about my book of verses
(underneath the brow),
And the stories and the plays I’ve not written
yet? I vow,
Twenty-eight! Twenty-eight!
You are most unfortunate ;
Pleasd' remember that I didn’t see you com-
ing, Twenty-eight!
Twenty-eight! Twenty-eight! I’m a pencil,
Life’s a slate;
I’ve been scratching silly figures at an easy-
going rate.
They are smudged and crooked numbers,
some are furtive, small, unseen ;
There are others written bolder than I’d
like them to have been.
But it really doesn’t matter, for you won’t
know what they mean.
Twenty-eight! Twenty-eight!
Hurry up and clean the slate,
And we’ll start another series—will you help
me, Twenty-eight?
Twenty-eight! Twenty-eight! Though you
think me reprobate,
Won’t you come and make a year of it, and
let us celebrate?
Time’s a tyrant Life’s a fool, all the world
may go amiss,
But there’s still another glass or two, and
maidens like a kiss.
When you come to make the audit will you
please allow for this,
Twenty-eight! Twenty-eight!
Since your middle name is Fate,-
You may want to take me with you when
you’re going, Twenty-eight.
N.S.W.
HUGO KNAPP.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
rejused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal. Social or other Articles, Verse, Short 'Titles or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. AIL communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. tvhen
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. , When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs In Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 30, 1918.
WHY AUSTRALIANS WILL HAVE TO
ROLL UP THEIR SLEEVES.
Millions are talked about these times like
tuppences. But behind the vague talk there
is definite meaning, and in the House of
Reps, the other day Treasurer Watt pro-
duced a mass of figures to explain what the
Australian citizen has to grapple with.
According to this arithmetic the position at
the end of April was a Commonwealth debt
made up thus :—•
War loans .. £197,507,169
Other loans .. 28,511,068
£226,018,237
This the Treasurer explained was, “from
one point of view,” the total indebtedness of
the Commonwealth. Whose point of view is
of no importance. That sum does not repre-
sent the debt to-day, and is a long way short
of the debt in sight. There are commit-
ments which have compelled the Ministry to
ask for authority to borrow an additional
£98,000,000; there is an unpaid account due
to the British War Office of £33,550,000;
also there is £10,789,738 owing to the States
for transferred property. These sums added
to the other lot make a total of £368,357,975.
Part of this is accounted for by advances to
the States; but the Commonwealth net debt
already contracted and in the making is not
less than £330,000,000, with an annual
charge in . the immediate future of
£16,500,000. And the war is still raging.
Demobilisation of the army will be a long
and costly business. Hence there will, unless
a miracle happens, have to be more borrow-
ing even after the current authorities are
exhausted. A Commonwealth debt of
£400,000,000 is practically certain—of
£500,000,000 not improbable. Meanwhile an
interest bill of £16,500,000 a year is on the
horizon.
* * * *
That, of course, is only part of the story,
The State debts in June last were
£375.000,000. Now they are over
£380,000,000, and the interest bill is
£15,000,000. So between them all the Com-
monwealth and States have the prospect of
paying £31,500,000 a year on the basis of
obligations already and about to be con-
tracted ; and it will be substantially more
when the basis is altered, as it inevitably
must be through conversions. It is a big
sum this, yet not beyond the capacity of Aus-
tralia to provide. -But it is morally certain
that the providing will have to be done upon
lines verv different from those that have
been hitherto followed. The taxation im-
posed to meet the older debt could be easily 7
borne so long as additional millions were
raised and tossed about. The practical
meaning of that performance was that the
country was borrowing to pay the cost of
previous borrowing. But a doubling of taxa-
tion under conditions which necessitate pay-
ments from legitimate earnings means some-
thing quite different; and that is why, unless
production in Australia is greatly increased
and the lunacy of the imported commodity
repressed (one thing means the same as the
hther), and a, substitute found for the pre-
vailing extravagance in government and ad-
ministration, the debt is eoing to be worse
than a permanent plague of mice and locusts.
In the year before the war Australia
imported goods valued at £70.749,653. r lne
exports were worth £78,571,709. With the
new debt to carry, the Commonwealth can
no more hope to do that sort of thing and
avoid disaster than it can hope to hear of
Hughes having restored the situation by
413 impassioned addresses about an egg-
laying competition at Warwick.
* * * #
It was casually mentioned by Watt that
during the next few years the States would
have to “meet or convert” the promissory-
note issued in the long ago for very substan-
tial sums. The idea that Cgiien will be met
on the doorstep by the local Treasurer, who
will pour several millions of gold into the
money-lender’s wallet in exchange for the
old lOU, is not worth bothering about. The
States will have to turn the old lOU into a
new one, and do it on the terms of the other
party instead of on their own. What these
terms will be is something more than a
matter' of academic interest; they will have a
verv real effect upon taxation and upon what
the Treasurer calls the “true national debt.”
Every fractional increase in the percentage
of interest pa/üble upon non-revenue-earping
debt must obviously discount the value to
the taxpayer of that portion of the aggre-
gate debt which allegedly provides its own
charges. During the current year N. S.
Wales has a loan of £12,649,318 falling due
in London, and lately made arrangements to
pass on the bill. The interest payable was
at the rate of 3| per cent. The renewal is
at 5f per cent., and charges bring the actual
rate to 6 per cent. Thus, instead of the
State having to find £448,000 every year, it
will have to find £758,900. The net effect
is to increase the burden of the £12,649,318,
spent when the sky was clear, to a borrowing
of £21,683,000 at the original rate. This
only requires to be done a few times to make
a vast difference in the burden of debt. Now,
that something or other equally or more dis-
mal will have to be done in a large way
before the nation is much older is practi-
cally certain. In succeeding financial years
State debts fall due as follows: —

Of this total £72,000,000 is redeemable in
London, the balance in Australia. The in-
terest payable thereon ranges from Si to
4 per cent. The annual interest bill is about
£4,180,000. If the loans are renewed on no
better terms than 6 per cent., the bill will
run up to £6,687,000. The Treasurer tells us
that most of the State’s indebtedness will
pay its own interest. But it is no good
juggling with figures, and no use blinking
the fact that works which paid 3i p.c. will
in future be required to pay probably 6 p.c.
if they are to be self-supporting.
* * * *
The Bulletin has no irremovable
opinion about the state of mind the British
lender will be in after the war, but it has a
dreadful suspicion that the borrower who
happens along with a p.n. for renewal at the
old-time rates is going to be received with
cold disfavor for many- years to come.
Wherefore this paper looks with alarm, not
so much upon the possibilities incidental to
these renewal operations, as upon the con-
tinued reckless piling up of the bill by
current additions-to the public debt. Watt
asks that the problem of the numerous
national blisters should be approached in “a
spirit of sobriety.” Any change from the
spirit of hilarity in which millions have been
and are still being added to the national
burden would be as welcome as rain in time
of drought. But whether people who with
the stupendous difficulties presented by these
debts ahead of them persist in still running
14 Houses of Parliament and maintaining
seven Cocked Hats and borrowing to keep
alive the old institution of Boom and Bloat,
are anywhere near sober is another matter.
It is no use anybody, Treasurer or other-
wise, talking in a large, loose manner about
not being concerned about the financial situ-
ation. They ought to be concerned about it.
Politicians, at any rarte, are where they are
for that purpose; and unless they soon begin
to patch up some of the leaks and get down
to the bedrock question of production in Aus-
tralia, the sooner they are tossed out to be
vaguely eloquent somewhere else the better.
THE RED FLAG.
“The Melbourne Trades Hall has decided that in
future the red flag shall be flown from the hall, in
addition to the Australian flag, in celebration of all
Labor events.”
So “Labor events” will have cause to be
proud of themselves in future with the
garish, foreign emblem of anarchy, excite-
ment and mixed disturbance floating in their
honor. The red flag is' the symbol of the
sort of faith held by Communards and Bol-
sheviks and other breakers-and-smashers. It
doesn’t stand for any constructive policy—
at least those who carried it never got so
far as successful construction except the
building of babel and chaos. It has been
the sign that the old things, good or bad,
were to be dragged down, even if there were
no new things to be put in their place. In
regions where the arm of the oppressor is
long and the hand of the oppressor is heavy
excuse may be found ; but it is difficult to
find it in a land like Australia, where public
affairs are run by the whole adult popula-
tion on an equal basis. In the first case the
rag of the bull-frightening color represents
the protest of the excluded majority, against
the control of the privileged minority. In
the second case, which is our case, it repre-
sents the rising, or the proposed rising, of
a soured or disappointed or wildly ambitious
minority, trying to snatch the power from
the popular majority. There is no engine
of tyranny hereabouts to be wiped out by
upheaval save the majority, and there is
nobody to upheave against the engine save
the minority. The only thing that can be
abolished, if society is to be re-organised
and there is to be a new heaven and a new
earth, is the rule of all the people expressed
in the best way that all the people know
how. The alternative implement is the rule
of some of the people, who may, by force
and froth and loud denunciation and abun-
dant promises and pretensions, make up for
lack of numbers. The red flag here is the
banner of the proposed oligarchy. A section
of Labor leaders or Labor spokesmen lias
been drifting of late towards theoretical in-
surrection. The official hoisting of the sign
of chronic rebellion and combustion sug-
gests hope of turning the theory into prac-
tice.
* # # *
Some of the literature circulated in the
name of Labor these days is strangely and
wonderfully like the literature which the
Hun Government’s stipendiary traitors have
been disseminating in France, Italy, the
United States and elsewhere. It is emi-
nently “pacifist.” It sets forth that a deci-
sive victory over the Hun is impossible, and
thus pours the water of despair on that
enthusiasm which leads to victory. It is
against “conscription,” and gives a mild
approval or no approval at all to voluntary
effort. It teaches “defence” only in the
sense that any heroic effort should bq post-
poned , till the Hun is right here, which
means postponement till our Allies are licked
and we stand, or rather fall, alone. It clam-
ors for “negotiation,” thereby persistently
assuming in defiance of the past and the
present that the Hun is the good sort of
fellow whose word can be relied upon and
whose negotiations are as honest as the
average. If Germany doesn’t pay for the
encouragement of that view it can only be
because some people are willing to work
gratis provided the cause is bad enough.
There is a persistent demand that the
blessed Hun shall not be weakened by the
exaction of an indemnity; that his tender
feelings shall not be humiliated; that he
shall be allowed to get. out of his own dis-
honorable “war of frightfulness” with honor
if he is willing to go out; that he shall get
his chance in haste before the great inflow
of United States reinforcements blights his
hopes; and that he shall be left in good
order and condition to start afresh when
there are fewer reinforcements about.
* * * *
One strenuous pacifist, Wallace, M.H.R.,
struck a very decorative note the other day
by stating that “while he held no brief for
the German,” yet “all Governments seem
much the same in their treatment of the
under-dog.” If that meant anything it must
have meant that Britain and the United
States and France treat their possessions,
such as South Africa, India, the Philippines,
Papua (and possibly Australia) just as the
Huns treat Belgium and Serbia and Rou-
mania and Poland, and that v T e have the
same desperate need to defend our hearths
to the last gasp with gun and steel against
the Senate and the House of Reps, as the
most down-trodden of them all. In other
words the Ilun is ns good and merciful and
unaggressive as the best of the Allies, and
he wrnuld be as good a master here as the
next person. The holding of a brief for the
world’s butcher could hardly go farther. The
pacifist crowd with the blood-colored ori-
flamme always seems to have that sort of
document sticking out of its pocket. It is
among other things a persistent dealer in
foreign grievances as well as foreign revolu-
tionary literature and ideals. It can wal-
lop itself and its audiences to fury about the
old woes of Russian peasants under a dead
and gone regime, until it almost creates a
conviction that hordes of Grand Dukes are
pouring into our cities, and that unless the
ballot-box is upset and the popularly-elected
Legislature and the Public Service and the
Treasury are chased into the bush we will
inevitably perish beneath the heel of the
despot—name of despot unknown. It is in
regular communication with feoreign red-
flaggers who are willing to help us keep an
eye on the despot. And when it heard that
two plausible scoundrels, each trading under
a false name and both strongly suspected of
being Hun agents, had captained a great up-
roar and filled Russia with pillage, arson,
murder, hunger, misery, disease and chaos,,
and abandoned its best provinces to Hun’
militarists, it wanted at once to send an
agent to Russia to report how things are
done.
What kind of system the minority leaders
would supply if they obtained control of this
ballotbox-ridden country is a matter of con-
jecture. They are fairly good at stopping
the wheels of industry. They can hang a
large grievance on a small peg. They live
in constant cheerful disregard of the indus-
trial laws which were passed by the down-
trodden people for their own down-treading.
They give heed to industrial awards when
so inclined and not otherwise. They pro-
pound off-hand theories about “taking” the
control of one thing and another —the fixing
of prices, the reduction of working time to
five days per week, or the management of
big and intricate financial problems—“out
of the hands” of the “politicians” who are
the miserable, ignorant, despised Legislature,
which is, in its turn, the embodiment of the
miserable ignorant despised nation, and
putting matters generally in direct charge
of “the workers.” The minority leaders
have apparently no use for the nation. They
prance on the verge of a paper insurrection,
which may become some sort of flesh and
blood insurrection if the red flag is flapped
hard enough. And under the new order of
things, whatever it may be, the “diabolical”
conspiracy which, according to an official
Labor declaration, Holman and his party-
had devised for reducing the public to star-
vation, will be exposed and frustrated ; and
the scheme, also officially mentioned but not
explained, for raising abundant loans with-
out interest, will get to work; and peaceful
pro-Hun literature will be abundant; and
every Grand Duke or Irish landlord who is
found oppressing the peasantry in Collins-
street or along Circular Quay will be ex-
pelled or worse.
* * * *
Yet amid all these prospects there is time
to feel sorry for the Australian standard.
It has something of a record. It is the
banner of a whole continent, and that con-
tinent stands for one race (barring Bol-
sheviks and other imported sundries), one
speech and one Government —and no stan-
dard ever stood for such things before. It
was devised to represent a white race which
was pledged to remain white even if the
“universal brotherhood of man” was busted
up in the process, and a Parliament which
never admitted political privilege in any
shape or form. If it isn’t good enough for
Melbourne Trades Hall without the cosmo-
politan rag of upheaval to keep it company
on high days and holidays, then Melbourne
Trades Hall might wisely take it dowm, and
fold it reverently away to wait for better
times and better society.
TIME AS A MUNITION OF WAR.
Of all things that you can throw at an
enemy there is nothing that will do him
more damage than a piece of well-directed
time. It was the knowledge of this that
gave the Germans their tremendous advan-
tage at the beginning of the war; while the
Allies’ failure to realise it has accounted for
most of their reverses. The Germans began
the war somewhere about 1872; if Britain
had started it even in July, 1914, tilings
might have been different. Had the glorious
little contemptible army that suffered at
Mons been at Liege, as it might have been,
four days after the actual “declaration,” the
whole shape of the commotion would have
been altered. And had the Australian troops,
who were then engaged in a futile attempt
to wear out their enthusiasm in Egyptian
sand, been at the Dardanelles at the moment
when the warships first tried to force the
passage, there would have been no further
trouble worth mentioning with the Turk,
and the Bulgar would never have given us
an hour’s worry. The idea that time —a
very long time —is on our side at last may
prove correct; but hitherto we have cer-
tainly not hit the German hard with it. All
those years between the first assurance that
the Teuton menace was a real one and the
opening of the great killing itself might
have been as a flail in our hands with which
to thresh flat the disturbers of the peace;
but we would not or could not use the
munition that lay readiest to our hands.
Now, when the war has raged and destroyed
for nearly four years and no man can see
the end, there are still indications that time
is being loosely handled while the enemy is
hitting us with it fiercely and often. The
blocking of the ports which were the chief
submarine bases was a fine feat; but why
wasn’t it attempted before most of the ship-
ping on which the Allies depended for food
had gone to the bottom of the sea? The in-
vention of the tanks marked a big depar-
ture ; but the notion was one that might
well have been carried out in days of peace
and held as a surprise. Five hundred tanks
at Mons would have taken the edge off the
Prussian sword, redeemed Belgium and
hanged the Kaiser. Always it was time that
mattered. And always it was time that
worked for Wilhelm because our side did
not employ it.
# * * *
In this regard the position is worse
probably in Australia than in any other
Allied country. The others have allowed
time to slip from their fingers; we have
deliberately mocked it, kicked it, danced on
its head. A dozen years ago it was plain
to everybody who paused to do a little think-
ing that this big, thinly-peopled continent
must sooner or later be tried in the flames of
war. The system of compulsory service
was introduced in a half-hearted fashion
after public feeling had been so roused on
the matter that the people would willingly
have gone the whole hog and created a citi-
zen army of real strength and efficiency.
Still, the pressing problem which faced our
statesmen at the time was not the raising
of a defence army but the discovery of some
method by which the fighting force of the
nation could be multiplied by ten. There
was no German war in immediate prospect;
the skies were calm; the people willing. It
was then that time lay ready to our hand —
and we did nothing. Our means of trans-
porting troops to the parts of the Common-
wealth most open to attack are no better
now than they were a dozen years ago; the
dangers are greater than they were; our
man-power has been reduced by casualties
in a distant field and our enthusiasm by
muddle at home. Had decent foresight been
shown in those days of profound peace—
foresight backed by honest and persistent
effort —the weight of our blow in France
might have been magnified without any-
thing like the losses we have sustained.
Take the matter of the aeroplane alone. The
history of air invention traces its. beginnings
to Sydney. In Sydney the principles of, the
wings that soar and of the engine that drives
them were first experimented upon and de-
monstrated ; yet to-day, when all Europe is
a land “shadowing with wings,” half the
people living in this continent have never
seen an aeroplane at all, although the aero-
plane looked from the first the very best means
bv which the fighting power of a small and
scattered army could be increased. Had
we gone straight ahead wuth the develop-
ment of mechanical flight in the days of
peace and trained flying units in all parts
of the continent, the outbreak of the war
would have found us in a position to render
enormous service to the Allied cause. If.
instead of the first 100,000 of our troops
transported half-way round the world at
such a cost of shipping space, we had been
able to equip and send to Europe lO.Out
eager, expert airmen who knew the game
better than the other fellow, the Australian
would have come near being the deciding


factor in the war. Instead we let time slip;
and although, coming in at the heel, Aus-
tralian airmen are now giving a splendid
account of themselves, there is no possibility
of making up for the lost years. Further,
we still have done nothing or next to nothing
to provide air-equipment for use in our own
country, just as we have done nothing or
next to nothing to provide ourselves with
an arsenal, without which no country can
be said to have any real defences at all.
* * * *
However, time, though so wasted in the
past, is the sort of munition of which there
may still be a sufficient supply. The tor-
menting doubt and the weakness always lie
in the faqt that no one can ever exactly
gauge that supply ; the hope and the strength
lie in the other fact that it is cheap and
ready, and can be grasped at once by any
honest and patriotic hand. The workshops
of Germany were waging war bitterly a
generation ago in full anticipation of a vic-
tory which they only narrowly failed to win ;
what forces, political, social or industrial, are
even now waging war upon us—a war which
may possibly not “break out” in the accepted
sense until the echoes of the present commo-
tion have long died down? Against such
disquieting likelihoods our great munition
still is time. The thing is with us, sharp-
edged, strong, effective, if we are ready to
take it and employ it for our national
safety. To merely talk about a thing of
urgent importance is a crime against the
future which will assuredly be punished in
some way, it may be through blood.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Prejudicial to Recruiting and Offensive
to Our Allies.
May 24 was seized upon by various news-
papers and individuals as an opportunity
for working off a lot of loose eloquence about
the Empire. In the evening the rising
generation was presented with crackers and
sky-rockets and incited to disturb the sub-
urban peace, and the full-grown Imperialist
proceeded to bang the drum of his cult and
give three cheers on general principles ; for
it was “Empire Day” and the time was ripe
for blither and boast and brag. Sydney
Telegram rose to a great height of what
The Bulletin suspects its old friend re-
garded as “true patriotism” and poured bosh
upon an unoffending populace with generous
hand: —-
At a time when, as never before, the elements of
our heritage are being compacted and arrayed in
the perspective first visioned by Chatham, we pause
to take stock of our Imperial destiny—-of what we
have and are. The day we celebrate is a day that
finds us more truly an Empire than before. No
longer is May 24 a date on the calendar surrendered
to cherished memories of a great Queen in whose life
we sought a symbol of the always existing purpose
of the race. It is a moment when, after wanderings
in a wilderness of blind alleys, we discover that we
have passed another milestone on a path directed
to a goal visible to all. This fourth year of war is
the fourth year of intensive Imperial achievement.
Heaven only knows what this gorgeous out-
burst means, unless the Telegraph was
giving someone a polite tip to go slow about
Victoria the Very Good. But whether it
meant anything or not matters l.ttle. The
main point about all the rhetofic was the
endeavor to make the Australian people
believe that justification for participating
in the war is to be found in rhodomontade
concerning the Empire, and that their high-
est possible achievement would be an exten-
sion of Imperialism in this and other coun-
tries. “We are all doing nothing,” yells
the Telegraph, “that is not intimately
associated with a struggle to maintain the
new-found mission of Imperialism that came
with a certain date in August, 1914.” This
is a perversion of the truth. Australia is
doing nothing it would not have done in this
war if Imperialism was as dead as mutton
and Britain was a republic and the flag of
an Australian republic was in the breeze.
Australia is fighting because war was made
upon the Commonwealth, and is continuing
to fight for the same reasons that the United
States of America and the Republic of
France are fighting—and that is to save their
democratic bacon. If the war is anything
at all it is a war of democracies to throttle
Imperialism and all the insane jealousies and
brutalities that Imperialism stands for.
Does anyone in all the world believe that
Uncle Sam is at war to back up some new-
found mission of Imperialism—that the
Allies are anywhere thinking in terms of
kings and emperors and far-flung flags and
subject peonies and particular brands of
kultur ? Yet The Bulletin has no real objec-
tions jo the Imperialist spreading his bom-
over the land in these days. From
thi purely Australian point of view it may
be a good thing for him to do so. But upon the
ground that any attempt to hinder the Aus-
tralian citizen from realising the direct signi-
ficance of the war to the Commonwealth must
have prejudicial effect upon recruiting, and
because moreover it must be offensive to our
Allies, this naper urges the Federal Govern-
ment to make the distribution of hot air
about “the Imperial destiny of our race”
and similar subjects an offence under the
War Precautions Act.
The Defeat of an Attempt to Misuse a
Court.
One result of the strike of last year was that
many unions in N. S. Wales had their regis-
tration cancelled, on application by the
Minister for Labor, for breach of awards
and law. The main result of the Governor-
General’s recruiting conference was a resolu-
tion, agreed to by the Labor representatives,
undertaking to assist enlistment, conditional
upon the removal of certain Labor griev-
ances, of which these de-registrations were
one. The promises given by N. S. Wales
Ministers on this point were definite. A
clause in the amending Arbitration Act
passed recently made provision for re-regis-
tration being sanctioned by the Court on an
application approved by the Minister. To
redeem the conference pledge Minister for
Labor Beeby submitted affidavits to the
Court the other day in support of applica-
tions made by 15 unions for re-registration.
The cases came before Justice Heydon, the
Chief Judge in Arbitration. The ground
relied upon by the Minister was that con-
currence would help recruiting. “These
unions,” he said in effect, “feel they are
being unjustly treated. Re-register them and
they undertake to help in obtaining re-
inforcements for the army.” The issue for the
Judge was to decide whether he, who had
held that de-registration was a proper
penalty to impose for breach of award,
should say that in his judgment the penalty
ought not to be paid provided the offenders
promised to encourage military enlistments.
Obviously the issue was political, not judi-
cial. Heydon, J., after a prolonged struggle
with himself, said “No.” He said the right
and the courageous thing. Had he said
“Yes” the answer would have set up a
precedent making the Court a mere mario-
nette, to be moved one way or another by the
Crown —would have been a proclamation
that the Court had abandoned its judicial
function and could be swayed by a politi-
cian’s sense of expediency. Australia has
seen something like that happen often
enough in the not remote past in relation to
industrial affairs not to want to see it again.
There was no reason why a Judge should
have been asked to say “Yes” except that of
political convenience. The re-registration of
these unions is essentially a matter for
Parliament —and now the Legislature is to
be summoned to special session to deal with
the matter. It is a queer sort of bargaining
that has been going on, and, strictly speak-
ing, the unions seeking registration are with-
out a leg to stand on, morally or otherwise.
They did a rotten thing, and on eye-for-eye
and tooth-for-tooth principles richly deserved
all they got. But it is better to heal a sore
than scarify it. The new Arbitration Act
is there for trial, anyhow, and no purpose is
served by shutting unions out from proceeding-
under an allegedly better system than pre-
vailed before. Parliament may therefore
properly do what it would have been grossly
improper for Justice IIeydon to do.
Wandering in the Wilderness.
The struggle of N. S. Wales Labor to dis-
cover where it is on the war generally and
the voluntary system in particular led to
wild and woolly happenings at Sydney
Trades Hall last week. Apparently there
are going to be more wild happenings at the
end of this week, and latest despatches from
the Front were rather more than suggestive
of a Trades Hall shaken to the deepest rock
of its foundations. The disturbance turns
on the resolution in regard to recruiting
carried at the Governor-General’s Confer-
ence. This was submitted to the Trades
Council for approval by President Morby,
one of the delegates to the Conference, and
before he had gone very far he had run into
serious trouble. An amendment declaring
the resolution to be out of order was carried
against him by 79 votes to 75, and by 84
votes to 01 portion of an executive minute
was expunged. Whereupon a long, verbose
amendment moved by Judd, a revolutionary
Socialist and a frequent anti-Labor candi-
date hitherto, became the substantive motion,
and after great uproar Morby adjourned the
proceedings. They were resumed on Thurs-
day amidst great excitement, the net result
being that Judd captured the situation and
Morby declared the proceedings adjourned
again. It seems beyond doubt that Morby
is in a minority, and that Judd’s pro-
gramme has the heavier backing. The gist
of his proposal is that, instead of Labor
pledging itself to assist the voluntary sys-
tem, it should “refuse to take part in a
recruiting campaign and call upon the work-
ers of all countries to urge their Govern-
ments to immediately secure an armistice on
all Fronts and initiate negotiations for
peace.” This paper expects to hear of this
or something like it being carried. Hands-up
though this be, it has strong support, in-
cluding that of the P.L.L. Executive. If it
is carried it will mean, in cold English, that
the official Labor machine has been so cap-
tured that a majority of trade-union reps,
in N. S. Wales are in favor of quitting in
the fight, of abandoning the army which was
sent away by a Labor Government and of
taking a German-made peace, for no other
kind of peace is at this stage within the
bounds of possibility.
Co-operation in Cockying.
Victoria has had a conference of farmers’
cooperative societies, with a small repre-
sentation from the other States; and if there
is among them an organiser with a square
chin and unbounded energy, something may
come out of the meeting in the shape of a
federation for wholesale buying and selling.
The conference had the benefit of the experi-
ence of two or three leaders of the move-
ment in Maoriland; and had these men come
into contact also with the Repatriation
Department it might have been greatly
to the country’s advantage. Certainly
if Australia had such co-operative soci-
eties as Maoriland possesses our pro-
blem of “repatriation.” so far as it
is connected with land settlement, would
be vastly easier. For these bodies have
been doing for years past precisely the work
that needs to be done for the returned men
who want to become farmers. In practi-
cally every district the now federated and
always more or less friendly societies have
agencies which keep them informed of land
and farms available. In Australia, if a
city man has wanted to put his son on the
land, he has found the job of selection and
subsequent sunervision surrounded bv almost
insurmountable difficulties. In Maoriland,
blessed with a dozen or more co-operative
societies —one of them alone has a business
of four millions a year—he is able to turn
to a body which will be a foster-father to the
beginner; will see that he gets the right sort
of farm at the right price; will see that it
is stocked right; will make an inspection
half-yearly or oftener, and guide the begin-
ner in the way he should go ; will see to his
supplies and to his sales. Obviously, quite
apart from the large financial gain to the
farmer from co-operative tradinsr, this is
a social service of great value. The butter-
makers of Australia know something of co-
operation ; but apart from these there is in
the. whole of the Commonwealth hardly any
of it worth mentioning. Even in S. Australia,
where there is a company of some size, the
co-operative activities are-woefully restricted.
That conditions should remain like this is
more deplorable than ever it was in view of
the Commonwealth’s urgent need for increas-
ing its production. But how is the machinery
to be started? The longer we leave the job
undone the more vested interests will there
be, and that means increased difficulties.
The Governments cannot afford to any longer
wait for the individual to move. In every
State there is a Department of Agriculture;
in most there is a Department of Labor or
Industry. Between them and the Depart-
ment of Repatriation they can and should
provide the initial movement. If the records
of the Agricultural Colleges are analysed it
will be found that not one-fifth of the students
settle down as farmers of any sort. There is
something radically wrong here. And this
paper believes the thing that is most wrong
is the absence of such co-operative organisa-
tions as Maoriland has, not only equipped to
make things easier for the farmer after he
has become established, but able and willing
to perform that foster-father service of which
every new settler on the land is in great
need.

Caesar’s Hun Wife.
The Labor Party stands for the immediate ces-
sation of hostilities and for the calling of an inter-
national conference to settle peace terms. Such
terms must include: (1) Withdrawal from invaded
territory and reparation by the invader; (2) Self-
government of all small nations; (3) Bight of people
of disputed territory to a vote on their own future
government; (4) International control of captured
possessions pending agreement; (5) No annexations
and no indemnities. —Labor manifesto to the electors
of Flinders (Vic.).
Caesar said on a memorable occasion that his
wife must be above suspicion; and if Mrs.
Caesar had been in the habit of staying out
all night in dubious company he would have
had still more reason for his remark. In a
somewhat similar fashion the Australian
Labor party, though it isn’t necessarily pro-
Hun, shouldn’t be always exciting the sus-
picion that it allows itself to be led by a
pro-Hun minority, either because it is com-
pletely enslaved by an astute crowd which
has captured the political machine, or
because it is too much accustomed to be gov-
erned by catch-words and phrases to realise
that the platitudes put forth in its name are
loaded. Certainly the above pronouncement
is loaded so heavily that it is a marvel the
gun doesn’t burst. It is the same old story
of giving the Hun the benefit of the doubt.
Let the proposals be considered seriatim: —
A conference is to be called to decide terms of
peace. These are to iuclude, among other things,
“withdrawal from invaded territory.” But while
the conference is sitting to decide whether the terms
are to include the withdrawal or not, the Hun will
naturally remain in possession of his invaded terri-
tories and may keep on plundering and outraging
the people and putting men and women and children
to the most awful kind of enforced labor, just as
before. For these things, as he has shown after the
peace in Russia and Uoumania, are not “hostilities.”
Also he may go on reorganising and strengthening
his armies in case the peace shouldn’t come off.
These aren’t “hostilities” either.
There is to be “reparation” to the plundered and
slaughtered and tortured and outraged peoples, the
reparation being decided on by a conference at
which Germany, Austria, Bulgaria and Turkey will
have a voice. The reparation can only be the
amount that the Hun will think it worth while pay-
ing as the price of peace till he is ready for war
again—if he thinks it worth while to pay anything.
At present he thinks (and says) that the Allies
should pay him.
Anyhow, “reparation” can only be for material
damage. All the losses through the slaughter and crip-
pling of the civilian population are our losses, for the
Allies have had no chance even if they felt inclined,
to butcher the unarmed population of Germany. As
it is impossible for the Allies to deliberately slay,
cripple, torture, infect or drive mad by cruelty a
similar number of Germans in order that the losses
in productive power and fighting power and mental
power will be equal, the only way to even partially
balance the account is to exact an indemnity.
The Labor party holds that , there should be no
indemnity. It might hurt the Hun’s feelings.
Also, there being no indemnity for fear of hurting
the Hun’s feelings, the people whom the Hun drove
into a hideously expensive war are to pay their own
bill. If the Labor man is damaged by his em-
ployer’s inadvertence —not by brutal malice but mere
carelessness —he rushes to court for hundreds of
pounds’ worth of indemnity. But he wouldn’t treat
the Hun like that on any account.
There are to' be no “annexations,” so the Hun is
to keep Alsace and Lorraine. They are German pro-
vinces—not “disputed territory.” There must be
two parties to make a dispute, and Germany refuses
to be one.
People of “disputed territories” —which can only
mean such places as Luxemburg, Serbia, Russian
Poland, Courland, Livonia, etc., which used to belong
to one party and are now held and governed by
another —are to have a vote re their own future
government. Not a decisive vote—only a vote. And
nothing is said as to how the people in possession
are to be presented from supervising the vote, and
voting themselves, and also deciding on the qualifi-
cations of voters. The people in possession are
Germans in every case. So there is to be a poll,
with the Hun as returning-officer unless somebody is
strong enough to drag him away, and this poll is to
resolve whether the Hun will or will not finish by
keeping his steals.
As regards “possessions”— i.e., places like German
Papua, East Africa, South-west Africa, etc., where
the population hasn’t reached the voting stage—they
are to be handed over by their present holders to
an international control. The people who hold cap-
tured “possessions” are Britain, Belgium (which got
some of its own back in Africa) and Japan (in a
small way) and these Allies are to retire in favor
of a committee of nations which will necessarily in-
clude some of our four enemies.
So the Hun is to keep all his old territories in
Europe, for the Allies are nowhere on Hun ground
in Europe and his, old estate hasn’t come into dis-
pute. And he is to have a gamble for the retention
of much stolen territory in Europe and all his
possessions elsewhere. The loss by military expenses
is to be divided, each party paying its own costs.
The loss by material damage is to be divided, Ger-
many paying something, assessed in part by itself
and its Allies, for breakages. The loss of strength
and reproductive power caused, not by war but by
the promiscuous murder or disablement of peaceful
fathers and mothers and prospective fathers and
mothers, is to fall entirely on the Allies.
And in another clause of the precious mani-
festo it is specified that the “humiliation” of
Germany must be avoided. The humiliation
of Belgium isn’t even mentioned. What Hun
agent inspires this sort of literature?
Rahab.
“T. Pallid” : One practical detail of Miss
Rout’s scheme which appears to have
escaped notice in the ethical mix-up between
“Dr. Nil,” Mr. “H. 0.” and others is the
unfortunate but undeniable fact that no
guarantee of even reasonable freedom from
risk of disease is afforded by any system of
woman-examination. The most careful of
prostitutes, when at all busily engaged in
the general practice of her profession, is
practically certain to be infectious at fre-
quent periods, even if she herself is not
actively infected. The local and constitu-
tional signs of infectivity are frequently
absent, often slight, and usually intermit-
tent. The Americans in Manila insti-
tuted a most elaborate system for the
examination of prostitutes. The ordinary
visual examination was carried out in
a particularly thorough manner by highly-
skilled specialists; yet out of every hun-
dred cases of discovered infection, only
40 were found on examination, and the
balance of GO were detected in the labora-
tory a couple of days after the lady had gone
back to business. The experts estimated
that, despite all the care taken, about 50
per cent, of infectious prostitutes were un-
detectable at any given examination, owing
to intermittency. The detection of infec-
tivity in the male is almost equally diffi-
cult. Persons of either sex may be viru-
lently infectious and yet be quite unaware
of the fact. A tiny whitish spot on a
mucous membrane may swarm with the
spirochaetes of syphilis. A reddened spot
several inches inside the uretha is all there
is to show for the presence of myriads of
gonorrhoea germs, a survival from some pre-
vious infection of years before. This scribe,
an experienced old bird, would counsel Miss
Rout to give up the idea of starting a
“clean” establishment. It simply can’t be
done. The best results are got from the
issue of preventive appliances against infec-
tion. and the instruction of the men in the
method of using them effectively. The
“clean” prostitute is a physical impossibility.
“The Irish Answer.”
“J. Wallace Greer” takes a point for the
Presbyterians:—-
In The Bulletin (16/5/’18) “Henry Horse-
collar” propounds his theory for the settlement of
the Irish question. But I flatly deny his insinua-
tion that it is the Presbyterians alone who
are the cause of the Home Rule deadlock. Why,
the leaders of the Anti-Home Rule movement during
(say) the last 30 years have been nearly all mem-
bers of the Anglican Church, better known in Ire-
land as the Church of Ireland. What about the late
Colonel Saunderson, one of the staunchest and most
uncompromising there has ever been of Anti-
Home Rulers? Then also the late Marquis of
Londonderry, the present Earl of Erne, Colonel
Wallace, Sir James H. Stronge, E. M. Archdale
and dozens of other prominet Ulstermen whom I
could mention, all members of the Anglican Church.
Ulster’s opposition to Home Rule appears to come
from the Presbyterians, because the population of
that portion of Ulster opposing Home Rule is largely
Presbyterian. According to “Whitaker’s Almanack”
for 1915 Presbyterians formed at that time 10 per
cent, of the population of Ireland, and Protestant
Episcopalians (i.e., Anglicans) 13.1 per cent. The
difference comes in here —that whilst nearly the
whole 10 per cent, of Presbyterians are to be
found in (say) the Counties of Antrim, Down,
Armagh, Londonderry and Tyrone, the 13.1 per cent,
of Protestant Episcopalians are scattered all over
Ireland, and are, therefore, from the nature of their
surroundings, unable to use their numbers so effec-
tively as the smaller but more compact body of
Presbyterians in Ulster. It is only fair to the
Irish Presbyterians that the above should be made
known. ,
And “Hawkesbury” protests that it won’t
suit the Irish :—
“Henry Horsecollar” proposes to answer the
Irish question by transporting a minority section of
Irish people. That is a Saxon remedy utterly useless
and undesirable to an Irishman. The true Irishman
is the dark, black-eyed Iberian; the invaders include
Celts, Scandinavians and Saxons; the Welsh —most
of the Fitzes— and Scotch, the last a blend of Piets,
Danes and Celts; also a percentage of Savoyards. In
result the Iberian has put his brand on the whole
crew, and their character has been constant. National-
ism has always been subordinated to sectionalism.
An Irishman delights in rows as many an Australian
delights in beer ; it is a great diversion. Could you
abolish the local thirst by transporting the breweries?
The injustice to Ireland is largely myth ; but the
Irishman would not lose that myth for worlds. His
land, school and church difficulties were remedied;
the country is more prosperous than ever before; but
the inherent delight in a grievance and a shindy
remains. The Irishman is willing to flght the
foreigner, but he is more willing to fight the man in
the next county, and still more willing to fight the
man over the way. Take the National party. To
outsiders it had a clean-cut policy; but it was rent
in mortal feud by the Redmondites and the Ilealy-
ites. What the difference was about no one out of
Ireland, and very few in it, understood. But what
did that matter? It was a row. Neither parish coun-
cillor nor muck carter could get a job unless he took
sides.
While an Irishman lives there must always be an
Irish question; and that is where he interests Aus-
tralia, for he is here, his everlasting grievance
with him. If he is inclined to slumber on it, a
newly-arrived Irish prelate prods him up, and no
sooner lands than he trails the tail of his coat. The
North of Ireland Orangeman is equally willing; a
good barney is meat and drink to both of them.
And it is on this point that “Henry Horsecollar”
and the majority of alien writers make their miss.
They look for peace, order and tranquillity as an
ideal; while the Irish instinct is for excitement and
variety.
On such a people clearly we cannnot impose our
ideas, or take their words and acts to have the com-
mon significance. We might, however, take a tip
from Archbishop Mannix and keep Australia for Aus-
tralians ; which would mean the barring of all im-
ported prelates and church dignitaries.

bound by the State Land Tax valuations and are
deprived of the right to vary those valuations or to
hear appeals against them disqualifies the Act, as it
stands, for life. First of all, the councils must pay
the State Commissioner of Taxation for copies of the
valuations upon which the rates are to be based, and

under a system of patronage which was responsible

supporting the local Parliamentary rep.? In one
case the writer knows of the State valuations were
made between drinks in a little office in the township
by an old fossil who knew nothing when sober and
only half that when fuddled. When he got tangled
over the task he called in an equally incompetent
assistant and drank with him. The valuations are
not made upon each separate tenement but upon
the whole of a man’s property irrespective of whether
it is in one, two or three municipalities or sub-
divisions, and the brilliant Act will not allow the
municipalities to make the necessary adjustments
themselves, and without adjustments innumerable
in country shires it would not be possible to enrol
the occupier. A great many councils realise the
advantages of the system and are anxious to adopt it
when made practicable; but repeated requests for
amendment of the Act during the last three yean)
have not resulted in any more than promises. It
takes something to get Bumble to scrap his conser-
vatism and adopt a progressive system such as rating
unimproved values. It is madness to choke him off
just as he has taken hold of the idea.

Mars Resumes Business.
Apparently the lid has been lifted from Hell
again. The German artillery on the West-
ern Front delivered heavy attacks on Sun-
day, and on Monday the enemy began to
move to a new address. At the time of
fvriting the news was only in the making,
but it appeared that the Germans had ad-
vanced in some parts of the Rheims and
Soissons sector, north-east of Paris. Here
there has hitherto been heavy fighting; close
by the French gained certain tactical advan-
tages at very heavy cost just before the first
battle of the Somme. Whether the enemy
is definitely committing himself to a deci-
sive struggle in this place remains to be seen.
If he is lie will certainly get badly handled,
whatever else happens, for the ground is very
difficult and the French defenceis are excep-
tionally strong. If he gains any material
advantage, something can be expected from
the Crown Prince’s army, which sits to the
westward. This force, massed in the Valley
of the Oise, is the spearhead from which
trouble will most likely come to Paris if it
comes at all. Its participation to any great
extent in the battle will be a not very
pleasant sign ; it will mean that Ludendokff
reckons he has a sure winning hand. There was
fighting, too, on Monday in the vicinity of
Ypres, a region already heavily drenched with
British and German blood. If, as the first
advance suggests, this is only a side-show, we
shall soon hear of more important happenings
not far away. Probably by way of prepar-
ing us for the new outburst another long rig-
marole from the Secretary for the Colonies
has been published, allegedly giving “a review
of the situation.” It is even cruder than the
last, every line colored by the shallow
optimism of stupidity. The Bulletin has
already protested against this poisonous stuff
being scattered in Australia. Every repeti-
tion is an added insult to an intelligent
people. It is good to hear of the American
success in transnorting men to France. There
is reason for feeling more cheerful about the
submarine since Lloyd George spoke about
recent successes, though it has to be remem-
bered that previous statements by the Prime
Speechmaker on the same subject were tragi-
cally misleading. But the significance of
these things will be in better focus when we
hear of what happens between now and the
end of the battle which opened on Sunday.
If
There are two phrases which during
Labor’s recent search for a war policy have
been given the benediction of much applause:
The time is ripe for the working class of this and
other countries to make emphatic declaration that
the war must end.
and
If the workers had refused to take up arms there
would have been no war.
But whenever the worker of Belgium makes
-an emphatic declaration that it is time the
war came to an end the German either
shoots him or sends him to dig trenches. The
Russian worker also made the most emphatic
declaration about the time being ripe for
the war to stop. He downed tools, dissolved
the army and went home. Whereupon the
German followed him, and is busy annexing
his country and collaring all the available
bread and meat. If the entire population
of Italy, France, Servia, Britain. Ireland,
the United States, Australia and S. Africa
simultaneously declared that the time was
ripe, the only possible result of the yell
would be to emphasise the inescapable issue
put by the German to them all long ago—
tight or quit! In a vague, remote sort of
way the aforesaid phrases are profoundly
true; but used in relation to the conditions
of the moment they are wildly grotesque.
The choice Australia and other countries
have is not between war and peace, but be-
tween resisting defeat and taking defeat
lying down. There is tragedy either way.
In one direction there is what Russia is
getting as well.
Wanted, a Stop-the-War Programme.
A long resolution has been put before Syd-
ney Trades Council as an alternative to
giving assistance in making tjie voluntary
plan a success. One clause is as follows:
That the greatest service we can render the men
at the Front, their loved ones at home, and humanity
in general is to do all in our power to stop the
war.
In the event of this being carried, what do
they propose to do? Mere talk is no good.
That won’t stop the war, any more than
Hughes’s talk could win it. But there arc
many ways by which, if “stopping the
bloody thing” is all that counts, Australia
could do something effective. A general
strike; the release of all interned Germans;
the supply of coal to enemy raiders; the
handing back of Rabaul to the Kaiser; the
refusal of pay or pensions to soldiers —these
and other things would at least help a little
to bring the proceedings to an end. They
are the only things Tiie Bulletin can think
of as likely to be the least bit effective. If
they are not in the scheme of the promoters
of this resolution, what is? What do they
propose to do to stop the war? To merely
say that the best service they can render
humanity is to “do all in their power” and
then do nothing looks like playing a confi-
dence trick on humanity. This paper wants
a programme.
