Every five minutes or so someone explains
that Australia possesses magnificent timbers,
that with an intelligent forestry system there
would bo. no more need to import wood than
there is to drag coal to the Commonwealth
—and so on. Now Australia is about to
begin building some steamers. A great deal
of wood will be required for the internal fit-
tings of these vessels; and the official specifi-
cations provide that, except for a little red-
gum, the whole of it shall be imported white
pine, oregon and kauri. Cargo fattens, deck-
coverings, hatches, awning-ridges, boat-
chocks, gangways, cross-bunkers, bridge
bulkheads, topmasts, signal yard and even
the flagpole must be made of imported tim-
ber. The Australian flag made of imported
materials attached to imported halyards run-
ning up and down an imported pole rounds
off the shipbuilding farce quite nicely.
“Plain Australian” : “Ruraidh O’Conor”
produces a fine coil of real home-cured Irish
history, but he does not answer my question
as to how long Ireland would “stand erect”
if the protecting hand of Britain were struck
down, and the Boche got in the boot. Real
history differs from Irish history, and it
records no serious attempt by Norman Eng-
land to invade Ireland. Giraldus Cambren-
sis, who was sent across by the Norman con-
querors to investigate, reported that the coun-
try was not worth the trouble of subduing,
but that if it were decided upon there would
be little difficulty on account of the purehas-
ability of the local chiefs. It also records
that Elizabeth and Ckomwell both were
forced to send military expeditions to Ireland
to teach the Irish that they were not per-
mitted to commit atrocities on the English
and Scottish settlers. On both occasions Ire-
land got a lesson which is remembered by
her people to this day. In the eighteenth
century, again, she got another on account of
the same kind of treachery as she is attempt-
ing to practise to-day. Does “Ruraidh”
know the air of “Croppies, Lie Down,” and
what the song stands for? I made no men-
tion of the Irish question, but only of Ire-
land. Naturally Australians take some in-
terest in the Irish question, as they do in
snakes and the Red Plague, and for the same
reasons. But if Ireland sank at its moorings
to-morrow, the Australian feeling—including
that’of a large part of the alleged third of
Irish-descended —would be one of relief that
England had one less chance of being stabbed
in the back as she fought, and that there
would be no more Mannixes to import. The
writer is not, as “Ruraidh” pleasantly sug-
gests, of German descent. If he were he
would not ask inconvenient questions of the
countrymen of Casement and the Sinn Fein
traitors.

“Bart” : Most of what has been written
about “Motherhood” is pretty, but cuts no
ice. We want a practical encouragement
for the baby-producing just the
same as for other vital industries. Free-
trade has failed here as elsewhere. Dr.
Arthur got very near the right track. His
principle was right but his method wrong.
Direct bonuses don’t hit the spot, and there
is too much room for abuse. How many in-
dustries do bonuses encourage compared
with a Protective tariff? Protect the baby
industry by a good tariff and fill the cradles.
I*i short, extend the Federal system of allow-
ing father a deduction from income ta± for
every child. To begin with, increase the tax;
but for every child make a substantial deduc-
tion —not a deduction that is farcical. I
paid my State (Tasmanian) income tax the
other day and was permitted to deduct ss.
in respect of two children under 16: at the
rate of 2s. 6d. each! With a nice family
of eight I would save £1 per annum in State
income tax! Let the deductions be gradu-
ated. If a man have an income of £3UO per
annum ana one child, let him deduct £5
actual tax; if two children £l2; if three £2O,
and so on. If a man have £lOOO a year, no
deduction in respect of children need be
allowed. The production of degenerates
would not be encouraged—observe I do not
say it would be discouraged—for the simple
reason that in the vast majority of cases
the degenerate parents would not have tax-
able incomes, and therein the deduction has
a huge advantage over the bonus system.
“Fax” : There’s matter for a hundred for-
cible sermons in a recent assertion of
“Glen” in The Bulletin. “Vic. railway
toilers are divided into two main sections —
‘sports’ and ‘wowsers.’ The sport is a good
bloke who doesn’t mind pinching a half-case
of grapes, £5 worth of roofing iron or the in-
toshicated racegoer’s roll of notes. The
wowser, of course, is the unpleasant man
who won’t.” Those two terms aforesaid are
doing more harm than any 50 other words
in the language. O Sport, what sins are
committed in thy name! The boy or girl
who gets unwillingly led into temptation by
the cheap sarcasm of a pal is offered the
choice between being a “sport” or a “wow-
ser,” and the poor weakling elects to be a
sport. Most of the illicit babies one hears
of ought to be christened “Sportling.”
They were begotten in the name of “sport,”
which, by the way, had a specially amorous
significance in Siiakspeare’s time. The
maiden who takes all the risks that Mother
Grundy warns her to avoid is a “good
sport.” The incitement to take a drink,
where drink spells danger, is “Be a snort,”
whilst the popular argument against pru-
dence, honorable scruples or modest impulse
is “Don’t be a wowser.” The latter is the
more mischievous, perhaps, because among
intelligent youngsters the tendency to dis-
like wowsers is stronger than the admiration
of sports. No alternative is offered to the
tempted one. The object of this modern mis-
application of the old term and the new
must either sport or wowse. The one was
invented as a term of endearment for easy
frivollers; the other as a label for saintly
hypocrites. And the Devil took them both
into his vocabulary.
Said the Kaiser recently :
Right will eventually prevail.
Pity he didn’t warn 8010 Pasha of that.
From the Gallery :
Captain S. M. Bruce, the new member for Flin-
ders (Vic.), in the House of Reps., holds his pre-
decessor’s place in the back corner bench on the
Government side. Bruce is abcut the first genuine
whiff of Flinders-lane to blow in. He is also the
firs£ decorated warrior, and would have been our
first curio from an English University if Willie
Kelly had never been born. Willie, however,
burnished his bleat at Oxford, whereas Bruce shook
all the vulgarisms out of his vocabulary at Cam-
bridge. He is a tall, good-looking Australian
with a dark, glamorous eye and an air of trying to
conceal his modesty behind a camouflage of ennui.
“The Pen Drawings of Norman Lindsay”
was a success from the jump. 1500 out 'of
the 2000 copies of the 12s. fid. edition were
sold to “the trade” within three days of pub-
lication, and the _ whole of the three-guineii
edition (150 copies) was taken up before
publication. There is no doubt that, as the
cheap edition will not be reprinted, it will
quickly go up in value.
“Sapper”: The Bulletin’s protest
against the militarisation of the non-military
services controlled by the Defence Depart-
ment might have gone further. At Caul-
field Hospital masseurs and other special
attendants have to frequently knock off work
and learn how to do some utterly useless
and unnecessary parade tricks. I am not
going to say that this means that some of
us have to forego our proper treatment.
But I do say that if we get our proper
treatment under this system a smaller num-
ber could give it to us. at less cost to the
country under an intelligent system. It
isn’t deemed necessary for the attendants at
our great public and private hospitals to
know anything about forming fours. The
sooner the same principles of commonsense
are applied to military administration the
better.
All things come to the John who waits
long enough at the corner. Detective-ser-
geant T. J. Kiely, of Victoria, has just been
promoted to first-class sergeant and given
another bob a day. He passed the qualify-
ing exam. 27 years ago.

week at Sydney Majesty’s, is a satisfactory

by seizing her husband’s coat-tnils as he

Georgiana. Connie Ediss is once more the
impossible widow three times removed who
distrusts man. Frith, wearing the inane
look of an inquisitive gander, succeeds again
as Wowser Wix. His flat-footed walk-out
after singing “Snatched from the Cradle’’
suggests that lie has studied earnest deacons
going up the aisle to get the collection-plate.
Field Fisher as Percy Pinklove, the hoarse,
sun-cured cadger of the deep, is one of the
joys of the show. Cecil Bradley makes a
dainty Dorothy whose song-words are always
heard, and there’s a recall for Madge Elliott
after one of her dances. Tom Preston, Jack
Hooker and little Rita Nugent do the comedy
stepping. “Three Twins” arrive on Satur-
day. The advt. says these children amused
New York for two years on end.
“The Bird of Paradise” will escape from
Sydney Cri. to-morrow night, leaving not a
feather behind. After that it will be “The
Easiest Way,” which seems like the answer
to a great universal conundrum about win-
ning wars or getting out of debt, or some-
thing.

Now, Cyril walked in purity,
A pastor of the Malbourne fields;
But meeting with fair Alma Lee
He to the old temptation yields.
And, consequences coming, they
At night-time try to find a way.
They met at the old trysting-place,
In Winter when the frost was white;
O’er his black coat and fear-drawn face
He wore a coat and hat of light
Grey tweed, the clothes of Everard,
By Cyril’s sister Lil hit hard.
The father of the girl, Ben Lee,
With loaded gun has tracked the twain,
But by a trick of Fate’s decree,
He, not the Parson, is the slain.
Cyril clears home, while, lying hard,
Alma denounces Everard.
For eighteen years he’s sent to jug,
(Lily remaining always true),
While Cyril lived in quarters snug,
Revered by everyone he knew.
And though his conscience seared and stung,
Dean Cyril Maitland held his tongue.
Then comes his son, young Benjy Lee,
From Canada by Alma sent,
Bearing a letter saying she,
Dying, has now grown penitent.
Ihe Dean denies his son—though wrung
By deep remorse, still holds his tongue.
Then Everard, at liberty,
Flies straight to Lily’s loving breast,
And in her brother’s deanery,
A letter writes to say ’tis best
For all hands now to hold their -tongue.
On reading this the Dean wide flung
The door of silence closed for years,
And in the old Cathedral grey,
He tells his guilt while Death’s grim shears
Snap his life-thread in tragic way.
From many an eye the tear is wrung
For that frail Dean who held his tongue.
So once more “The Silence of Dean Mait-
land” threw its gentle spell over a crowded
Opera House in Sydney on Saturday night.
The dialogue is redolent of Evensong. Bible
Classes and Mothers’ Meetings; but George
Cross manages to invest the yes-no Dean
with an appealing dignity he doesn’t deserve.
Lorna Forbes, as Lilian, sister of the Dean
and bespoken wife to Everard, comes through

The faithful movie photographer has done
his bit again. Mae Marsh, now progressing
down “Sunshine Alley” at Sydney Royal, is
brilliantly nictured in hundreds of feet of
film, and the story is beautiful enough to
make any young person with a heart sniff
sympathetically in the gloom.
“Time, Please!” is fairly started on its
non-stop run at Sydney Tivoli, and the word
has gone round the suburbs thau it’s a din-
kum show. The de luxe picture matinees
of “Barbary Sheep” keep the doorkeepers
and ushers on the job every afternoon.
** * *
The Scarlet Troubadours are still working
at Sydney Playhouse, which hides at the
far end of Castlereugh-street. But the show
is worth the pilgrimage.
*
At Hoyt’s and the Haymarket in Sydney
Billie Burke is getting married in haste
owing to the inconsiderate invasion of a
Belgian town by the Prussians. At the
same house Mae Marsh handles a miniature
zoo in “Sunshine Alley,” and if Le Souef
sees the way she does it lie’ll offer her a job
at Taronga, and then there will be trouble
with Robert Harron, the millionaire lover
with the hard fist. The Crystal Palace hangs
out a sporting placard, “Kick In.” The
story doesn’t, however, deal with the foot-
ball season —only with the efforts of a re-
formed crook to lead the higher life.
“Mother o’ Mine” is a charming sketch at
the Alhambra, and at the Empress Billie
Burke revives “Peggy,” and the inimitable
Charley does a juggle with “Dough and
Dynamite.”

Tlie cinema drama which Captain Conant
produced at Melbourne Auditorium the
other night, under the title of
“His Only Chance,” was interesting only to
those whose ghosts figured on the screen, and
their friends who had come to see the
spectres. The photography of the film was
ah right; but the gallant captain’s story was
stereotyped melodrama in its nakedest sim-
plicity. The “author” filled the largest
amount of individual space in his artless
narrative, playing an alleged German spy
who came to a hasty end from cold poison.
“Thus Germany’s greatest agent met his
doom,” or words to that effect. Whilst the
author was assuming the evil smile of the
Greatest Spy another military person dodged
around as the weak-minded little johnnie
who fell into the clutches of a tame adven-
turess and gave her a forged cheque for
£2OOO when she threatened to tell his papa.
Truly, a cinema drama of surpassing silli-
ness. The sassiety ladies were horribly dis-
figured by their facial make-up and the
glittering crowd alleged to be arriving at a
music-hall at night was evidently entering
Melbourne Majesty’s in broad daylight and
evening dress. A couple of dancing turns
from “The Bing Boys” and a horse-race
scene relieved the puerility of the drama;
also Jack Cannot and Clyde Cook introduced
some cheerful comic relief. Even then the

What had been
billed as a Guy
Bates Post matinee
for the Red Cross
at Melbourne
King’s turned out
to be mainly a con-
genial sassiety en-
tertainment, with
hardly enough of
G.B.P. to leaven the lump. It was an
.afternoon-out for the Most Expensive
People—worthy say, £lOOO to the Fund and
£250 to the florists. It provided vast scope
for ti'ibutes, the final item being a patriotic
tableau with talk in it, in which a number of
dumb lady-conti’ibutors were not forgotten
wdien vegetables were served. Miss Veitch
had devised the tableau, so the speaking parts
were adequately filled and the notion of
Britannia calling to Australia to do her bit
was worked up to a dramatic climax. A
tall, beauteous lady who sang indifferently
in a nurse’s garb, supported by half a dozen
of the saddest singing soldiers that Dennis
ever rhymed of, had beem stage-managed by
Florrie Green. Two of Miss Green’s star
pupils, Margot Lester and Winnie Rossiter,
did solo dance turns, and both were inter-
esting. The toe dancer, Miss Lester, made
a clever attempt to do as Halina Schmolz
use' to do in the Swan dance, and succeeded
very well in soite of the orchestra. Little
Miss Rossiter also had a depressing orches-
tral accompaniment for a song from “The
BHle of New York,” but her gestures and
dancing were charming. Walter Ivirby and
Mrs. Norman Brookes were associated in a
sort of harem scene, where even Walter’s
second-best vocalism couldn’t atone for his
appalling appearance in a chocolate face and
black chin-whiskers. Adele Ritchie (Mrs.
Post) had the advantage of a plain piano
accompaniment in two or three light dramatic
songs, artistically rendered. Her tuneiul
tale of the restaurant proprietress was the
best of the batch.

“The Bing Boys’’ will cease to bing at
Melbourne Majesty’s after Friday of next
week. By that time everybody will have
seen the show that came with a bonzer repu-
tation from Sydney, though the local appe-
tite for revue is not so keen that the multi-
tude in general can eat it greedily and come
for more. Even the Ladder of Life scene
which concludes the sliivoo is said to lack
variety. It is a great feast of shapely legs,
but, the limbs being all of one sort, the effect
is a trifle monotonous, and as a display of
healthy physical exercise it hardly appeals
to ladies who object to climbing stairs. How-
ever, the positively last week of Bing Boys
will doubtless be rushed. So many people have
seen the show that the others can’t afford to
say they died without having done likewise.
“Katinka,” which is musical comedy, will
have a first outing in Australia when the
revue makes way for it.

Guy Bates Post continues to make “The
Masquerader” gee at Melbourne King’s. This
is the fifth week of the Loder in Chilcote’s

** * *
“The Million Dollar Girl,” at Melbourne
Tivoli, was an easy matter to liven up, and
the Samples company has done it fairly well.
The enlivened proceedings don’t sparkle like
a diamond on the shirt-front of Solomon,
but they keep the pay-boxes busy, so all is
well. A good word may be said for Vera
Spaull (formerly of infantile charactei’s)
and her Ten Little Bridesmaids. The idea
of the song oidginated with the Ten Little
Nigger Boys of a London panto, which hap-
pened in great-grandfathei , ’s time, and has
been adapted to a hundred subsequent re-
quirements. The Bmpii-e Girls, of the
Rickards period, used it in their collective
ditty of Six Little Bui-gulars. Bantock
applied it to his staff of Wives in “San
Toy.” It sounds now like an echo in the
caverns of memory, and comes as a reminder
that the songs of the fathers are visited
upon the children unto the third and fourth
generation. But the enduring idea must have
a snap in it.

One night last week the Liedertafel gave
an attractive Red Cross concert at Mel-
boui-ne Town Hall, the big crowd of male
voices rendering some part-songs impress-
ively between the solo items. This society
is the suiwivor of two that used to be when
amateur c-horistei’s were more plentiful and
enthusiastic in Melbourne than they are
nowadays, and it was only for the sake of old
times that a small majority of members re-
cently rejected president James Dyer’s pro-
posal to change the name of the concern.
The title might as well be changed, for all
that. Under the name of Sons of Song, or
Offspring of Harmony, they would sing as
sweet, and if there ever was any excuse for
calling them a Liedertafel it had vanished
long before the war attached a stink to the
term.

Concerning a Melbourne Art show:—
At the Athenaeum Gallery a little show of oil-
paintings, closing to-morrow (31st), has deserved
more notice than it has had. Not that the artist,
A. E. Newbury, is quite unblameable for the neg-
lect. The average patron of pleasing landscapes is
apt to take exception to the painter’s impressionist
tendencies, especially in his green or pallid-brown
“The Scarlet Trouba-
dours,” at Sydney
Playhouse.
Renn Millar warbles
a lay.
groundwork. One is
struck by the fact
that a carefully-
finished landscape
(lent by Dr. Sewell),
with a crowded city
in the distance and a
distinctly Coroistie
atmo.-phere, is the most
attractive canvas in
the collection. ~/ - Some
of the 60 pictures are
even more smudgy in
the foreground than
others; and the pro-
mising appearance of
a frankly unfinished
work on the end wall
would be more im-
pressive but for a
doubt as to the amount
of finish it is going to
get. But when all
the shortcomings in
Labor are admitted,
the truth remains
that Newbury gets
some charming effects
of trees at their green-
est. He deals with
nature in shade rather than sunshine. His art has
no time for Australia dry and dusty. The pictures
are restful and soothing in their manner,
and when he paints a big tree in the central
mid-distance (No. 24—“ By the Basket Willows”)
the visitor feels tempted to look around the trunk
for the best place to sit down and loaf. Four par-
ticularly likeable productions are Nos. 9, 29, 37 and
39, the last two being catalogued at the war-time
price of five guineas only* A lot of people should
give A. E. Newbury’s first show an encouraging
look-in before it disappears. They are bound to
look longingly at “An Autumn Morning” (16) and
“Still Morning” (17) ; also the view of Yarra at
Richmond (23), which has the delicacy of a water-
color.

The opening exhibit for this week at Mel-

❖* * *
The week’s topliner at Hoyt’s in Mel-
bourne is Gabrielle Ray in “Arms and the
Girl.” The story happens in Belgium under
the German domination. There Gabrielle, as
a prisoner, employs her beauty to soften the
rigors of her confinement, and reveals such
a gift for reducing Hun generals to a lamb-
like compliance that she ought to be smug-
gled into Belgium to end the war. Vivian
Martin is another working bee in the cur-
rent programme. She works through the
honey-sodden narrative, “Giving Becky a
Chance.”

Phil Newbury and Amy Murphy have
dropped into Melbourne Bijou. Their reper-

thoughts, and the audience greedily swallows

latest “queen of syncopation,” roars friendlv
ditties in a voice that sounds like a cold
mixed with brass filings. Two other new-
comers, McLean and Lee, have a cheeky
quarter of an hour in a sketch wherein the
language is slightly moistened with Beer.
Art with the smallest a trickles off the
brush of a painter named Little, who carica-
tures W. M. Hughes and concludes with a
pictorial tribute to the “hero” Les Darcy.
Now The Bulletin is wondering whether
Darcy knew that Mr. Little was going to do
it.

plans. At other times there are long asides,

“Unsuccessful Candidate for a Portfolio” :
The deficiencies of Prime Minister Hughes have
been more than ever realised since William Watt took
over his job. Hughes’s temper is bad, likewise his
unpunctuality, and his small manoeurves, and his
hurry which never gets him there any faster, and his
lurky or round-the-corner way of doing things; but
his cat-on-hot-bricks tendency is worst of all. He
gives an impression that he always wants to leap
up and down on the same spot while in conversation.
A member (you can guess his name in twice) said
of him that he was never in one place but always in
another or just leaving it. His thin constitution is
probably responsible for most of these things. If
Hughes could be turned out for agistment in a rich
paddock and fattened, lie might be a new man.
Every year since N. S. Wales smashed
the agreement for a uniform railway gauge
someone has remarked that the dreadful
piece of cussedness should he repaired, and
that each year increases the cost of the
alteration. To which the answer (when
there was any answer) was to the general
effect that: (1) It was a matter requiring
much consideration; (2) it couldn’t be
rushed; (8) there were many interests to
be considered; (4) it would be difficult to
get the States to arrive at an agreement;
(5) the division of the cost would be a
serious matter; (6) it would be wise to
appoint a Commission, and send experts
abroad to measure the width of foreign rail-
ways. An estimate made 21 years ago was
that the adoption of the 4ft. Bsin. gauge all
round would cost about £4,000,000. Six years
ago it was reckoned the job would cost about
£37,000,000. It would cost a lot more now,
and will cost a great deal more later on. Any-
howls years’ deep thought over this inevitable
problem meant a loss of £33,000,000, or
£2,200,000 a year, or £42,300 per week,
which is a heavy nrice to pay for thinking,
especially as many of the thinkers never had
a real think in their lives.
“Tekelo”: Paragraph 8 of a recent
petition to the Secretary of State to con-
stitute the Solomon Islands a Crown Col-
ony runs:—
That the whole interests, agricultural, commercial,
financial and political, as well as the entire popula-
tion, European and native, are opposed to these
Islands being placed under the control of the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth. The natives will readily
grant a deed of cession to the Crown direct, but it is
doubtful if they will do so to any other Government.
The petition also accuses the Commonwealth
of being “unsympathetic in regard to the
conditions which are essential to their
tSolomon Islands’] well being.” The truth
is that the whole of the interests in this
Group (and I’m on the spot) are not
opposed to Australian control. The native
population knows absolutely nothing about
politics, white or black. To infer that it
does is pure perversion, and the talk of the
natives granting deeds of cession to one
Government as against another is a wild
jest. Which way the native jumps depends
wholly on who engineers the performance.
It has been customary to regard it as
musical comedy when one more little Eatin-
American republic issued its declaration of
war against Germany. But the little States
know what they are doing. No one could
be more anxious than they to keep out of
the trouble and sell sugar and coffee to all
parties. But the Ilun residents—planters,
mine-owners and, above all, commercial
men—became a curse. They established
centres of information, wireless stations,
petrol-supply stations, etc,, in unexpected
places, for the Hun benefit, and the
allied Powers said things that were
unpleasant; and yet short of interning
the Hun population, which a neutral coun-
try could hardly do, the Government of El
Dorado or San Pacifico didn’t quite know
how to stop the annoyance. Corners were
worked in local products to secure the whole
supply for the Hun and leave none for the
Allies; and when that caused trouble the
Boche explained at needless length to the
owner of the country that every country
would shortly be Boche country. Just for
the joy of running that Boche into a prison
camp and seizing his treasonable papers,
and shooting him under martial law if
necessary, States no bigger than two-and-
sixpence rushed gladly into war and told the
Kaiser to do his worst.

The big red lamp of Chemist Keat,
Shone out along the rain-soaked street;
While all within glowed warm and bright,
In contrast to the gale-swept night.
The customers were frequent, too,
Keat still a steady trade could do;
For wise was he, and made that sure
By “pushing” Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.

“C.T.R.”: Usually I’m no lover of argu-
ment, but I worked up a fine frenzy and
vanquished various army surgeons who
wanted to take off my leg after it had been
smashed at the knee by the recoil of a field-
igun. I must admit it was a hopeless-looking
WHEN BILLY COMES SAILING
HOME.
Making good use of the pretext that economy was
called for, woman shortened her skirts shortly after
the beginning of the war. —Fashion note.
Returned Soldier ( mistaking his wife for
his daughter ) : “Why, Esther, darling,
how tall you’ve grown! You must he
nearly as hig as your mother now!”
mess; biW it was my leg and I wanted to
keep it, if only to limp with. As I was
passed on fivm hospital to hospital, I got
to know and hate the leg-snatchers who
wanted to lop like a tree and prune me
like a hedge. They meant well, these flesh
gardeners; but one man’s operation is an-
other man’s meat, and I could never imag-
ine myself chasing a crust on crutches. One
bv one I beat them off and derided their
professional prophecies. Now lam home,
willing to walk miles any. day, and don’t
►even use a stick. It was a Suvla Bay man
who wrote, after saving his arm, that many
■soldiers owed their limbs to the fact that
there never are enough doctors to go round
sifter a big action.
* * * *
There’s an airman in Sydney who has a
■shapely enough foot with only three toes. If
Tie hadn’t been a quick thinker and a hard
doer he would either be one-legged or dead
in France. While arguing with a flying
Hun, one of Fritz’s “tracer” bullets ripped
through his foot and the phosphorus started
to burn it. He knew he couldn’t last long
and turned his machine for home, the Hun
pursuing. Arrived safely over his own lines,
he was just about to land, when he remem-
bered that it was 25 miles to the nearest
hospital. That would mean time,, and the
poison was eating into his foot like a kennel
of tigers. Pulling himself together, he put
the machine up again and flew on, landing at
the hospital door within a quarter of an hour.
Inside five minutes he was on the operating
table, where a neat job was done. It was
one of the same squadron who had his left
arm smashed by a bullet when up 14.000 ft.
Cutting out the luxury of a faint, he experi-
mented and found that the sinews could still
work the fingers. So with his right hand
he lifted the limp arm to the controls,
crooked the fingers round them, swung his
right hand back to its work and came down
safely.
“The Lamb”: Perhaps The Bulletin re-
members the masterly way Billy Hughes
scotched the Hun chemical manufacturers
when he allowed a local firm, working under
license, to perpetuate the trade name of
“Aspirin” so that Fritz could find it all
warm and comfy after the war. The firm,
to give it its due, cut out the name on its
own account, though it meant a big fight to
familiarise the public with the new monni-
ker. But what’s the use? Fully 60 per cent,
of the doctors go on ordering aspirin, and in
the military camps, where, if anywhere, you
would think the Hun taboo, the old name
holds and the drug is ordered under its
Fritzer name scores of times every day. It
is not a lone example either. A couple of
years ago the several Australian State
Pharmacy Boards went to the expense of
sending Sawbones a printed list of German
“patent” drugs with their proper chemical
tabs attached, so that he could order his
dope without asking for Hun proprietaries.
But he’s been too lazy to memorise them.
A “Mongolia will” {ids been before S. Aus.
Supreme Court; and there are likely to be
others. London makes a careful copy of any
will found in a dead soldier’s pay-book, and
sends original and copy to Australia by
different mails. In this case the copy came
safely to hand, but the will itself is sup-
posed to have been lost with the Mongolia.
The trouble is that the hasty Billjim docu-
ment was not witnessed, and so needed a
strong affidavit by someone who knows the
handwriting ; and on a mere copy, however
complete, that, of course, cannot be done.
In this instance the boy’s mother was the
sole legatee, and in case of intestacy his
father would take everything. Their lawyer
admitted that they didn’t mind which way
it was, so the Court let it go as a case of
intestacy. The instance of a lost will which
leaves everything to a girl, or otherwise, but
away from the next-of-kin, and which is
morally though not legally certain to be the
genuine last wish of the soldier, has yet to
be faced.
# * * *
Trof. Meredith Atkinson was lecturing to
a Melbourne highbrow audience, including
some of the Very Nicest, on “The Social
Evil,” and had just stated that ladies who
took to the primrose path did not, as a rule,
last more than six or seven years on it, when
a well-dressed lady in the audience rose and
begged leave to set her personal experience
against the Professor’s dictum. Amidst the
gasps of the social students she calmly
observed that she belonged to the oldest pro-
fession on earth, that she had been following
it, not for six or seven, but for 20 years, and
that she did not feel in the least like quit-
ting. When the lecturer got his breath back
he mildly remarked that he had been speak-
ing in averages, and rushed on before there
could be any more diversions.

“Copabella”; After the war the Y.M.C.A,
will have a small army of “secretaries" look-
ing for billets, and a tremendous .organisa-
tion liable to go rusty. So it is casting round
for fresh fields to conquer. Last week in
Yarraville (Vic.) it put in its first two saps,
one each at the great works of the Cuming-
Smith and Mount Lyell fertiliser companies,
in the form of “huts.” It hopes to place
one of these in every great labor-employing
factory in Australia. The hut is to be some-
thing between a cheap dining-room and a
mutual-improvement society. The em-
ployer finds the site, equipment and as much
cash-subsidy as the spirit moves in him, the
head-centre of the Y.M.C.A. finds the cook-
missionary and his salary, and the employees
the committee of management and any lux-
uries they fancy. The great heart of the
worker is reached on co-operative lines, the
ideal aimed at being a daily lunch at Is. lid.
per week, with a menu that runs: Soup, Id.;
pie or pastry, 3d.; tea, 3d. a week. But the
rate depends on the amount of support
given. Any profit on the commissariat goes
THE RULING PASSION.
SPECTATOR ( describing accident) : “It fell over that cliff up there. There ain't a scratch
on the car, hut the people were injured something frightful.”
ENTHUSIASTIC MOTORIST: “ Wonderful, by Jove! What’s the make of the car?"
to the equipment and luxury fund. The
missionary, in addition to making his soup
and keeping his pies hot, will be a sort of
stipendiary big-brother to the employee; and
when industrial troubles arise it will
be the missionaries’ duty to stroke
down the ruffled plumage of the raging
union secretary, and to beard the roar-
ing boss-lion with the other side of
the story. In Yarraville, after a first
sniff of suspicion, the thing has taken
on well, and the missionaries’ washing-up
is running to some 300 pieces of crockery
each mid-day. Libraries, sport-teams, and
game tournaments have been started, and
gym. classes, social evenings and sundries
are in the air. The firms have played up
handsomely to the scheme, donating fine
roomy “huts,” a piano each, books and
papers, and cheques that run into three fig-
ures.

“Aghala” : The most brutal wound I have
seen inflicted on a soldier was at the Land-
ing—not in Gallipoli, but at Port Melbourne
Pier. A boyish Billjim, beside himself with
delight at his home-coining, had just hopped
into a waiting car, and was smiling back at
the cheering crowd, when a blatant oaf with
whose acquaintance he was cursed blurted:
“I s’pose you heard your father was dead?”
Jack hadn’t —and he spent the rest of the
drive with his head in his hands, the sun-
shine all gone.

Judge Gordon lately told an astonished
“criminal” in S. Aus. that he was “one of
the problems that baffle all students of pen-
ology.” Now 33, he started at Maitland
(N.S.W.) by getting 12 months, at the age
of 14, for indecent assault. Most of the
States have bundled him into gaol for in-
decency of some kind. South Aus. gave him
two sentences of a year last July. They ran
together, and he was out at the end of April,
only to be arrested again two days later, on
the old charge of lewdness. The Judge, who
hates the lash, found that he was bound to
order it, the law having been altered to meet
the recent outbreak of offences against
women and children in S. Aus. —an outbreak,
by the way, which followed the abolition of
recognised prostitution in that State. But
the very human old Judge was very sa,d
about it: —•
This being your second offence in this State I
have no option under the Aot but to order a whip-
ping.... In view of the fact, however, that in my
opinion nothing will deter you, and that I have no
hope that to inflict the lash would -be of any service
in this instance, either as a punishment or a deter,
rent, I propose to order you a very light whipping,
but you will serve the maximum term of imprison-
ment. It appears to me that the only hope of your
reformation is that you be kept in custody and under
discipline.
Four years and 10 lashes, accordingly. But
was jt a case even for gaol? Could this
unfortunate wretch help doing the things
he has done any more than Foch, say, can
help being a strategist? Medical, and per-
haps surgical, science ought to have a word
to say on the question,
BEFORE THE STORM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The night was quiet.” —War repmt.
Behind the lines men wrought with fevered haste;
A thousand fears were crowded in the gloom.
No moment dare the waiting armies waste,
While nations trembled on the edge of doom.
Wheel up the ready death to feed the guns 1
Stand to your post lest murder should awake!
A chill of horror through the midnight runs—
So soon again these solid hills shall shake
And the loud lightning break the lightning’s shield,
Testing again God’s judgment in the field.
A quiet night! There shall be quiet nights
For the sound sleepers under growing grass.
New summers wearing all their starry lights
In other days shall softly bloom and pass.
Where' the guns wait with death, young feet shall
play;
And where men march to die shall harvest come
Hiding with bread the shames of war away. •
Laugiiter shall live when all the guns are dumb...
Strain up the flagging courage for the fight;
Earth shall have victory and a quiet night!
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.

The persuasive Acting-Prime Minister
Watt has made everything clear, to every-
body’s relief, and now nobody need worry
any more. Hughes’s Federal police force
isn’t a police force at all, though what it is
doesn’t yet appear. It has duties to perform,
and they are performed regularly, even fre-
quently. It really existed before the egg
tvas thrown at Warwick, so the suspicion that
it is an omelette is unfounded. Where it
existed will be more fully known by-and-bye.
The fact that it emerged from obscurity just
after the breaking of the egg was a mere
coincidence. The force doesn’t make a prac-
tice of coinciding, but it did then. If any-
body’s mind will be eased thereby it won’t
coincide again. It was at first mostly sta-
tioned in Queensland for reasons quite un-
connected with the egg, and, besides, it may
only have appeared to be mostly stationed
there while in reality it was mostly some-
where else. Any aspect of unemployment
or loafing on its part was mere camouflage.
Its salary is paid regularly, and there is no
blot on its character. So that matter is
settled.

“Mayne Stay” : Some 12 months ago the
Navy Department decided that it was neces-
sary to have a guard on oversea and inter-
state ships. This business is controlled by
the navy, but apparently there are not enough
flatfeet to go round ; wherefore in Melbourne
the guard has been composed of home-service
swaddies in khaki. Now these chaps are to be
converted into pukka sailor-men by the simple
process of putting them into baggy pants and
jumpers, at a cost of a thousand or two of
good War Loan. W’hat difference it makes
whether the man who leans on a bayonetted
rifle at the foot, of the gangway is in blue or
drab I can’t see; but I do grudge my share
of the cost of the new clothes.
* * * *
“Fuse-Setter” : Reading in the cables that
14 U-boats have been bagged in a week by
destroyers sends my mind back to the Eng-
lish destroyer I saw under repair in a
Northern port. The stern had been snapped
off by the depth bomb she had been drop-
ping for a submarine hog. These seaquakes
are so powerful thaT the dropper has to set
out for the horizon at top speed directly the
present for Fritz splashes overboard. The
bob-tailed cripple I saw had lingered a few
seconds too long. If surface craft catch it
like that the submerged U-boat must flatten
out beautifully.
* * * *
“H.H.” : “Delilah is always on the side of
the enemy,” said a Sydney feminine orator
to the Australian League of Honor, and she
also was mean enough to call the subject of
her discourse a “temptress.” Surely Delilah,
who was a good Philistine girl and a hair-
cutter more than two millenni-
ums ago, deserves better treat-
ment. She was married by com-
pulsion to an impossible, hulk-
ing, hostile Israelite comedian,
who used to tear up lions
and carry away gates and pro-
pound conundrums. It is speci-
fically stated that he dragged
her into matrimony because he
thought he could use her in
some way for the damage of her
people. Her wedding with the
Hun was by no means volun-
tary, like Queen Victoria’s. The
one-sided Hebrew historian
always denounced the woman
who wouldn’t turn dog on her
race for his benefit, while such
as Rahab of Jericho, who was
a Flossie and did turn dog,'was
called blessed. Rahab was told
to mark her window with a red
sign so that the Hebrew might
know that she was the right
Flossie and spare her when her
townsfolk were slaughtered;
wherefore the Flossie in many
places, notably some parts of
Maoriland, puts a red blind on
her window to this day. Delilah
stood to her country, like Joan
of Arc or the Maid of Saragossa or
George Washington. She was certainly
pretty, as every girl ought to be, and she
could probably spell big words a great deal
better than her husband.

“The Snake”: The hangman’s business is
not so new. J. F. Archibald didn’t discover
it; nor Jack Abbott. I go back a bit to a
story that the old lady was never tired of
reciting, with her clay pipe in her mouth,
down south in Maoriland :
Aweel, Potts was hangit at the Cross o’ Paisley
on the seventeenth day o’ August, 1797, and the
gibbit, forbye, projectid from the corner o’ the
steeple in sich a way, laddie, that it could be seen
frae High-street an’ the Cross. An’ weel I know
it that the buddy hangit half-mast fr’m half-past
three till half-past foor. An’ in the evening the
magistrates assembled i’ the inn-aye, the Saracen’s
Heed Inn —the where they had their parrudge an’
fixings, the whuch cost, forby, £l5 3s. Id. for their-
seves an’ the hangman an’ his scullion.
I have the hangman’s cup—it is a small,
spirit globe with two silver handles.

“Country Ped”; Six or seven years ago
a nice little country school situated at
Byrneville, on the Wimmera Plains (Vic.),
housed about 20 scholars, most of them of
German descent. The German parents of
these children, disliking the secular educa-
tion given in the State School (so they
said), erected a Lutheran school close by,
and appointed a teacher (pure German).
Then the German youngsters left the
State school, which the Education De-
partment promptly closed. After that
the five or six little Australians had to
journey six miles and more to other
State schools. When war broke out.
the Minister of Education
took steps to —no, no! Not
to close it—“to eradicate the
German element in Lutheran
schools.” He forbade the use
of German textbooks and the
use of the German language;
and he ordered the district
inspectors to report on them
four times a year. Whether
the regulations are obeyed at
all times no one knows —-ex-
cept the Germans. But
this I know: the Byrne-
ville State school is
still unoccupied. Its
windows are broken;
the once fine garden is
a bed of weeds; and
the pole on which our
flag flew every Monday
morning while the kid-
dies saluted it has rotted
and fallen to the
ground. But the Lu-
theran school flour-
ishes, and the little
Australians who want
an Australian school are obliged by their
Australian rulers to travel six miles or more
a day.
* * * *
“0.L.”; The dispute between doctors and
lodges in Victoria has led to an under-the-
rose resuscitation of the old lump-sum sys-
tem, so freely practised in the years before
the B.M.A. began to be run on strict trade-
union principles. Dr. Balls-Headley, who
died lately in ’Frisco, was one of the most
consistent employers of the scheme. He had
a considerable practice among the Chosen,
by whom the idea of a single yearly pay-
ment for the physicking of the whole family
was greatly approved. Nowadays, with
lodge patients and doctors both doubtful as
to the financial outcome of the dispute over
fees, numbers of prudent practitioners are
making quiet arrangements with prudent
housefathers for the payment of a single,
inclusive fee for the year’s treatment. The
B.M.A. knows something about this, of
course; but as such contracts are mostly
undertaken by the older doctors who have
been pulled out on strike by the younger
men, the B.M.A. is watching the proceedings
with a detached air.

“T. 0.1.”: In the cemetery of St. Mathew,
Windsor (N.S.W.), is a gravestone whose
inscription runs:—
This stone is raised, by his comrades, to the
memory of Private Dunn, who was kicked out of this
life by the Colonel’s horse, as a mark of respect.
.Here lies Private Dunn,
No more alive and quick;
He died—and so would any one
That had so hard a kick.
Side by side lie a captain and a private,
buried in Jhne, 1823. Orders were strict
in those days. Runaways were numerous, and
.anyone hovering around after dark and not
giving the countersign or password when
challenged, was shot. A very nervous sentry
was on duty, and Captain Charles went out,
“for a lark,” to frighten him. The sentry
fired on getting no answer to his challenge
and shot the officer dead. When he dis-
covered what lie had done he went up to his
room and hanged himself. They were buried
next day, with full military honors, side by
side.

The reflections of the Office Deacon:—
I didn’t realise what a terrible war it was till I
saw the Archbishops’ committee recommending the
abolition of pew rents. For ages it has been the
privilege of those who could pay the best prices
to secure the best seats; and the idea of throwing
rich and poor together in one unseemly scramble
to hear the Word is nothing short of revolutionary.
And where is it going to stop ? For all rents are in
a way pew rents. The tremendous religious offices
of sun and air, rainfall and harvest, are intended to
carry a gospel to the souls of men of which the ser-
vices in a church are only the echo. The great
principle involved in letting every man and woman
and little child get near to the priestly ministra-
tions of the sun, and hear the choir of nature sing-
ing bounteous hymns, must follow as a natural corol-
lary on the abolition of reserved seats for the preach-
ing of grace. And as the ecclesiastic has lagged far
behind all other reformers, the fact that his mind is
\yorking is proof that the mind of the nation is
working also. England has always, been a country of
sui prises; and revolution there is likely to be that
moves in spirit before it takes shape in law. Long
ago a Norman Conquest changed into Merry England.
It was the result of continental wars. The Norman
was drawn to his English followers by their united
WAYS AND MEANS.
“Your dad objects to me because he says I
have no prospects.”
“Well, why don't you enlist, and then there
will always be your pension!”
service in battle; and on their return home those fol-
lowers had a new view of their leader and the leader-
had a new pride in his men. To-day, with Conscrip-
tion in force, there is a great levelling in the battle-
area. Aristocrats march in the ranks; “common:
people” hold authority. The calamity that tears;
bodies throws them very close together. England is;
feeling it. The English archbishops have felt it.
The world is going to feel it.
THE LITTLE SON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To-day the men came home.
Along the streets the stirring message ran.
Flags of the nations swirled,
And in the van
Of citizens I met a man.
Two-foot —an inch or so to spare.
Under the crowd, nigh trampled in its haste,
We elbowed each aside, and thereby faced.
“Oh, sir,” he said, his breathing coming fast:
“Give me a li/t; my dad is marching past.”
N.S.W.
JOHN BARK.

CALIFORNIA SYRUP OF FIGS.
Harmless fruit laxative cleanses
stomach, liver and bowels
without griping or
discomfort.
An acid stomach, clogged with fermenting,
ill-digested food, means
doing its duty. Nature provided ripe fruit
juices to keep the liver up to its work.
California Syrup of Figs—“Califig”—is a
deliciously refreshing fruit laxative which is
always in season. You can keep it at hand,
and it will be an unfailing friend whenever
the tongue is coated, bowels sluggish, breath
foul, throat dry or sore, stomach full of
wind, and head hot and aching. You can
take a dose whenever it is needed, as safely

BARNIS
LINSEEN
OIL
John Forrest, is in such poor health that
the House of Reps, has probably seen the
last of him. He is the only member of the
first Australian Ministry left in politics:
Barton is a High Court Judge; Kingston,
Lyne, O’Connor and Turner are dead;
Deakin, Drake and Fysh retired.
TUNING HIM UP.
William McMeekin, a Warrnambool native,
who died last week, did most useful public
service in helping to Victoria’s
pioneer butter-factories and in founding the
export trade in fat. His declining years
were largely spent with a black Bible under
his arm. Better-known brother Sam
is in the regular sky-piloting business.
Petered out in Launceston, at S 2, Augus-
tus Simson, one of the best-known company
managers in the Speck. He handled the
Tasmania mine both during its prosperity
and its adversity. The Tasmanian Copper and
other cos. were also in his charge. Outside
mining he was known throughout Australia
and further afield as a keen naturalist. In
liis early days, while a wandering prospector
on the mainland and in Tassy, he assidu-
ously followed his natural bent, and for-
warded many hitherto unrecorded species to
Europe. In addition he amassed a very
large and valuable private collection. His
butterflies were purchased by Walter Roths-
child for the Tring Museum a few years
since, and his collection of beetles was*
secured by the Adelaide Museum this year.
In addition to being an entomologist he was
a sound botanist and conchologist, and knew
more than most men about stamps’, coins
and music.
Alexander Willoughby Britton, the new
secretary of N. S. Wales Department of
Navigation, comes of well-known journalis-
tic stock. His grandfather founded the Mt.
Alexander Mail (Vie,) in the goldfields
days, and both his father and uncle (Alex,
and Henry Britton) were well-known figures
on the Melbourne press. Later, Alex. Britton
crossed to Sydney to take up, editorial work
on S. M. Herald, and was subsequently
appointed Government Historian of N. S.
Wales. The late Howard Willoughby, editor
of Melbourne Argus, was another uncle.
The new secretary’s only known vice is
chess.

A foregathering in a graveyard was held
on the 19th to celebrate the anniversary of
Batman’s arrival in Port Phillip. It seems
a cheerless way of celebrating anything, but
there was method in this mournfulness. Most
of those who attended at the Old Cemetery
were interested in blocking the encroach-
ments of the Victoria Market on the mouldy
bones of ancient Melbourne. That ready
and willing platformist, Isaac Selby, was the
chief mouthpiece of. the assemblage, and had
something interesting to say of the “hundred
representative men” who lie buried in the
historic plot.
Louis Francis Heydon, N. S. Wales
M.L.C., who died recently, was a son of
Jabez Iv. Heydon, and was born in 1848 in
King-street, Sydney. The A.B.C. Chambers
now occupy the site. There the father, J. K.
Heydon, sold in bulk Holloway’s Pills and
Ointment, for which he was sole agent in
the Southern Hemisphere. Louis, like his
brother the Judge, started life as a bank
clerk ; but promotion being slow and salaries
small, they took to the law, one as a solici-
tor, the other as a barrister. After practis-
ing for a while Louis entered into partner-
ship with T. M. Slattery, and the business
of Slattery and Heydon prospered. Heydon,
sen., was a man of many parts. For some
years he was proprietor of the Freeman’s
Journal. At another time lie hung out the
Lombardy Apples.
Old Harry Hay, of Collendina squattage,
is dead in his 73rd year. Harry, of Collen-
dina, and Alfred, of Boomanoomanah, who
went to his last camp two or three years
ago. were sons of the late William
(“Hoppy”) Hay. M.L.A. of N. S. Wales.
“Hoppy” had owned both runs, though Boo-
manoomanah was his headquarters, and
there in the’seventies he tallied 80,000 sheep.
“Hoppy” was one of the old sort, keeping a
rough tongue for his “hands” and a kennel
of staghounds for undesired visitors. Alfred
was a lean, solemn sheepman; but in his
prime Harry was a nuggety son of his
father, full of stoush and hobnailed speech
and death to “travellers”— gr-r-rh! The pair
generally looked to Melbourne for their
slack-season diversions.
Three or four years ago John Forrest was
rather proud of taking a bigger collar than
any rival in Australian politics, not even
excepting George Reid. One day lie went
into a Melbourne shop and asked for his
size in this wear. The shop didn’t have
them. “H’m,” said John —“what shall I
do now?” “You might try the saddler’s,”
said the playful youth behind the counter.

have gone right. Cato left Stawell as a lad

two main principles are strictly-

the pomps and vanities. ' When

he bought an immense dwelling
on the southern outskirts of
Haw-haw-thorn, surrounded by

lals. It was a rather poor spec.,
as nowadays there is a gas-
ometer to the south and a brick-
works to the north, both of them
greatly discounting the grandeur
of the Cato mansions. Still, he
has had plenty of activities to
take his mind off thosb worries
—always there has been the
extra 4d. to chase around the
corner.

guson has had his head hurt
while felling a tree in Melbourne
Domain. One of Von Mueller’s
trees, he explains, as if to justify
the destruction. Old Baron Von
Mueller was a German, but the
best of his kind, and an ardent
tree-lover, persistently offering-
good advice which, if accepted
at the time, would have had re-
sults worth millions to Aus-
tralia to-day. Somehow just at
present I remember the remark
he threw out of his old muffler
at a suburban mayor, head of a
council which was then busy tearing down
a _ fine avenue of, gums. “Useless, mein
friendt, useless! Dere vill alvays sdill be
von tree left to hank you to.”
To Sir Henry Blake, who died a few
weeks ago, was due a new rule with regard
to the appointment of Australian Governors.
In the ’eighties, when it was proposed to
send him to Queensland as Governor, there
was a roar of protest because of the man’s
notoriety as a suppressor of Irish disturb-
ances during “Buckshot” Foster’s rule in
Dublin. In his perplexity the then Premier
of Bananaland appealed to other Premiers
of Australia. Kingston, of S. Aus., took up
the matter and fought it with such fervor
and ability that the British Government
agreed to submit the names of its future
nominees before appointing them. Inciden-
tally, Blake decided to stay at home.
Gone at 82, John Frauenfelder, orchard-
ist, of Lavington, near Albury, and one of
the pioneers of the N.S.W. wine industry.
As he used to say, he had the happiness to
dig the first hole for the first vine planted
in the district—that was in 1851. He came
to Australia in ’49, and never left it. In
1860 he started the now famous Murray
Valley^vineyard, and two years later had the
first 25 acres of vines planted. Of late years
he gave a lot of attention to the prune busi-
ness, which he greatly helped on to its feet.
T. .1. Lindsay, killed in the Hun’s big
push in France, was a grandson of that J.
W, Lindsay who was chaplain at Norfolk
Island and Port Arthur in the bad old days.
Yeung Lindsay won the gold medal of Syd-
ney Chamber of Commerce at his last
school exam, in 1905, and went into the
engineering branch of the Railways. In his
spare time he climbed to a militia captaincy.
When the real thing arrived he went to
New Guinea as a private, returned in six
months as sergeant, re-enlisted, and got to
Gallipoli in time for the closing acts. Inva-
lided home with enteric, he put in some
time as instructor in Liverpool (N.S.W.)
camp, and then went through Duntroon Mili-
tary College and caught the boat as second
lieutenant for France, where he picked up a
second stay and a mention in despatches.
CAPTAIN PIN LAY RON, Censor,
Melbourne.
Now that Adela Pankhurst-Walsh is
otherwise occupied, the Vic. Socialist party
has given its organising job to Harry Scott-
Bennett, the hero of a thousand tongue-
fights.
At Windsor (N.S.W'.) recently died Mrs.
Ann Ouvrier, aged 93. Her husband, a
Frenchman, had, so tradition says, a market-
garden where now is Anthony Hordern and
Sons’ universal providery.
When Sergeant Cornell was up for re-
election to the Westralian Leg. Council the
other day his wife took his campaign iu
hand. The result couldn’t have been more
satisfactory if he had done the job himself.
Gone west to join his brother Clarence,
who was also a captain and M.C., • Neville
Wallach, of Bondi (N.S.W.), killed in
France on May 1. Neville was one of the
seven athletic sons of Henry Wallach, whp
were all educated at Sydney Grammar. Six
of them got into khaki, and two are still at
the Frbnt.
It seems to be an Ulsterman’s pup that
leads the Parnell family. Charles Stewart,
the Irish leader, went over in circumstances
that were tragic enough. Of three sisters
Fanny, the poetess, died young at Boston
(U.S.). Anna was drowned in a pond in
England. Now the cable tells that Mrs.
Ricketts, the last of them, has died after be-
ing a week in South Dublin Workhouse,
where her identity was unsuspected till
some papers were found after her death. She
was probably out of gear mentally, because
she had plenty to live on in comfort.
Many Melbourne people lost the keystone
of their faith when the almost-famous Mrs.
Rising passed out last week. The quaint
old clairvoyante amassed a motor-car, a
mansion and a considerable bag of money
out of the credulity of thousands who sought
her aid. In sickness all that was necessary
to do was to send Jessie a lock of hair or a
bootlace belonging to the patient, and she
would soon be gazing trancefully into his
distant inside. Jessie is alleged to have
defined and cured diseases that had baffled
plain Science, but she hadn’t enough know-
ledge to save herself. The power of self-
diagnosis is not given to mediums, apparently.
She was Scotch, and her clients included the
late Linlithgow, then Ilopetoun, Governor
or Victoria.
GENIUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now most of us be well content to keep the beaten
highway
Anti fear to leave the dusty road or tempt the dewy
hyway;
We never burst the guarding hedge, although we be
not craven,
But. dearly do we love the track, the lighted inn and
haven.
But sometime comes a youthful soul whose eyes are
full of rapture—
He hears beyond the hedge the songs that thrill and
fill and capture;
He leaps the ditch and dashes back the brambles
that would blind him,
And lo! another world appears—the road is all
behind him i
He presses on and on and on; the bog-lands draw
him under
And many a time the storms dash down to daunt
him with their thunder;
The hills that seemed so fair afar are rough with
barren boulders,
And rain and frost and hail and snow will lash his
aching shoulders.
Yet, when the day is on the wane, if courage do
not fail him,
From peaks of gold what views unfold, what songs
of welcome hail him !
He hears the sea in myriad caves her countless
warriors wheeling,
While down the hills the pipes of Heav’n roll forth
their organ pealing.
And newer worlds at night are his, to pay him for
his daring,
And naught shall e’er entice him back from yonder
lofty faring;
What heeds he now the tearing thorns, the lash of
storms untender,
When he hath won the Mountain Gate that opens on
the splendor!
But we, we others, keep the road, we know of noth-
ing surer;
And, though we miss the Glory Peaks, our supper's
much securer;
We do not grudge our friend the crown he won by
tears and toiling,
Content to know we’ve gained an inn that keeps its
pots a-boiling.
QUEENSLAND.
P. ATREY.

The Sun Newspaper, Ltd. (Sydney),
which bought the assets of the Australian

or £3132 less than they were then alleged to
be worth :

Considering all things the Sun did well to
make over £15,000 in the past year. Besides
the strike, which cost the co. over £3OOO, it
has had to pay a war price for paper ; the pre-
sent quotation is 400 to 450 p.c. above normal
—or what was normal before the, war. In
addition it had war-time trouble in connec-
tion with improvements in its Sunday edi-
tion—troubles which apparently mean that
it is unable to make profitable use of expen-
sive additions to plant. Fortunately the
war found the Sun on the up-grade and
with a new organisation capable of supply-
ing an excellent cable service in which no
other Sydney paper shared. Had the world
drowsed along in the old rut this new ser-
vice might not have yielded much profit, in
view of the fact that comparatively few
papers shared the expense; but it meant a
lot when the centre of Australia’s news-
interest shifted to the other side of the
equator. This gave the Sun a boom in cir-
culation. Then came lavish expenditure of
loan money in Sydney and the mobilisation
of much of Australia’s foreign trade at
the same port, partly because of the short-
age of shipping and partly because of the
development of trade with America and
Japan. These things made Sydney out-
wardly more prosperous than it had ever
been before; and the Sun, by reason of its
boom in circulation, was able to command
advertising at increasing rates. So you
get an explanation of a profit of £15,000 in
spite of the tremendously increased cost of
production before mentioned.
Twelve months ago it looked as though
the co. was on thin ice so far as indispen-
sable supplies are concerned. Stocks (at
cost) were down to £27,910. Since then the
co. has had the luck to build up to £89,550 ;
and, the directors say, “contracts have been
made for supplies of printing paper suffici-
ent to enable us to carry on to the end of
1919.” But the stocks in hand are far more
important than contracts entered into. Con-
tracts are easily enough made; the trouble
with a country which has practically no
shipping of its own and no printing-paper
mills is to get them fulfilled in a hurry. The
increase of the stocks has, of course, affected
the financial position :
1911. 1914. 1916. 1917. 1918.
Capital . .£24,950 £83,598 £122,050 £125,463 £145,837
Liabilities 44,334 26,140 27,490 19,441 63,787
Freehold.. 21,277 41,650 62,870 62,870 61,370
Plant .. 36,238 60,118 61,552 69,350 78,084

Book debts 6,825 19,435 14,110 25,075 27,303
Cash, etc... 1,056 2,670 5,962 2,233 1,444
Liabilities are thus £44,340 greater although
over £20,000 of new capital has been issued.
But as stocks are nearly £62.000 heavier,
and consist chiefly of one of the most sale-
able commodities in the world, there is no
need for anyone to stay awake at night
thinking about the overdraft.
The reserves are shown above as £10,503.
The table, however, isn’t quite satisfactory:
no table can be when the co. won’t stick to
one form of balance-sheet. Some years ago
the directors used to exhibit the amount writ-
ten off for depreciation. They don’t now.
But, in addition to the reserves shown above,
they have a plant and machinery reserve,
which has moved on from £22,017 to
£27,592 during the year. There is no good-
will item among the assets. Assuming good
management, this puts the co. on an excel-
lent wicket for scoring a high rate of divi-
dends.
The directors have lately acquired, “at a
satisfactory figure the assets and under-
taking of the Newcastle Newspaper Co. A
new co. has already been formed, in con-
junction with the shareholders of the old
co. at Newcastle, for the purpose of publish-
ing the Newcastle Sun and in which this co.
holds a controlling interest.” Newcastle’s
big manufacturing future is as well assured
as that of any town can be in a country
which still suffers from suppressed Freetrade
mania; and apart from the new paper’s
dividend prospects it will suit the Sun very
well to have more non-competitors who will
share the cost of its expensive cable service.

1 his is another remarkable illustration of
the advantages of co-operation. The co. was
promoted by the N. S. AVales Chamber of
Manufactures; and as the members send in
their business without canvass, the costs are
astonishingly low. Look at these figures:—

So for the four years the expense rate has
been 14.1 p.c. of the premiuins and the loss
rate 26.3 p.c.; together 40.4 p.c. This, after
providing a satisfactory reserve for unex-
pired- risks and for contingencies, has allowed
of the return to policyholders of 36.2 p.c. of
their premiums. The shareholders mean-
time have had a dividend of 10 p.c. p.a.; but
that as well as a bite for mother (for the
past year the Chamber gets a grant of £200)
has come out of interest earnings. Last
year the _ new Workmen’s Compensation
Act came into operation in N. -S. Wales:—
This Act increases benefits to injured workers cer-
tainly by 33J p.c., and probably bv 50 p.c., over
and above the benefits of the 1910 "Act. Although
these benefits have addpd greater liabilities to
workmen s compensation policies, insurance cos. have
generally reduced the rates for this class of insur-
ance by an average of at least 33J p.c Dur-
ing the year under review this Association took as
its basis these reduced rates.
The immediate elfec't has been to increase
the loss rate to 36.3 p.c.; but the expense
rate remains at the average of 14.1 p.c.,
allowing, after providing for contingencies,
of a return of 30 p.c, of the reduced pre-
miums.

trifle in outstanding accounts
(£111.)) and furniture (£72), everything is

getting what it deserves, too :

Thus deposits and other liabilities have in-
creased in four years from £4,439,469 to
£4,990,419. Of that £550,000 only £lll,OOO
was held liquid as at March 31; and in view
of the uncertainties of the present position
those figures would take a good deal of justi-
fying if they stood alone. There is no
doubt something to be said in extenuation.
The Colonial has a considerable pastoral and
other country connection ; and owing to the
system of wool appraisement a good deal of
the clip remains to be paid for on March 31.
This probably explains why the advances
which on September 30 were £2,975,807
moved up to £3,369.539 in the next six
months. And the wheat harvest has also
had to be financed. The advances are there-
fore probably much more liquid than
advances generally are.
Even on the figures as they stood at the
end of the half-year, liquid assets were equal
to 44 p.c. of the total liabilities to the
public; and in ordinary circumstances that
would be reckoned something more than a
fair proportion. Capital is £439,280; add
visible reserves, and you have a total of
£748,741, which is equal to £1 for every
£(> 13s. of the public’s money in the business.
There is nothing unsafe about that either.
All the same, with this amount of the pub-
lic’s money at risk, a shade over 7 p.c. isn’t
very much profit to make on the shareholders’
capital and reserves combined. There has
been a lot of talk in England lately—-and
there is more than usual just at present—
about bank amalgamation; but, however it
may be there, we could do with some more
of it in Australia. There is much overlap-
ping—more of it than there seems to be upon
merely comparing the lists, of branches of the
various banks, inasmuch as one bank estab-
lishes a branch at Tabletop not because there
is enough business for a bank there, but
because it hopes to get a bit of business
from Pegleg, where another bank has a branch
which has been serving Tabletop as well.
This country isn’t so much over-banked as
it is over-branched; and if amalgamation is
too hard a thing to manage it would be better
for shareholders and customers, too, if Boards
could parcel out the country. We don’t do
the producer a good service by putting one
bank at his back door and another at the
front, although as a rule he thinks we do.
Somebody has to pay for it all; and it isn’t
the man in Collins-street.

however great the co.’s merits, they don’t
seem likely to favor it to anything like the
same extent in the near future.

There are pubs and pubs—pubs that cater
for the comfort of their customers, and pubs
that cater for their discomfort. The latter
make most money. The Grand is one of
Melbourne’s first-class hotels, and its history,
partly shown in the table, is anything but
cheerful. Even in the good times the co.
was unable to adjust its tariff to a scale that
would give a decent return for the service
provided. Under the best of conditions the
residential hotel in Australia is an uncer-
tain business which requires the most care-
iui management. But in troublous times like
the present, with the tourist traffic practi-
cally dead, liquor trading restricted and pro-
vision prices sky-high, the big caravanserais
are having a particularly rough passage.
The Grand showed a loss of over £4OO dur-
ing the three years following the outbreak
of war; but the position was really worse
than that, for apparently no provision was
made for depreciation. Last year’s recovery
was partly due to increased rents from the
self-contained flats into which part of the
hotel has been converted, and also to “the
personal interest shareholders are taking in
recommending the hotel to their friends.” Of
the profit of £3282, however, £l5OO has gone
into a depreciation fund, which now stands
at £3OOO and is included in the reserves in
the table. Capital is £39,319. The book
value of the hotel (exclusive of furniture,
etc.) is £04,025, but there is a mortgage of
£02,000, which calls / for £2600 of interest
per annum. The co. owes £1419 and there
are liquid assets totalling almost £5OOO.

And since the term closed another eighteen-
pence has been declared payable on June
21. But this isn’t anything to lose your head
over. It is a lot better than the promise of
a few years ago; but there is not very much
ahead, unless the Pinnacles mine, lately
bought at a price which the directors don’t
disclose in their report, turns up trumps.
The Junction North itself has, it is esti-
mated, 192,000 tons in reserve; and there are
also on hand £40,399 (cost price) of pur-
chased slimes. Last half-year 18,872 tons of
ore and apparently £16,783 worth of the pur-
chased slimes were dealt with. That is to
say, the purchased slimes will last a little
over a year at the rate they are being used
up ; and the mill, if it worked full time, could
easily eat out the mine in two years. Nor is
there much chance of finding more of any
consequence in the mine; and prospects of
buying more slimes are almost as poor.
Junction North management must be given
credit for plunging in slimes when it did.
It happened—principally through its former
metallurgist, Owen—to know more about
slimes than most of them. But it doesn’t
know any more now: others, in fact, have
gone further than the Juncy North. If the
war and the boom in metals hadn’t hap-
pened, though, all the profit that this co.
would have made out of its purchases would
have been harmless. What it will make out
of the balance is quite unknown. In fact,
what it will make out of what it has treated
during the last half-year is only a guess,
because of 10,435 tons of zinc concentrate
produced orfily 3503 tons were despatched for
shipment. There is a guaranteed post-war
market for the vast quantity of zincs which
this and other Broken Hill cos. are accumu-
lating; but there’s never a word said about
price. In addition to the profit which can be
made out of the dumps and mine during the
next couple of years or so, and any surpluses
it may collect on stuff already produced, the
co. has £130,307 net of liquid assets, in addi-
tion to £40,592 of stores. There are 180,000
shares.
The Pinnacles are one of the most con-
spicuous features of the Barrier silver-field,
and 30-odd years ago there were people who
would have backed the Pinnacles mine
against anything under the Broken Hill hump.
A Melbourne co. was so sure about it that it
put up a smelter and refinery—one of the
first on the Barrier. But they didn’t know as
much about the metallurgy of lead ores then
as they do now: and the property was a sink
for money for 15 years. Then, the dog hav-
ing been given a thoroughly bad name, it was
abandoned—which was probably a quite un-
deserved fate. The ore-bodies are much more
irregular than in the main section of the
Broken Hill lode; but there are very big
bunches of ore, and it is quite likely that
money will be made out of them. They are
about 10 miles from Broken Hill, and there
are no engineering difficulties whatever in
the way of a tramline if mine developments
warrant it. The Junction North will issue
shares to pay for the lease and
develop it.
For weeks past the market has been
guessing- what was going to happen with
Broken Hill Prop, shares. Something, it
quite made up its mind; and there has been
big buying. The secret was let out on Tues-
day. There are at present 1,500,000 shares
of 8s. each, of which 1,181,006 are issued;
authorised capital is thus £600,000 and sub-
scribed ditto £472,402. Under the re-organi-
sation the capital will be £3,000,000, made
up this way :—•
Present 1,181,006 shares of 8s. .. ..£472,402
318,994 new 8s. shares (premium 32s.) .. 127,598
Bonus of 12s. per share on 1,500,000 shares 900,000
Bonus of 600,000 new £1 shares .. .. 600,000
Shares held in reserve .. .. .. 900,000
Total .. £3,000,000
This means (1) the right to buy at 40s. one
new share for every four old ones (or there-
abouts). The shares made up of fractional
interests will be sold and the proceeds dis-
tributed in cash. (2) One new free share for
every two at present held, or thereabouts.
Fractions will be dealt with as in the other
case. The net result will be that the co.,
while transferring to capital £1,500,000 of
reserves (its formal reserve funds were
£1,875,000 as at November 30 last), will
get in £637,988 of new capital, which will be
used to further extend the iron and steel
works at Newcastle. In addition , to the for-
mal reserve funds above men'tioned, on
November 30 last there was a I*, and L.
balance of £234,966. Besides the share capi-
tal the co. at the same date owed £901,700
on account of debentures. Its other liabili-
ties were £801,466; its assets apart from
plant and works and interests in works were
£1,456,987. The proposals for the recon-
stitution of the co. will be put before a
meeting of shareholders on June 10.

* * * *
At last Duke Extended, Maryborough
(Vic.), has something to talk about. The
rise off the west under level has gone into
the gutter 1500ft. ahead of the faces worked
from the main reef drive. Thence about
150oz. a fortnight is coming, with only one
or two parties of men in the wash; and, of
course, some profit is flowing in. All the
same, it would not take long to get out all
the available ground if there was nothing
up the gutter, or down it to the north-east*
But the rise off the west drive may mean
a great deal. If a fair-sized gutter is shown
there, then it is .reasonable to say that the
lead should junction with the main system
where it broke off to the north of the old
main shaft. That would give a mile of
drained wash on a gutter that for the most
part has been a consistent gold getter. An-
other problem is whether to the north-east
of the present stoping-line the reef has split
again, with the gold-bearing portion going
to the north-east. Recent work seems to
indicate that it does. So Duke Extended
has a double-barrelled chance. But even
that does not mean that it can carry a high
market capitalisation.

Only 35oz. came from the 100 tons sample
crushing from the Rising Star Extended,
Daylesford (Vic.), whereas lOOoz. was
looked for. Result: a sorrowing crowd that
had built castles on the little heap of spark-
ling specimen stone shown for days in a
big Collins-street window. Another sadly
dimmed Daylesford show trying hard to get
the clouds to roll by is Nuggetty Ajax. A
yarn was told lately that the flat make off
the No. 3 crosscut had opened up into a
jeweller’s shop. This filled the market with
joy for a day. Then there was the usual
disillusion.

Lode Hill (N.Q.) is sluicing intermit-
tently with two nozzles, and 12,309yds. were
washed in March for 5 tons tin oxide, worth
about £1000. Only 10 men were employed,
so the average amounted to £100 per man—•
helped of course by the large outlay on
• water-races and plant. Although sluicing is
very profitable with black tin selling at £200
per ton, shareholders can’t expect whopping
dividends on 100,000 shares from the work
of 10 men. Shares have been sold at Is. in
Melbourne, notwithstanding that the co. has
been so recently on the dividend list.
