From the sec. of the Wallaroo and Moonta
Co. —
The restoration of the so-called Taxation Reserve
to the Accumulated Profits Reserve was not intended
to imply that this co. is exempt from the War-Time
Profits taxation. The transfer was made, as it was
considered inadvisable to reserve a definite amount for
taxation when it Wi.' impossible to gauge the amount
required. As a matte, of fact, this eo. is not exempt
from the operations of the War-Time Profits taxa-
tion.
No, it isn’t. But the directors of certain
Broken Hill cos., who know what Hughes’s
War-time Profits Tax means and was in-
tended to mean as well as anybody, lately
reported that they didn’t expect to have to
pay anything.
* * * *
During the half-year ended March 31,
Mount Bischoff Extended (Tas.) crushed
5943 tons ore for 79 tons concentrates, aver-
aging 71 p.c. tin, worth £14,844, or
£2 9s. lOd. per ton of crudes. Net profit
was £4026. Although the Board had an
accumulated profit equal to more than 10s.
a share, it handed out a modest shilling
(£800), and spent no less than £6000 during
the term on enlarging the plant to 20-head,
which will enable the management to handle
a much larger tonnage and reduce costs.
This co.’s future, however, depends entirely
on the behavior of the tin market, for its
lode is narrow and the grade under 1 p.c.
Tributors have got a footing on the
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.). The surface
area, excepting that occupied by plant and
buildings, has been divided into 21 blocks
and tributes let down to a depth of 100ft.
There is enough ore broken and unbroken
below that depth to keep the co.’s plant
going for some time, and until this supply is
exhausted and a little further exploration
is done no ground will be leased below 100ft.
The mine has been on the producing list for
nearly 20 years, and in that time has yielded
many tons of gold and paid nearly 1£ mil-
lions in dividends. At one time it was
worked like a rabbit warren, and had the
distinction of being the worst opened out
and most secretively run mine at Kalgoorlie.
The ore was picked in order to keep the out-
put at a considerably higher figure than it
should have been if the show had been
worked as a mine should be; and when the
crash came and the methods of working
revealed, the property was considerably
lowered in the estimation of the public.
From the poor condition in which it was
left by the gougers the mine never recovered.
It is, however, still good enough for tribu-
tcrs, and portions should provide remunera-
tive employment for small parties «of men
for years to come.
* | * * *
The expectations of Golden Butterfly
(W.A.) again seem to have been blighted.
The first trial parcel of 55 tons from its
much-talked-of Tuekabianna show yielded
only 17oz. 17dwt.,and the second of 36 tons
produced only 7|oz.; total, 91 tons for 24oz.
It looks like another case where assay values
have not been borne out by mill results.
* * * *
William Henderson, of the Mararoa
(W.A.), so likes the Light of Asia, outside
Cue, that he urges his Board to take _ a
working option. Out of the Light of Asia,
working it to 180ft., the late Ned Lloyd
hustled 25,000 tons for £74,000 of gold.
When he passed out, the Rae crowd got
possession. In Lloyd’s day there was a hot
lawsuit over an encroachment by the Queen
of the May, which ended in Lloyd getting
hold of that show, too. So now the Mararoa
has two shots in its gun—the main Asia
shoot and the Queen of the May run of
stone. The Queen is flooded at present, but
water is an asset not to be sneezed at in
those parts. The record of the two mines is
about an ounce per ton, so that if the
Mararoa proves the shoots to live below the
well-worked levels it will have got hold of a
good claim after its four years’ hunt. At
the Mararoa itself there’s nothing cheering
nowadays.
* * * *
Commodore, Meekatharra (W.A.), having
got something very like a blue duck in the
Macquarrie lease, is trying to pick up the
Ingliston shoot from next door. Trade is
not on the Board nowadays, or he might say
where all the gold in the Macquarrie has
gone. When the Adelaide crowd has tired
itself over the Ingliston shoot he may be
called in to be a guide as of old.
* * *• *
The people running Mary Mac (W.A.)
still reckon there is something to be made out
of the show. In the beginning shareholders
were to get a lot, but they were disillusioned
long ago. Last half-year’s development was
confined to looking for gold in the bottom
workings and sinking a prospecting shaft.
The manager is unable to give any estimate
of ore in sight, owing to the disturbed state
of the lode, but he hopes to run the battery
shortly on the best of what there is. Mary
Mac may swell the Westralian gold output
for a time, but on present appearances it
won’t make money for shareholders.
* * * *
From Westonia: —
The principal mines here yell for co-operation.
Apparently the Edna May believes that with extra
pumping plant everything will be all right, but
there’s no guarantee that the frequent break-downs
of the past will not occur again. Meanwhile Deeps
is menaced not because the cementing may give way,
but because other and higher lines of less resistance
may obtrude themselves, and one small leakage plight
soon become a deluge. Then if, later on, Central
works the main lode the water there would be hard
to keep out of the other mines. So that the outlook
is almost as serious for one show as for the other.
Disaster threatens the field unless the Government
steps in and compels all the cos. affected to com-
bine and keep the water down.
* * * *
Gibb Maitland, Westralian Government
Geologist, has gone on a long holiday, ana
Torrington Blatchford has temporarily
taken his place. For a time, at any
rate, the custom of sendiug field geologists
out to make extensive surveys and write
lengthy technical reports that merely puzzle
claim-holders will cease. Instead the staff is
to be sent out into the mineral-bearing dis-
tricts to search for localities likely to contain
payable gold and base-metal deposits.

Sydney, May 27, 1918.
My Dear Arini, —
The Pilgrims who journeyed to the grave
of Henry Parkes on Saturday passed near
the little cottage at the entrance to Faul-
conbridge grounds in which for many years
his sister Polly lived. The old lady, blithe
and rosy-cheeked under the shade of a sun-
bonnet, spelit most of her time pottering
about in the little garden in front of her
crib. Live in the big house she would not.
But she had the devoted attention of the
kindest of women —Parkes’s first Lady, that
Clarinda whose little side-curls bobbed
round a benevolent face which had grown
many lines of care when the family lived in
the mountain home; for though Australia’s
most picturesque politician might manage
the affairs of State, he could newer make a
smooth job of his own. And on the shoulders
of his womehkind fell the burden of the
household’s tangled finance. Annie, the
clever daughter who was Dad’s right hand
.and secretary, went with him to England in
the height of his fame. Australian poli-
ticians hadn’t then learned tin* trick of
travelling with an “entourage.” But neither
she nor Lily, whose mother was Clarin-
da, appear to have joined the Pilgrims’ Pro-
gress on Saturday. They were not the only
notable absentees, though. Suspicious col-
leagues and warm admirers of the G.O.M.
stood aloof, being not too well satisfied with
the bona-fides of some at least of the pro-
moters or supporters of the expedition. You
see, there is much vacant land to sell at
Faulconbridge and thereabouts, and some
glorious views to show the visitor with a
pound or two in his pocket.
Poor Annie Parkes —the jolly vigorous
Annie of her big father’s day—is, alas! a
bent cripple now. Most of this year she
has spent in bed, nursing her rheumatism.
She had an office until then ; but of course
it had to be given up. They live together,
the two daughters; and each believes the
other the most wonderful creature in the
world.
Charles Bryant, a local man who has been
for years on the other side, has been added
to the lengthening list of “official artists.”
A brother of organist Alice Bryant, Charles
is still remembered here as a small boy with
waving locks, manfully struggling under a
big portfolio towards the studio of Lister
Lister. Going to London lie studied for 10
years with John Ilassall, then did more
work with marine-painter Olssen. had his
first picture in the Academy in 1911, and is
well and favorably known at the Paris Salon.
Bryant offered himself for military service,
but was rejected. Now lie’s going to paint
the other fellow.
At the beginning of the war young Was-
sell left Sydney as a captain with the First
Field Ambulance. Since then he has been
in most of the big things, including the
Landing. He came back a few weeks ago, a
lieut.-colonel with a D.S.O. and only 27
birthdays behind him, and made good use of
part of his leave by marrying Dorothy Burt,
recently of Stantliorpe (Q.). Wassell, sen.,
was well known in Brisbane’s earlier days
as Inspector of Police. I presume that if
the lieut.eolonel goes back it will be neces-
sary for him to re-enlist as a private. Any-
way, that is the wonderful Defence Depart-
ment’s new rule on the subject.
Mrs. Jane Foreman, wife of the. well-,
known surgeon, died last week. The : body
was taken to Adelaide for cremation, and the
ashes will come back to Sydney. Possessed
of considerable private means, Mrs. Fore-
man had an open purse for every charity.
In St. Stephen’s one fine evening last
week, Minnie White, fourth daughter of the
Henrv Whites of Whitehill station, Long-
reach (Q.), took a gold circlet from Lieut.
Johnstone. The ’groom’s father is the Rev.
J. C. Johnstone, of Caulfield (Vic.). The
bride, a beauteous young damsel, was richly
garnished in trappings of white satin and
silver tissue. White orchids .as a posy. The
’groom handed his Best Girl a diamond
bangle, and strewed the bridesmaids with
gems. Johnstone, lieut., is just home from
the war.
The goodbye evening to Captain “Car.,”
M.C., was another crush —it was all crush
in Empire week —and the Town Hall, which
hadn’t had a moment to itself, was ehoc-k-a-
block with goodwill to the departing soldier-
politician—w'ho, by the way, under the new
rule of the Defence Department, wouldn’t be
allowed to return unless he v r enlisted as a
private. J. C. L. Fitzpatr: x spoke for the
Government; Mayor Smith’s exuberant ora-
tory twined garlands, so to speak, around
Car.’s long neck; and music and song and
the presentation of a boomerang were in the
list.
Recruiting is being so shaken up by mar-
tial music, khaki speakers and parades of
troops that last week was one long rally, so
that we lost toll of dates and didn’t rightly
know whether Wednesday, Thursday or Fri-
day was May 24 and consecrated to flag-
fever. But the calendar was unmoved and
churned out the dates with cold precision.
Amongst the fixtures was a big welcome
home. But in the days when the Defence
Department moved always in mysterious
ways its blunders to perform, and any public
reference to the grievances of Billjim was
frowned upon, we got into the habit of fob-
bing him off with the second or third best.
And it is no use pretending that it’s any
different now. We talk a lot about the
best being not too good for Billjim. Beautiful
hypocrisy ! There's a boy who left his com-
fortable farm three years ago to go fighting
for us. He had a farmer-friend, a neighbor,
who didn’t go. The farmer-boy returned
lately, gassed and ill. After being nursed in
Sydney for a time he was sent off to the
good air of the farm with a pension of 15s.
a week. Hi; and his neighbor who had
stayed at home left the city together. The
neighbor had a sleeper. We sent the sick
boy back in an overcrowded second-class
carriage. Nothing’s too good for—whom?
There was the same sort of thing in connec-
tion with last week's welcome-home. Mum had
given the faces of the children a shine to
greet Dad in Martin-place about 10 a.m.
That meant, to eager hearts, hanging on the
kerbstone any time, after 9 o’clock. But the
long hours passed, and the crowd changed
from oue foot to another till the belated
train limped into Central Station. It
wasn’t till half-past 12 that the first lot got
its welcome from Sydney.
A note from a returned wanderer: —
Observing a clamor to provide “Car.s” Thousand
with a band, 1 rise in wrathful protest against this
wasteful exploitation of brass. Cook’s rooms at
Cairo and various base depots in France hold upwards
of SO bands contributed by generous but misguided
people in Australia. If anyone doubts inv statements
let him go and look for himself, and he will find
that band instruments hang there as thick as heads
in Bluebeard’s chamber. Give the boys caps,
mufflers, socks and Tommy-cookers till the cows come
home; also sweets and tobacco. But leave the brass
alone. If a patriotic citizen exists with time hang-
ing on his hands, let him go and salve a few of these
musical derelicts and put them aboard the troop-
ships. Some music would be very heartening there;
and it has been known to save a peck of trouble on
a dreary outgoing, too.
Australian born of
Italian parentage
and typically Italian
in a large and hand-
some way, Mrs. Fer-
rari-Passmore is in
the forefront of the
struggle to make
June 28 a day of
days. A passionate
patriot, her black
eyes flash when she
talks of Italian Red
Cross nurses. “They
get no pay—nothing !
In the war zone they
are provided with
two meals a day.
For breakfast, coffee
and brown bread.
Sugar they never
see. Then for a second meal there is dinner
—very light. When they are not at the
Front these devoted women must provide for
themselves. In Italy our people are taught
that Country is before all. So when war comes
you get the Italian Red Cross nurse and her
daily sacrifice. Then Italy must help the
refugees whom the Austrians —-whose devil-
ish cruelty to our women is not known here
—drive before them. They only take
prisoners those who will be useful, and —oh,
it is too horrible! Italy must feed and pro-
tect those of the rest who are spared to
live.” Yet this good woman’s labors are not
all for the Land of Song. Last year
she raised about £2OOO for other patri-
otic funds. Besides being on the executive
of the June 28 (Italy Day) committee, Mrs.
Ferrari-Passmore (she’s the widow of the
late H. Passmore, Inspector of Customs) is
lion, organiser of the Italian opera night, and
gives her spare hand to the Festa at the
Town Hall. The photo is from the Swiss
Studio.
The handsome Mitchell Library, which
faces Macquarie-street and turns its back—
which is only half hooked up, as it were —
to the inner Domain, represents Australians
at its most fascinating. David Scott Mitchell,
M.A.. to whose munificence we owe this
storehouse, died some years ago. He was, in
fact, too ill to be present when the com-
memoration-stone was laid by then Premier
Joe Carrutliers. The new wing (for the
Public Library, now over the road, but
later to be hitched on behind, is the body) is
really part of the romance of early Sydney.
In iS26 10 gentlemen met at the Sydney
Hotel to consider the formation of a reading
room and circulating library. The leading
spirit was Lieut, de la Condamine, A.D.C. to
the just-arrived Governor Darling. The late
D. S. Mitchell possessed the only copy, so
far as is known, of the ancient document
sotting forth the rules of the new library.
The donor of the treasure-trove, which has
been so worthily housed by the Government,
was born not far from the building which is
his memorial. Small Dave opened his eyes
in the medical-officer's quarters of the mili-
tary hospital which now forms part of Par-
liament House —where (whisper!) sensat-
tional things may happen within the next
three weeks.
Tlie Mitchell’s
chief librarian, Hugh
Wright, began as a
youth in the old Pub-
lic Library, and step
by step has climbed
to his present perch.
He was in Seville
when war broke out
—he had gone to
England to buy for
this State the Mac-
quarie MSS., and was
poking into other
possible sources of
information about
Australia before the
dawn. Away from
his precious books
Hugh Wright will
talk about roses and
aphis, clay soil, snails and kindred topics—
and hurry away to once more analyse the
Milky Way through his own telescope. He
is a' Fellow of the Royal Astronomical
Society and this year’s president of the N. S.
Wales Historical. Society; and Judith
Fletcher has made a capital likeness of him.
Clarence Prescott, who has been fastened
to a Military Cross, is a son of C. J. Pres-
cott, headmaster of Newington College.
Young Prescott was the crack athlete of the
college in his time, and represented it in
football, cricket, shooting and on the run-
ning track. He collected athletic and other
honors at Sydney Uni. later on. and was
articled to Sydney solicitor Iloldship when
the biggest game of all called him.
Clerical women war-workers, who do their
bit for our sojers when many more leisured
people are eating their lunch or ambling off
to evening bridge, held a bazaar in the city
the other afternoon. It opened itself about
8 p.m., and had an official re-opening
by the Cullen lady an hour later. An
eager crowd pushed its way to the
stalls, where miracles of stitchery and
mixed temptations were piled on the
stalls. Tall president Armstrong’s yellow
hair-bow waved above the field that had
come to be harvested, and hon. sec. Huxtable
helped in the patriotic reaping.
.The Phil, gave a good account of itself in
‘"the Creation.” A big audience of devotees
flocked to the Town Hall, and, generally
speaking, walked on its toes and clothed its
body from ear to ankle. President Chalmers
r nd his family got into their seats early, and
only an occasional squeak from a door-hinge
desecrated the bass’s thunderous "In the be-
ginning.” The voice belonged to Fred
Collier. Soprano Mary Conly, unrivalled
in oratorio, had much applause, and beamed
over a large fichu in reply. Phil Newbury,
with majestic music to warble, wasn’t in
top form. Conductor Bradley waved ener-
getic arms over the proceedings.
The Charles E. Waters’s garden fete for a
C. of E. hut fund—of course, it’s for the
benefit of all soldiers, regardless of creed —
was another staggering financial success.
About £7OO is likely to be counted up. Lady
Davidson opened the raid, and Justice Harvey
explained the length and breadth of the
scheme. One of the younger lady pirates
lurked inside a cretonne frock, well hooped
and entirely surrounded by pockets filled with
brown-paper parcels—a cute idea top-dressed
with a fascinating poke bonnet. The engage-
ment lasted two days.
The Soldiers’ Entertainment Fund gets a
nice cheque from the successful show in the
Town Hall on Empire Night. Massed
bands, bunting and enthusiasm were lead-
ing features. A neat drawing-room edition
of a dug-out was surrounded by pot plants.
Above it hung a huge sign, painted to look
like an old town in France. Watchmen
having passed the word along, the conductor
took the National Anthem at a gallop as
the sub-regal party appeared at a side door.
His Ex. and Lady Davidson were followed
by a long official tail. The Cullens, who
have returned from holidays in Maoriland
(his Honor’s fine fleece beautifully combed
up), Mrs. George Fuller and her usual good
looks'(her Chief Secretary in attendance),
the Earp family, Captain Glossop and Mrs.
George Lee were on adjacent chairs in the
forefront. Chaplain Eaton handed Mrs.
Earp a bouquet for her steady slogging at
the job of providing amusement for the camp
at Liverpool.
A grandson of explorer Gregory got into
the casualty-lists in France the other day.
He is Capt. A. Woodward Gregory, of the
Flying Corps, and one of the bright young-
sters (he’s not 21 yet) who have graduated
for their wings under Lieut. Stutt at the
State Aviation School at Richmond. His
mother is Mrs. G. E. Murdoch, wife of the
manager of Petty’s.
The Victoria League, founded nearly 10
years ago, fainted quietly away till some-
one loosened its corsets and gave it what you
call a snifter. I think, in 1915. The lady
was strong enough to be “at home” in the
Women’s Club the other evening. The
Davidsons, who are as good showmen as the
Rawsons were in their reign, turned up to
the tick, and Beulah Bolton read about the
Lady Victoria’s domestic affairs. There are
Philistines who describe the League as a
little bit of fluff, But it means well, and it
will never be prosecuted for omitting to sing
“Rule Britannia.”
Mrs. Herbert Nelson, who got home from
the U.S.A. the other day with only chick
Gwen, chats of N’York and ’Frisco fashions,
the Los Angeles earthquake (which they
missed by a few hours) and Auntie and
Uncle’s determined war-attitude. “Auntie,
says the Australian woman, “is as well-
dressed as ever, but in a quieter style. We
heard that clothes were an ‘awful price’ in
the States. Don’t believe it. I bought cos-
tumes in N’York at about half the price
asked in Sydney.” For over a year Gwen
Nelson climbed ladders, dived into space,
crossed into Mexico, and had a revolution
for breakfast, all for Fox Films. Now she’s
resting at the Australia with Mum and Dad.
A pretty Freneh-Australian and a gallant
young French soldier; love at first sight;
and an engagement ring. The demoiselle is
Fleurette, a daughter of Mrs. Iliginbotham,
head of the French Circle of Conversation,
and one who remembers as a terrifying
nightmare of her childhood the Siege of
Paris —1870-71. The fiance is Sergeant
Paul Estripeaut, with three years’ war ser-
vice and the Croix de Guerre to his credit.
He’s a tall and handsome young man.
The Rafalewski duet —the lady is soprano
Lili Sharp—have signed a 12 months’
engagement. Harry Friedman steers the
little co. through Japan, India, Java, Singa-
pore and China. A pupil of the Rafalewskis,
Eva Lea-Coates, with top notes that touch
the stars, goes with the party.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.

Powdered Coal and Engineering Com-
pany, at the works of the River
Smelting and Refining Company,
Florence, Colorado, reduced their
fuel bill from 1350 pounds of coal
per ton of charge, to 745 pounds
of coal per ton of charge with the
Powdered Coal Carburetter.
The complete control of the two
factors, fuel and air, is the funda-
mental basis of successful use of
pulverised coal. The Pruden Coal
Carburetter provides this control.

Melbourne, May 27, 1918.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Well, the Conant movie has happened.
The Auditorium was like a hill of Sassiety
ants on Thursday night. Mere rain and
sleet couldn’t keep away any who were with-
in motoring distance. ' Lady Helen had to
go to Ballarat and Governor Stanley was
helping to make Warrnambool a city, so
they couldn’t be there. The' G.-G. arrived,
however, wearing a look of extreme good-
nature and a patch of sticking-plaster on
his brow where the tree hit him. Lady
Stanley came in her gorgeous blue brocade
cloak with a little nasturtium dress under-
neath. A small Stanley child with long fair
hair fled across the balcony to sit next Sir
Ronald. Lady Doris Blackwood sat be-
tween Captains Conant and Clifford. Conant,
in civvies, nursed his shining top-hat and
looked modestly self-conscious ; Major Iverr-
Pearse sat frigidly by himself well along the
front pew, and a bevy of girls “in the play”
sat in a vivid row behind.
Mrs. Geoffrey Syme banded her hair in
silver and purple and wrapped herself in
fur and brocade. Norman Bayles and his
wife were there. Tall Norman’s figure was
well braced up. He had frills on his linen
boiled shirt and perky little frills out-lining
his boiled waistcoat. His waist looked about
18 inches and his dress clothes seemed to
have been painted on to him. Down to his
creaseless patent-leather toes he was a vision.
Norton Grimwade looked bored. The Melba
Armstrongs were there to see themselves as
others see them. In fact, half the house
was occupied by intense people on the same
mission.
As it turned out, the amateurs had made
an unintentional burlesque of their job.
The house choked with laughter as each
new star appeared. Mrs. Morris Gibbs’s
large eyes had been made up with huge
black circles, and she rolled them in the first
tragic scene with coonlike effect. Sir Henry
Parker fell into the amateur movie actor’s
worst fault of mouthing his words. Dr.
Noyes Finch gave a dentist-view of his
mouth when he laughed. Villain
got a view of his own profile that he has
never seen before. Mrs. Gass, a smart
woman off stage, was made up like a dusky
belle. And some of the others were nearly
as* bad. Little Dr. Pat Croom was a con-
spicuous success. He had to go the rounds
of a hospital, and he moved naturally
through his part.
The prettiest sassiety girls pi the show
suffered a blow to their vanity. Few pretty
girls now bother about deportment, so most
of these pets progressed like skittish calves.
Worst of all, they had to sit still and watch
themselves being awkward. When the hero
chases the train (with the villain aboard)
to Dover, the screen showed the Frankston-
Nc-pean-road. And so all through. Conant
might easily have made his story local.
Still, the film' provided the heartiest laugh
that some people have had since the out-
break of war. And as a money-making
novelty for the benefit of the Red Cross it
was an inspiration.
The “Federal Wives,” amalgamated with
the other matinde-ing Red Crossers, got off
their combined show at the King’s last Tues-
day. It went like a 15in. shell. As well
as two acts of Bates Post, there were numer-
ous Naice amateur shows. Mrs. Norman
Brookes, as Cleopatra, hung with real
jewels, and Walter Kirby, as (not exactly)
Antony, did an Idyll of the East. Dorothy
Pearce; Nancy Isaacs, Alys Bridges, Mrs.
Dunne and some other girls lay about on
cushions as Cleo’s court. They wore very
few clothes and very many jewels. Nancy
Isaacs had a band of diamonds round her
brow, and the little Lucas girl danced for
the lady. Kirby, his ferocious moustachios
dyed black and his face chocolate color,
wore a turban and a cloak of pale-green bro-
cade. When he stooped over Cleopatra’s
couch to caress her the lady missed a note in
a helpless giggle, while Kirby rolled his eyes
and warbled tenor soothing-syrup.
Then Fan Dango Mackay put on an ex-
quisite dream-boat song and tableau. Fan,
in luscious drapings, sang the song and 20
girls rowed in the background. She had
Mavis Thiel, Betty O’Hara, Dulcie Cooper
and a whole lot of other maidens for choris-
ters The clothes show was magnificent.
W 1 en Prime Minister Watt came on to the
stage to speak a piece he referred humbly
to his work-a-day duds among people dressed
like Solomon at bargain-sale time.
Mrs. Charles Lyon (she was Vera Dodds',
dressed as a nurse, had some real Anzacs
as a chorus. Her nice little drawing-room
voice could hardly be heard; but the
Anzacs helped her out and she looked awfully
well. The Reps., with Gregan McMahon
leading, gave “Makeshifts.” The Kiss Trot
was a swell ballet of sassiety girls, all in
black net filmies and bright-green obis. They
had been trained to the last kick, having
practised hard for the Conant film. The
sister of the Baillieux and Heather Keep
did a turn which they called “A Simple
Melody.”
Senator Pearce and a flpek of his con-
freres refused to be boxed and were crowded
in somewhere at the last minute. The boxes
had been raffled again and again for the
Cross, and were a sort of No Man’s Land.
Mrs. Gatehouse filled liers with a changing
stream of guests. Sailors from a warship
crowded into another. Lady Helen, Doris
Blackwood and a poss 6 of aides sat in the
circle. The amalgamated Red Crossers
fused so imperfectly that they took separate
stands. The “Federal Wives” division under-
took the floor of the house, and the others,
with Mrs. George Fairbairn at their head,
roosted upstairs.
An unearthly uproar, so far only lmlf-
audible through closed doors, has been going
on inside one of Melbourne’s smaller but
most incessantly-advertised “patriotic”
bodies. Members have had several lively
foregatherings, full of sound and fury, in
which the loudest distinguishable howl has
been for a heart-to-heart statement of
accounts. Tears, challenges, blushes, denials,
clamors for legal advice, allegations of lot-
tery-fakes and sharp practice have combined
to feed a stream of public cackle, now freely
slopping over. But no blame attaches to
anybody, of course, thank goodness!
By far the funniest episode at a recent
Uni. carnival was the Ugly Man Competi-
tion. Anyone could nominate anyone else,
and votes were a penny apiece. When two
profs, were entered votes rolled in by the
hundred, and it was soon apparent that it
would be a close finish between Mortarboard
and Textbook. At last, amid enthusiasm, it
was announced: “Professor Textbook, Ugli-
est Man in the Shop.” Textbook couldn’t
appreciate the joke, and even talked of
“proceedings.” The other pundit took it
more like an Australian. But perhaps that
was because he didn’t win.
Young Jim Baillieu struts in Captain
Conant’s film. The ex-Grammar youth went
away last year with the gunners, but has
been back here for some months with nerves.
Perhaps the young man ought to thank God
that his name isn’t Jim Brown. Jim Brown
suffers from nerves, too. But I wonder if
Jim Brown is told that he can go home.
And I wonder how many of those who are
charged with cowardice, and whose names
are going to be published in the future, are
brave boys for the time being unhinged—
subjects for sympathy rather than for
censure.
Another Head who has sustained in-
juries through putting his trust in trees is
Arthur Snowden, most ancient of City
Fathers. He, was pottering about his
Abbotsford garden when a piece of tree on
which he was resting snapped and let him to
the ground. With a younger man the mis-
hap might have been a subject for mirth,
but the aged knight had to put himself under
medical care.
Menzies’ writing-room looked like the holy
of holies in an expensive chiffon store last
week when Mrs. Fred Fairbairn had her
bazaar of undies, cushions and bags. Lady
Stanley came, and Sassiety followed. Grace
Palotta did a roaring trade with a spinning-
jenny, and the usual theatrical stars sang.
The Mayoress gave an At Home on
Thursday in the precious blue-and-gold bou-
doir. It was quite a Town Hall family
party. The Mayoress, in a little purple
toque and a black dress, received everybody,
informally, over tea. Mrs. Davey, Mrs.
Arthur Westley and all the aldermanic
ladies chatted and keenly discussed the
Town Hall kiosk, which will make money
outside the portico on Red Cross Day.
Those who love old editions, rare china,
Sheffield plate and Georgian silverware will
feel a sense of loss at the passing out of Pri-
vate Harold Halewood in France. Hale-
wood was a quiet sensitive little chap who
ran antique- and book-shops in Melbourne
and lately in Sydney. He leaves a widow,
whose stand in Victoria Arcade, Sydney, is
a well-known rendezvous for lovers of the
rare and beautiful.
Mrs. Tevita Tui Vakano, wife of Tonga’s
Premier, was handed morning tea at the
Oriental on Wednesday by a party mothered
by Mrs. Scott. The visitor is a large, lan-
guid lady tinted to a delicate chocolate
cream. Her eyes are large and languid, too,
and she droops wearily into the largest
chair whenever she gets a chance. She
speaks a little liquid English—and does
that languidly as well.
A Royal Red Cross goes to Nurse Isabel
Jobson, a sister of Sydney’s Brig.-General
Alec, D. 5.0., and of Ballarat’s Jeanie, the
school-teacher whose letters to Billjim have
been so highly appreciated. Isabel, with
another beauty, Leah Rosenthal, used to run
a private hospital at Toorak. The girls had
been pals since their training days at the
Alfred. Two and a half years ago they went
to the Front, and were both recommended,
for heroism under fire at a casualty clearing-
station. But, though both deserved it
equally, there was only one Red Cross to
spare, and Isabel got it.
Mrs. Sellheim has given 15 guineas which
has been turned into a wrist-watch valued
at £2O. The exquisite thing is being raffled
for the Red Cross.
Despatch from Adelaide :—•
W. L. Davidson has received word that son Hugh,
who has been fighting in East Africa with the
8.E.F., has been invalided to Durban and “men-
tioned.” An elder son, Roy, who was on his way
to England with his wife when she died in America,
is now in France, holding a commission with the
“tanks.”
Another “missing” Australian, Capt. Patrick 11.
Anld, has turned up as a prisoner of war in Ger-
many. He got the M.C. before the Huns nabbed
him.
Six pupils of James Ashton have won honors at
the Royal Drawing Society’s Exhibition in London,
despite that the works had evidently been damaged
by sea-water in transit. This is ; not the first time
that the submarine has made a mess of Australian
art samples, and the R.D.S. has decided to keep
possession of those in its charge till the war is over.
A fOte at Woodville, opened by the State
Commandant, brought the Red Cross Society
some much-needed money for succoring Australian
prisoners of Var, and a further £lO4 was added to
the funds by a gift of cattle at the stock sales on
Monday. The soaring price of meat may not be
quite so hard to bear if you can persuade yourself
that your particular joint came off one of the Red
Cross beasts.
Hilda Felstead, the Adelaide singer whose voice
has added piles of money to local patriotic funds,
has been much lauded lately in Asia, where she has
bees touring with “The Fantastics.” At Bangalore
(India) she found time to take unto herself a hus-
band, Captain C. M. Flanders. ‘ The wedding took
place on March 7, and to celebrate it the fortunate
man rode the winner in the Open Horse Race at
the Bangalore races. 'That night the new Mrs.
Flanders lifted up hev voice in the Victoria Hall
in the presence of the Governor and Lady Pentland
and an encouraging audience. When the singer has
finished her engagement with “The Fantastics,” she
will settle down in Bangalore.
The philanthropic work of two good citizens,
George Crase, lion. sec. of the Prisoners’ Aid Society
for 28 years, and the late A. W. Hendry, of the
Royal Institution for the Blind, is to be suitably
recognised by the public.
The Stock Exchange the other day handed out good
wishes and a wedding present to K. F. Timcke, one
of its members who was in the Gallipoli Landing
and was invalided home with wounds in August,
1915. Since his discharge he has twice tried and
failed to re-enlist. Two brothers are at the Front
and a third, who is just 18, is in camp.
Kenneth Henderson, the newly-appointed chaplain
at St. Peter’s College, is a Melbourne man who did
good work as a padre with the A.I.F. before being
invalided to England. He utilised part of his fur-
lough on the staff of Winchester College. Two of
his brothers, both of them with A.1.F., were killed
in the early days of the war.
Betty Paterson has divulged her undying
affection for Bruce Pearce, an amateur geiff
champion and a lad of some consequence in
Hobart. The young man, who is wearing the
title of captain, is shortly leaving to help the
Australian Comforts Fund in France. After
a spell of that he will return to seize his
bride and hurry off again.
Australian M.C.’s are going off before the
girls this side can get a chance. One of the
latest taken out of the market is Captain
Arthur Godfrey, M.C., who next month is to
wed a Sussex damsel named Barrett Len-
nard. The young man’s dad, Charles God-
frey, is a pillar of the National Bank.
“Inserted by his loving and proud fiancee”
is the postscript to a death notice in the Mel-
bourne papers of a Victorian sergeant who
gallantly won the D.C.M. and was killed in
France on Shakspeare’s birthday. That is
the cult of Sparta, and the girl who practises
it is worthy of a soldier’s salute. The civvy
salute, on the other hand, threatens to
become a drug in the market. A brood of
Warracknabeal damsels have just notified
their boys that they must put on khaki
within the week or leave their sweethearts
for good. This is the latest aid to recruiting
—the Jilt Patriotic.
The attractive Maitland McMicking girl
pleads guilty to intending matrimony. The
man is Murray Black ; he persuades sheep
to grow wool for him near Flinders.
Major Gordon Chirnside, who went away
with the first Australians as staff-chauffeur
and got promoted to Birdwood’s staff, is
making a brief return to Australia to see his
mother and have a peep at his fiancee, Mavis
Thiel. Rumor says they will marry ere the
Major returns to t rance. You see, an offi-
cer who may engineer leave can return to
France. But one who, possibly in a job
which has earned him the V.C., has been
wounded can only get back by re-enlisting
as a private. That’ll teach ’em to earn
V.C.’s.
Echuca, which sits on the border at the
junction of the Murray and Campaspe rivers,
wants all good Echuchus home for a carni-
val week in September, and to that end has
started to send coo-ees in every direction.
It says the shade trees in the streets have
grown wonderfully, and that the Oldest
Inhabitants are lasting just as well as the
red-gum arch they put up for Governor
Loch in ’B4.
One enthusiastic correspondent, bidding
me whoop up the Echuean pilgrimage, ex-
plodes like this: —
Come back to Echuca
And see how it’s grown !
Back here in Echuca
Your triumphs are known!
The city won’t miss you
Or end in a wreck;
Echuca will kiss you
And fall on your neck.
Now, here’s your chance!
Annie Macky, wife of Dr. Stewart Macky,
and Cecilia John have started a People’s
Conservatorium at the Guild Hall, and are
offering a course of English Literature and
at a guinea a quarter for union-
ists and their children and £4 4s. a quarter
for others. A liberal system of scholarships
is being arranged, and it is hoped later to
develop schools of drawing and painting.
Artist Max Moldrum, Tarcynsky (the long-
haired, blonde male who leads the Profes-
sional Orchestra) and clever Jollie Smith,
lady barrister and Socialist, are lending a
hand.
Cahill, the Bananaland pianist, is still
touring the towns of his native State with
baritone Brookes. He writes to half-frozen
Melbourne friends about a motor tour, of IX>7
miles during which they were frizzled by dsty
and mosquito-eaten by night.
Uosslyn Vane, who used to appear in
Naice roles before the war, is now Mrs. j
Arthur Greenaway. She is fretting her
heart out for some real stage work. Her
present job is to understudy Thais Lawton.
Mrs. Gatepost, which is the vulgar version
of the American mummer’s name, did her
bit for the Empire Day celebrations by sing-
ing from a lorry crowded with Pierrots. The
lady, who was a musical-comedy star before
she married Guy, has two hobbies —hats and
dogs.
The girls of the “Bing Boys” are organ-
ising a hop at the Town Hall on June G.
The Australian Comforts Fund is the jus-
tification.
The American War-Workers for Aus-
tralian Soldiers are a small lot to carry so
much name. Not more than 30 or 40
families supply the members, but the small
crew get through much work, and already
have several laundry baskets filled with sen-
sible goods ready for Red Cross Day. Some
of them are going to be sandwich-women,
too. They will on the day dress in black,
with their hair bound tight, and each will
wear a big canvas back-and-front apron
carrying a four-guinea advertisement.
Yours sincerely.
JOHANNA.

BARNES LINSEED OIL
“Bauragi” : “McNorth’s” yarn of the
prisoner who burned down his Papuan gaol because
it was Verminous recalls other calabooses in that land
of casuals. One white prisoner whom it was my
duty to detain m custody on a charge of murder had
to wait some months to bo tried, so it was agreed
that he should spend the day over at the storekeeper’s
and return to his dungeon at 9.30 p.m. The sergeant
was sent with a. lantern to escort him home every
night, and in the mornings he rose at his conveni-
ence and strolled over to the store, where he fared
sumptuously. One flight the pilot forgot the lantern
and both men fell off the bridge into a muddy gully.
The prisoner was Very stuffy about it next morning,
and complained so bitterly of being called away
just as he was drawing a pair to fill a full hand
that his time was extended to 11 p.m. and orders
given to the sergeant not to butt in till the Kitty
was killed. When he departed to do two years in
Samarai he left an impression on my mind that he
regarded me as a callous and brutal bureaucrat. Then
there was Mukowa. He murdered his boss with a
shotgun and was hanged. He was the cheerfullest
and most likeable prisoner I ever had the privilege
of meeting. When the missionary converted him and
asked me to stand godfather at the christening I
hadn’t the heart to refuse. Later, when, as deputy-
sheriff, I hail to superintend the send-off I was in
a position which 1 doubt if any godfather ever
occupied before me. Other notables were Vakemba,
who ate a whole prisoner while bringing him a long
journey to gaol; Tabe, who tested a magician’s
powers by sinking him all night in the river with a
stone round his neck, and got two years for the
experiment; and the seven escapees who sailed all the
way front New Caledonia in a boat of their own
building, only to be attested on landing in Papua.
They were finally reprovisioned and told to go to the
Devil, up German New Guinea way, which they did,
unrffjpicing.

‘•W.H.E.”: “Ivell B.’s” protest (B.
fl/o/’lfi) against the “breaking in” methods of num-
berless horse-murderers is more than justified. There
is no animal more susceptible to kindness, and none
gets less of it than the average moke undergoing the
“breaking” process. One of the pet methods of the
professional -is to run an animal into the 'stockyard
and starve it into docility for three or four days
prior to beginning its “education.” The horse is as
intelligent and apt to learn as the average schoolboy,
and the sooner the teacher adopts a tempting lolly-
stick in place of hip hide-splitting whipcord the
quicker ho will find out the wide gulf which separates
him from the true horse-master. 1 have just finished
educating our latest purchase. She was passed on
to me as incorrigible, yet hand massage and occa-
sional sweets as peace offerings have placed us on
the most intimate terms of confidence and friendship.
Whipcord? Its best use'is for restraining fools.
“Jacques” : Probably “H.M.’s” terrier bitch
who lias a habit of feeding off her tail is troubled
with worms. Next to distemper they-are the worst
scourge of the kennel. They are commonly an
accompaniment of lice. Areca-nnt is an excellent
vermifuge, and should be given fasting. A terrier-
bitch 12 months old ought to digest about as much
as will cover a threepenny-bit, in olive-oil, given in
the form of a capsule. Another good vermifuge is
about Tgr. or Bgr. of santonin every other day on an
empty stomach, followed by a dose of castor-oil. It
is quite possible, on the other hand, that the bitch
is being worried by an external parasite, such as
a tick, in which case a thorough examination should
be jiiiadn of her; if anything is discovered, equal
parts of paraffin and olive-oil should b« applied- with
a brush. Once a title-sinks his beak Into the flesh
never try to pull it off, as lie will leave behind part
of his face and the result will be a large, painful sore
* * » *
“F.Y.7”: Brumbies were .plentiful enough
anywhere in Western Queensland up to a few years
ago wherever the country was thickly timbered, and
hilly for choice. Your true brumby does not care
for open country. They were so thick in South-
Western Bunanaland in the ’Bo’s that it was thought
worth while to build big trap-yards with wings miles
long, not scantling affairs, hut high and heavy post-
und-rail, fit to hold elephants. One such in the trap
paddock on Noondoo station, in the St. George
country, was built over 30 years ago and still stands,
a fine advertisement |pr Australian timber and work-
manship.

“Mustard Gold’’: Gold-escorting nowadays
in Westralia has hardly any of the thrill which dis-
tinguished the despatch of metal to the banks in
bygone times. The Boulder mines send it into Kal-
goorlie mostly per lorry, with pallid, unferocious
clerks squatting on the square boxes, and nobody
would suspect that here was the precious metal in
transit by the ton but for the accompanying mounted
John behind with carbine negligently unswung. Qnlv
twice has an escort been attacked in the whole
history of the fields, and the stuff then in hand was
not the raw material, but money released from the
bank for wages. A manager going out with the
pay to a mine at Burbanks was found gagged and
bound, with the boodle gone; in the other case a
GETTING IT BOTH WAYS.
AGGRIEVED HOURSEHOLDER (surprising the early milkman) : “Here, old friend, go steady
with that water. Don't forget, I’ve got to pay for it."
masked horseman pounced on a party #hnt was carry-
ing wages along the Boulder road, and, snatching
a fair swag, vanished, and was never traced. Ex-
perience has proved that the unostentatious way is
the best way of getting the bullion to its destination
unscathed. Extraordinary' precautions aw’aken the
interest of the passing burglar. Thousands of
pound,s’ worth of gold are regularly floating in from
the mlilga to Kalgoorlie and other banks, just pitched
on to the counters by obscure individuals who
brought it along in the innocent old portmanteau
which travelled unnoticed in the train-rack or
jarhbed the entrance to the lavatory. A few years
ago a £20,000 parcel Of ingots from Morgans to
Kalgoorlie : lay unsuspected beneath the seat of a
packed train, while the youth in charge played
poker with’ wandering bagmen. Fred Merton, ere he
sold out the famous Reward to Kaufmarm, conveyed
most of his battery results —arid they were often
large—-to the bank at Malcolm, some- 20 miles dis-
tant, the'mine sulky, with a revolver lying on
the seat. A crony passing on the track was shown
glittering lumps of smelted material rolled up in a
chaff-bag at the driver’s feet. “Three thousand
ounces there,’-’ said Merton, as he jogged along the
lonely road' where any sort of ambush might have
been successful. Escorting sometimes went wrong
when some -mate in a mining enterprise, entrusted
with the job of putting the clean-up in the bank,
heaved himself aboard a convenient train and slith-
ered out of the country. Broad Arrow has a record
of a wily member of a cyanide “joint” setting out
with the approval of his three partners to dispose
of the final results extracted from an old sandheap
and getting clean away with the lot. They caught
up just in time to see the steamer breasting out into
Gage Roads from Fremantle, and the man waving
them farewell over the side was the absconding pal.
There were reasons why he wasn’t pursued to the
bitter end.

“Breeza”: Station men are well aware
that a dog soon learns when a sheep is needed for
the gallows, and how confidently, when he hears the
knife being sharpened, he makes tracks to round-up
the flock without waiting for orders. But this kelpie
went a bit farther. One of the hands was rubbing
up a knife on the grindstone, and Jerry woke up to
the noise. He quietly slipped away, brought up the
sheep and guarded them until, getting restless at the
delay, he singled out the meat, tipped it over and
stood by until the chap finished his job at the
grinder. Being a decent sort of bloke, the hand
killed the jumbuek rather than offend such a dog.
“Izaak Walton”: I agree with “Barcoo”
(B. 25/4/’18) that there are two species of the croco-
dile family in Australian waters. One resembles the
alligator in having a short snub, snout, while the
other takes after the .gavial with an elongated narrow
snout. The estuarine crocodile (C. Poroxus ) is the
more common. It generally, if not invariably, in-
habits the tidal portions of rivers, and may also be
seen some distance at sea. It is unknown on the
western coast of India, but is common on the east
coast and along to Southern China, and in North
Australia and the Solomon and Fiji Islands. It is the
largest of all the groups (caimans, ’gators, croco-
diles and gavials), being credited with a maximum
length of 33ft. Brutes of 26ft. are not uncommon.
This fellow seems to be the most savage Of its kind,
being powerful enough to attack cattle. The eggs
are generally; laid in a sandy spot and buried about
6i.n. beneath the surface. The infants break out of
the egg by means of a specially-shaped tooth. While
young they are able to utter buzzing grunts similar
to the sound made by the Queensland trumpeter.
Ihe harmless croc., or Johnston’s crocodile, is much
smaller than its brethren, attaining a maximum
length of six or seven feet. It has a long slender
snout, and forms the connecting link between croco-
diles and gavials. Its principal food is fish, particu-
larly freshwater catfish. Its sole habitat is the
•Northern Territory and N. Queensland. Ido not hold
that full-grown crocodiles are capable of uttering any
noise whatever/ The mangrove swamps are full of
stiange and weird sounds, especially at night, chiefly
due to the booming of the bittern. In the creeks
around Trinity Inlet (Cairns) the booming of this
bird is generally put down to the croc.

“S.T.”: “Stinkwort,” brought into S.
Aus. about 0 years ago—said to have been intro-
duced by German settlers as a flavoring for hams
and such things—is having its usual autumn cursing.
Ihe weed flowers arid sheep die, and the two things
are said to be cause and result. Place, the State
\ r et., does not think so. It is no poison, he says, but
the flowers “do undoubtedly cause a mechanical
obstruction. ’ Given three practical precautions, the
weed can be considered harmless: (1) Have it “fed
off while quite young; (2) see that the sheep have
other feed as well; and (3) in particular give them
access to a lick of salt and lime, with a dash of
sulphur.

FISHING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Waiting for the ferry, on the little pier,
Worries of the city drawing very near ;
But the flickering long line, where the’ shadows lie,
Holds each brain in thraldom, fascinutcs each eye.
Catching by the dozen fish with silver fins,
Clinging to the furthest pile with his wet, brown
shins,
Trim men, grim men, men with heavy frown,
Match the youngster catching fish, on their way to
town.
Once again the trim man is a ragged boy,
Hanging o er a muddy creek, knowing all the joy
(Reckless of to-morrow’s cane and its burning sting)
Of a truant holiday, fishing with a string.
1 ickling all the waters-still, into dimples deep
Trails the line in shadow where the small fish leap.
Grim man’s eyes are snapping: “That’s a tip-top
bait—
Giving .hem their breakfast sudden, aren’t you,
Mate?”
Frowning man is. dreaming—how the ripples spin!
Once a sun-baked youngster with a merry grin
Left the fishes biting, found his way to town,
Won a, trick from Fortune—and a heavy frown.
Linking up the city with the little pier,
Kilkily the ferry steals through the morning clear ;
But trim man, grim .man, man with heavy frown
Take a dream of silver-fins with them into town.
N.s.w.

STRENGTH, AND HOW TO GAIN IT.
INSTEAD of EXERCISES, PATENT
JOODS AND MEDICINES. TAKE
PHOSPHATE WITH YOUR MEALS.
The demand to-day is for men and women
who are strong in every sense of the word—
possessing the physical strength necessary to"
endure hardships and fatigue; the mental
strength to grapple with difficult problems;
the nervous force which endows the body with
vigor and vitality; the will power to triumph
over adversity and turn defeat into victory.
But such glorious strength is impossible so
iong as your nerves are weak and exhausted,
and therefore if you would be really strong
you must first care for your nerves. Weak,
exhausted nerves need food, and it has been
proved in numberless cases that the only food
element they can or will absorb readily and
naturally is phosphate in the well-known
torm of bßro-phosphate, which most chemists
stock in ogr. compressed tablets, so that if
you feel your strength is failing from any
cause you should get a supply of these bitro-
phosphate tablets and take one with every
meal. Practically all the minor ailments afflict-
ing mankind, as well as many of the more
serious maladies, can be traced to nervous ex-
haustion and lowered vitality, and probably
this explains why such a remarkable improve-
ment in the general health is invariably
noticeable when bitro-phosphate is taken as
directed, and the nerves are thereby revital-
ised and made strong. —Advt.

SPEEDING HIM UP.

“Yes, I know he’s very much opposed to long engagements.”
“Dug-out” : How’s this for a lively scrap?
One night a red-backed spider pegged out a claim in
the corner of my stable that a wasp had comman-
deered for her building operations, and proceeded to
use part of the wasp tenement as a support for the
web. In the morning when the wasp arrived with
building materials, she was quite overcome by in-
dignation. Dropping her bundle, she surveyed the
surroundings for the offender. Red-back was soon
located sitting with his legs crossed in his new
residence picking his teeth iii a self-satisfied mood
and evidently amused at the ravings of the noisy
mud-lumper. This state of affairs was soon altered.
In about two seconds the wasp had cleared for
action, and as a preliminary sought to break the
enemy’s morale with a flow of curious shrieks and
buzzings. These it followed up with a furious
charge straight through the entanglements. The
rush in massed formation staggered the spider, who
after a few lively rounds retreated towards a dug-
out some three feet away. After hurling a few
threats into the hole in which her defeated enemy
Iminus two and a half legs) had taken refuge, the
wasp returned to her building wearing a Napoleonic
expression, and in a few minutes* business was re-
sumed as usual. Always put your money on the wasp
in these affairs.
* * * *
“Melos”: Here’s the way to prepare a
dinkum hair-restorer. Get a flask of brandy and
search around till you find a real live “jumpers’ ”
nest. Drink half the liquor, and then, standing the
flask near the ant-heap, put a straw or reed into
the neck of the bottle, bending the free end down-
ward till it comes in contact with the nest. The
junipers crawling along the straw will lose their bal-
ance and fall into the juice. When the deceased are
piled up to the bottle-neck, cork the mixture and
macerate for two weeks. Cantharides, the active
ingredient in most hair-ticklers, is a back number
compared with this home product.
“Bill”: The generally-accepted idea that
emus will not breed in captivity is exploded by a
visit to the pleasure-grounds at Oatley Bay, a
delightful little spot on George’s River, near Como
(N.S.W.), where a pair of these birds will proudly
exhibit their latest arrival. Two chicks were
hatched, but for some breach of military discipline
the old cock knocked the stuffing out of one.

“Dug-out” : If it’s only a cure for boils
you want here’s the speediest and most reliable known
to bush science. Fill a pickle-bottle with boiling
water and let it stabd for five minutes. Then empty
and when the bottle cools slightly place the neck of
it over the tumor and hold in that position. If the
skin has not been previously broken make a small
opening with a needle before placing the glassware
in position. In 16 minutes the pus should be out
and the trouble over.
* * * *
“E.S.S.”: Some sheep-breeders have been
stating lately that the lousy starling (it is the host
of an obnoxious greyback, which it often carries
into houses) is a friend of the pastoralist, inasmuch
as it feeds on blowflies and their maggots. No evi-
dence has been produced to prove the
claim. While such a known pest is
championed by the pastoralists, it is
strange that dozens of really useful
Australian birds are either ignored or
treated with hostility. Nothing, of
course, is any good that does not come
from foreign Countries. Very few of
these stinking-fishmongers have a good
word for the crow; yet the black
scavenger feeds largely on grasshoppers
and maggots. Watch one round a dead
beast and you can soon realise how
much he does in keeping down the
blowfly pest. Yet, instead of being
treated as a friend, he is -blamed for the doings of
the raven, and up to a shilling a head is paid for
his destruction. It’s a double-barrelled extravagance.

“Nostaw”: Tell The Bulletin corres-
pondent who was surprised at a gent, staking beer
with his porridge that, when a kid, my father, in
common with hundreds of other men from Notting-
ham (Eng.), mainly subsisted on porridge made with
beer and washed down with more beer. And he’s no
invalid now, either.
"Two S«as”: I amerga from the seclusion
of my hollow log to assure “Bad Australian” (B.
9;"S/’IS) that some rock-wallabies do climb trees, or,
rather, run up those with a slightly leaning barrel;
and that all “rookies” are decorated with furry tails.
The animal with the bristly-haired tail that “8.A.”
saw in his misspent youth was probably the
“brusher” or scrub-wallaby, whose celerity in leav-
ing a vacancy when he felt his presence was super-
fluous gave rise to the expression of the bookie with
an empty bag when he sees the wrong number go
up—“ Give ’em brusher!”

As the prize ip a quaint competition sug-
gested for its annual show, the Vic. Agricultural
Society has been offered £3 3s. by William Kodier,
the ingenious citizen who came into prominence
some years ago through his proposition for ridding-
Australia of superfluous rabbits. Kodier’s plan, the
result of his own and some other people’s researches,
was to kill off all the females and let Brer Rabbit go
free. The State was indifferent, rabbit-suppression
having little to do with Votes, and nothing was
done. So now Rodier has written to the Agricul-
tural Society, urging that, as rabbiting has been
made an industry, it should be put on a sound com-
mercial basis by improving the breed, hi.s £8 3s.
prize to be awarded to the breeder of the best pen
of five rabbits! The sobersided society, having a sus-
picion that somebody might be pulling somebody
else’s leg, decided to “regretfully decline” the offer.

“Jobber”: Come to Flanders if you want
to see dinkum cockying. for intense cultivation 1
give the inhabitants the biscuit. Stock kept in
stalls nearly all the year, and the bedding and
manure carted out and ploughed in. We could take
a pattern from their ploughs, too; they’re all rever-
sible, and while we are turning on the headland
Jeanne is half-wuy down the furrow. There are no
fences, only an occasional hedge, and not a square
yard of a field is idle. The horses do one’s heart
good to look at; it’s most unusual to see one low-
in condition. Tell some of the lads at home about
it, will you? They might come over—if it’s only
to see the ploughing.

“Mrs. Lance itawson”: Re mechanical'
feet. Back in the ’Bo’s in Maryborough (Q.), Lassie,
a half-caste maiden, had a foot snapped off by
a shark. Bor some months she went about on the
stump and a stick. Then the citizens took up a
collection and got Lassie a new foot, which she wore
for- a time with some pride and a good deal of
swagger—not to say stagger during her periodical
drunk. Finally she confided to the subscribers:
“That too much nice feller foot no good wear ’em
ebry day, mine tirik. Keep it longa Sunday—him too
flash-feller longa ebry day.” Some weeks later when
I inquired for the foot she said: “Oh, that old foot!.
Him stop longa diUybag. Him no good\ longa foot.
Him very good hammer—knock ’em oyster bery good.
Close up me killein Charley Brown longa dat feller.
My word, him good lor.gu something.”
“Ida Dawson”: It will be interesting to
see if the old line “Many berries bring a cold winter”
holds good this year, for the gullies between Nimity-
belle and the McLaughlin River (N.S.W.) are a
crimson blaze with the berries of the wild briar. The
sweet-smelling but, unfortunately, noxious weed
has a heavier crop than I have ever before observed.
The poplars and aspens along the route, also, are
holding their leaves better than usual. As a rule
the leaves drop as they turn color, starting at the
top of the tree. This year the trees are still in full
foliage, and every leaf is a glorious yellow..

“Merrigal”: Though the ancient and ever
polite Samoans practised many forms of salutation,
sniffing being the commonest, family greetings were
not included. Even a husband did not salute his
missus after a long absence. Words of welcome for
early-morning and after-dinner visitors were, re-
spectively, Ua sautia (“You come with the dew on
you”), and Ua poulingia (“You come in the dark-
ness”). A visitor to make his marble good kissed
the hand of a common or garden chief, but when
the big man was Sacred ' the guest wiped his own
chivvy with the dignitary’s apron. There were
many othei interesting customs. A receiver placed
a gift on his head, in token of acknowledgment.
Admission of guilt and a plea for forgiveness were
signified by the delinquent making obeisance before
the tabernacle of the offended chief or in some
public place of the aggrieved village. A surer par-
don-producer was i the bringing in the hands of the

NEWCHUM : “Gripes! The Recruiting-Ser-
geant told me it was good sport at the
Front, hut I don't see much of it about

offender firewood and Monos, thereby presenting him-
self as a pig to be cooked and eaten. To carry an
open umbrella, past a - chief's caboose, and to cock a
log, or to throw anything over the head of another
"iff w ere grave insults. Travelling through or by a
village where a caliph was lying in state was an
offence. A war party considered a bird flying across
its path as a bad omen, and the appearance of a
1 ainbow in its rear was the office to postpone hostili-
ties. Travellers passed the time of day and made
nay for each other at meeting, and workmen downed
tools temporarily when a visitor approached. Some
Melanesian customs are also worthy of notice. A
stone wrapped in a leaf and deposited on a
New Briton’s pathway is a warning to make the
right-about. A leaf placed on the shoulder of
another is a compliment demanding a gift in return,
but if the vegetation be left where a crime has
been perpetrated it is a polite hint that the per-
petrator will be knocked endways. In some parts
of New Ireland patting the top of the head with the
hand means “Glad to meet you,” while in other parts
the citizens have a courteous method of expressing the
same thing, viz., by pinching the navel.

Business, Robbery, etc.
T.. 1.1).”: I started the following message
°fr to Sydney from a country P.O. per
’phone operated by a deaf postjnistress:—
Send eight-day eight-inch octagonal clock; hurry
gas fittings.
The nearest telegraph-office was operated by
a casual chap, who sent it on its journey
this way:—
Send eighty agents diagonal socks hairy gasfitters.
My agents wouldn’t accept the order; but
the sight of 80 hairy gasfitters wearing
diagonal socks would have been some com-
pensation if they had.
“Machete”: A correspondent in a recent
Bulletin praised the virtues of blackberry
wine and asked why the stuff isn’t put on
the market. It is—as a liqueur, at any rate
- —and it has been responsible for more head-
aches than even racecourse whisky. The
Greek cafe-keepers of Cairo specialise in it
as its sweet, syrupy flavor prevents detection
of all sorts of other abominations. Many a
Billjim can testify to its knock-out qualities.

“T. 0.1.”: The Paragon Hotel, which
formed part of the Hordern estate, has been
sold for £35,000. The ground on which the
house stands is “made” or “saved” from
Sydney Cove. Built in the early ’sixties, it
was first .occupied by Catherine Brown ; the
Watson family have been in possession since
1808, when they succeeded that pioneer. The
original It. A. Watson was a leading aquatics
“sport” of his day: hence the waterside pub.
* * * *
“Solomon M.”: “In evidence before. the
Inter-State Commission several witnesses
stated that they were not able to obtain
supplies of Australian-made cloth direct
from-' the mills. They had to buy through
the Wholesalers, who made a needless profit.”
It is more than 20 years since a N.S.W.
manufacturer told the writer the same story,
and gave’these reasons: (1) The business
wasn't very large owing to high expenses
and low tariff and the carefully fostered
loathing of Australian goods, and it was
widely scattered. If he ran it direct he
would have to send out as many travellers
for his one line us the importer who had
100 lines to sell. (2) When a bagman
dumped down the card of Smith, Australian
manufacturer, his only reward in most cases
was a shudder. When he dumped down the
card of “Brown, importer,” he could pass
some of Smith’s goods in the crowd. (3)
Brown, importer, was willing to sell the
goods of Smith, manufacturer, if he got a
sufficient commission and provided Smith
pledged himself not to sell his own goods
except through the Brown fraternity. Other-
wise Smith, manufacturer of Australian
goods with Australian labor at Australian
wages, could go to perdition at the bidding
of the almighty Brown, importer.

“The Oldest Inhabitant” :
Mardie and Gorman’s removal from Pitt-street
after many years recalls some of the early estate
salesmen of . Sydney, though, it is only within the last
half-century that estate salesmen, pure and simple—
if they ever are simple—came into existence. Some
of the first real property sold by auction in Aus-
tralia was that of George Barrington, the historic
pickpocket; this sale was conducted by Simeon Lord
at Parramatta on January 2, 1805. James Larra,
an innkeeper, had the conduct of the sale of the
estate of Captain William Cox when that pioneer
over-speculated in land. There were 14 lots, includ-
ing the historic Brush Farm, sold on April 4, 1804.
In the ’twenties and ’thirties estate-agents became
more numerous under a license Thomas
Bodenham, one of the most 'notable, hung out in
George-street, in the saloon of the Royal Hotel (after-
wards burned down). On March 14, 1832, he offered
for sale, in allotments, Hutchinson’s paddock, identi-
fiable now by Bond-street, which ran through the
block. Bodenham, who left about 1834, was the pre-
cursor of Tom Stubbs, who was flourishing in Sydney in
the late ’thirties and early ’forties, but emigrated to
Melbourne in 1847. Sam Lyons (the Union S.S. Co. is
now building on the site of his auction room) sold
12 lots on the N.E. corner of King and George
streets in May, 1834, for £10,318, a price until
then unknown. Bodenham’s successor was Thomas
Ware Smart, who became Colonial Treasurer. Abra-
ham Polaek—a name to conjure with at one time —
was commissioned by W. C. Wentworth to sell the
earliest Vaucluse subdivision; also a great part of
the Chippendale estate passed through his hands.
In the’forties T. S-Mort was in evidence. In the mid-
’fifties his sign was in Pitt-street. He was succeeded
there in the late ’sixties by Rayner, Treeve and Co.,
about whom silence were golden. In the later ’fifties
Robert Pemberton Richardson was in business on the
corner of George and Jamieson streets. Later he
moved into Pitt-street, where he was joined by
Edward Wrench, who had been manager of the
A.J.S. Bank, and Richardson and Wrench came into
existence. Hardie and Gorman made their first
appearance in 1873.
* * * *
“T. 0.1.”: In earlier days in Melbourne
seme novel cases of insolvency came before
the judges. A man presented his schedule
to Judge Barry, the punctilious. The liabil-
ities were not heavy, but the assets were
returned as nil. The Judge declined to
accept this, as the insolvent had no estate
to sequestrate. “What am I to do, your
Honor?” quoth the impecunious one. “I
am not your legal adviser,” replied the Old
Man, “but I should, if I were in your case,
spend a shilling on a razor—and then seques-
trate it.” Which the insolvent did.
* * * *
“0.L.”; The tram was crowded and there
was a lot of noise, but in between cackles
and rumbles this remark stood out plain and
shrill: “Annie lost her tail inside three
months!” Ermyntrude explained, when I
asked her about it later in the evening, that
no doubt it was Annie’s ten-guinea blue-
wolfskin which had parted from its south-
ern attachment. Nowadays the demand for
furs is so incessant, it seems, that the fur-
riers don’t worry about the quality of their
work, when putting together the various
parts which go to make up a “perfect animal
skin,” as the advts. say; and Annie’s tail
was therefore like a whole lot of other expen-
sive but poorly-fashioned war-time luxuries.
**. * #
Coyne, who has just handed in the Rail-
way portfolio in Bananaland to take charge
of Lands, is another believer in the resources
of his own State. Soon after he took charge
of Railways the high cost of imported tim-
ber used for handles of heavy tools set him
thinking. Soon a plant was set up at Ipswich
workshops, and now the articles are being
turned out. The timber used is crow’s-foot
elm, spotted gum and crow’s ash, and the
workmen have assured Coyne that the
quality is right.
* * * »
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: We’ve had the
I ositive Clearance Sale” of a very old Syd-
ney carrying firm, R. Shortland and Sons.
In the fifties it was John Woods and Thomas
McDonnell, “agents for carriers,” in Camp-
bell-street. That was in the days when
carrying to country towns by bullock-teams
was a picturesque occupation. In the early
’sixties Richard Shortland was on the scene
as a proprietor; about the same time
the late “Butty” McMahon was following
the same occupation. In 1867 Woods and
Shortland, with William Adair, entered into
partnership. Next year George Fox and
Butty joined the firm. In the first
seventies the Sydney United Omnibus Co.
was started. It mopped up most of the
small proprietors. The chairman was John
Young; directors: John Woods, Richard
Shortland, W. R. Hall and Henry Hoyt.
John Woods was managing director, and long
Tom Hales, who was said to hail from Texas,
was traffic manager. The carrying business
was now in the hands of Woods, Shortland
and McMahon. In 1875 one Barber joined,
and in the following year a dissolution took
place, Shortland and Sons, Butty McMahon
and John Woods conducting separate busi-
nesses. The McMahons are the survivors;
but omnibuses are unknown, and the carry-
ing trade is confined to the city wharves,
warehouses and the railway station.
* * * *
“H.M.” : Talking about sharkskin leather,
I have seen it made from carpet and brown
sharks caught by myself. That from the
brown species was made into a pair of boots
by the tanner, and they are still going
strong after two years’ wear. This tanner,
who has had nearly 50 years at the trade,
informed me that sharkskin makes one of
the toughest leathers he has had anything
to do with, the grain being crossed. Where
the sea lawyers are plentiful the foundation
of a new industry awaits development.
* * * *
“Bad Australian” (from Argentine) :
In the good old days on the Salitre Pampa, in
the provinces of Antofagasta and Tarapaca, when
the English nitrate cos. were “stacking them on
their edges,” almost everybody walked around with
a pocket full of shinplaster and a skinful of pisco
or rye whisky. The staffs of these companies were a
mixed crowd—English, Irish, Scots, Canadians, Aus-
tralians and a few South Americans. On one of the
Ti it rate establishments the boys had been improving
the shining hour to such an extent that the boss
received a cable from the London office asking why the
output had decreased to such an alarming extent
during the last quarter. There was a hurried con-
ference between all hands and it was decided to
reply that the output of crude nitrate of soda had
been greater than ever, but that a large percentage
had been eaten up by the comenchaca. The comen-
chaca is a dense fog which occasionally blows up
from the Pacific and causes the salitre exposed to it
to sweat and run like brine. In due course the boss
received a notification from headquarters that the
explanation was considered satisfactory, and that
in order to obviate any further trouble of a like
nature the Board had despatched 3000 rat-traps to
Chile, which it trusted would be sufficient to put
a stop to the ravages of the comenchacas. The
ironmongery arrived almost as soon as the letter of
advice, and the grin on the face of the Coast was
so wide for weeks after that it got wrinkles on the
back edges of its weather-bitten ears.
THE MINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It looms out on the common land
Beforg our cottage pane,
And I must heed its fierce command,
And seek my grave again.
I hear it growling through the night,
And ever in my bed •
Shall quake before the monster’s might,
And know it must be fed.
Its grey hair streams across the skv,
And burns against the west
The fury of its baleful eye
That drags me from my rest.
It murders peace, and ruthless kills
The joy of every scene,
And all the weary year it spills
Its vomit on the green.
I fain would walk the sunlit earth,
But with an ceric cry
It calls me from all cleanly mirth,
And, 10, its slave am I.
I fain would bathe in limpid air
The holy moon beneath,
But filters to me everywhere
The champing of its teeth.
O God, I hate this bloodless thing!
I loathe its steaming breath,
And fear its ghosts that bat-like cling
In avenues of death.
I shudder at the rending blast
A-crouch beside my truck.
A .Tonali I. The world has cast
Me overboard for luck.
More dread my woes than suffered he
Who fled the Lord in vain—•
Each day my monster swallows me,
And casts me up again!
YICTORIA,'
EDWARD DYSON.
The Inky Way .
> The death of Hugh Garland, of Adelaide'
Register staff, is. a reproach to- the system
which makes Australia, for want of enough
fresh men, send back to the trenches those
who have already done more than their
share. He was in at the end of Gallipoli; and
at Pozieres gained a commission, a D.C.M.
and several wounds. After recovering he
had a second turn in the line, and was then
sent home, but got away for a third cam-
paign in France. Of Register men, Lodge;,
Jacobs, Knowles and Steer had already been'
killed in the war; Garland makes the fifth-
as he really seemed to expect he would when
he wont back. He wrote enough good war
sketches to make a very readable book.
* * * *
In recent Australian papers appeared'
“the Official Reporter’s” tale of a stirring
encounter. “A Queensland officer” (it
would discourage recruiting to mention
names in such circumstances) was chasing
a retreating Hun, who eventually kam-
eraded and turned out to be an ex-waiter
in a London hotel. Here’s how the yarn
was cabled: “The Queensland officer
chased him, calling out, ‘Surrender, you
illegitimate!’” And in precisely those
words the faithful Argus printed it, evoking
shrieks of laughter from everyone in khaki
who read it.
* * * *
Killed in France, Lieut. John Wilson Kerr,
of the Vic. Government Printing Office. This
good fighting man, who left Australia as a
private, was a son of one of the very few
women who run Baekblock papers in Aus-
tralia —Mrs. Robert Kerr, of the Cobden
Times, which looks out on the world from a.
Victorian hamlet.
* * * *
“Chickadee”: Among the copper-catching
devices of the Melbourne Winner is the
offer of a prize to the owner of a marked
head in a group of people photographed at
a sports meeting or elsewhere. The chosen
head is encircled. At an up-country school
reading-lesson the subject was a famous
sacred picture in which every head wears a
halo. The school-marm endeavored to im-
press on the kiddies the fact that a halo was
the hall-mark of the angelic. One young-
ster entered a caveat. “Please, Miss,” he
said, “in the Winner I seen a soldier with a~
halo!”
* * * *
The Lyric Club in Adelaide has made a
surprising change of face. It has existed to
support the lighter forms of music, with
the drama as a side-line. It has a dwindling
membership, but commodious premises,
which lately attracted the journalists, who
joined in a body and are now (the last
annual report remarks) the “mainstay” of
the club. So it has changed its name to
Adelaide Press Club, and taken for its
president D. J. Gordon, who was Chief of
Staff on the Register before he took to
politics.
* * * *,
London Times, which, owing to cumula-
tive increases in the price of paper, had to
put up its own price to 3d. on March 11,
printed a list of changes since 1785: —
Jan. 1, 1785 .. 3d. Oet. 1,1861 .. 3d.
Apl. 21, 1794 . . 41d. May 5, 1913 . . 2d.
Jan. 1,1799 .. 6d. Mar. 16,1914 .. Id.
May 22, 1809 . . 61d. Nov. 20, 1916 .. lid.
Sept. 1, 1815 .. 7d. Feb. 20, 1917 .. 2d.
Sept. 15, 1836 .. sd. Mar. 11, 1918 . _ 3d.
July 1, 1855 .. 4d.
The maximum was reached in Waterloo
year; and after the awful drain of the
Napoleonic wars, that price had to be main-
tained for 21 years. Forty per cent, of
Britain’s shipping was destroyed in that
great struggle ; and shipbuilding wasn’t the
speedy business then that it is to-day.

Norman Lilley, who used to control the
Sydney Worker’s page about books and
writers, found that culture didn’t cut much
ice with the people who kept the paoer going
in thoke days, so he pulled out and found a
job on the Argus. Now he roars gently in
the leader-writers’ pen.
AS SMITH SEES IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a little, bald old man, with corns upon his
feet,
And when he talked his tones approached an almost
lamblike bleat;
Ilis back was bowed, he shuffled on like some down-
trodden soul
AVho passed his days in humble toil to earn his
weekly-dole;
His pants were baggy at the knees, his hat was quite
a freak,
His whiskers wild, his weirdly-mottled necktie just
a shriek.
Beside him strode a hefty bloke with broad and
arching chest—
A bold, imposing presence, of good looks and style
possessed,
His bearing unimpeachable, his manners hard to
beat;
He wore good clothes, his faultless pants were
pressed in creases neat,
And, as they passed along the street, this paragon
inclined
His well-bred head that he might hear the other
speak his mind.
And who was that ? The tall, young man, who —oh,
the bald, old lad ?
What? Yes, oh, yes, that’s him, all right—just like
his picture, sad
And serious all day long, as though he felt that was
the way
He really ought to look, because he takes the nation’s-
pay
And has his name, throughout the year, in every
daily sheet,
While all the Truly Thoughtful strew bouquets
around his feet.
Hey, what—that big, bold, brainy chap? Lie was
the one, of course,
AA ho lifted poor old Baldy up and put him on his
horse.
He made the old boy what he seems before the
public gaze
By toiling through long, weary nights and plodding,
patient days ;
The man Who'wields a slashing pen in his far-
reaching fist
To make the little great—why, yes, John Smith, the
journalist.!
VICTORIA.
O. L.

Government Notices.
ROYAL AUSTRALIAN NAVAL BRIGADE
(Naval Guard Section).
VOLUNTEERS are invited for Naval
Guard Duty on wharves and ships
alongside same. Applicants must be British
subjects, medically fit for the work re-
quired, between 20 and 50 years of age, and
must have had previous service in the Naval
or Military Forces of Great Britain or its
Dependencies. Service will be in Australia
until the termination of the Proclamation
calling out the Citizen Forces. The rate of
pay will be ss. per day for seven days per
week to able seamen. Married men or men
the sole support of a widowed mother will be
granted separation allowance: (a) for wife,
and (6) for each child under 16 years of
age. Uniform, rations and quarters are
supplied free.
For further particulars apply to
The District Naval Officer,
Naval Staff Office,
Rushcutters Bay.
