Somewhere— A Long Time Ago—
We read a pretty little story
About 'two sweethearts who
planted two acorns
Close together—
And the young people were
separated—or one died
(It’s a long time since we read
the story) ;
But the acorns became oak
trees,
Large ones, with wide, spread-
ing branches,
That twined about each other—
Like sweethearts—
And one tree could not be cut
down
Without destroying the other.
And we in the industry are like
that—■
We’re mutually interdependent.
One end of the business
Is so wrapped about the other
That its success or failure
Depends on the success or
failure of the other.
Imagine a Film industry
Without Producers and Dis-
tributors —•
Or without Exhibitors!
It couldn’t ~be.
So let’s “pull” together—
And icin together—
How else can we serve you ?
PARAMOUNT PICTURES.

POINTS
FORCE OF HABIT.
WILSON : “I’m right glad to see you, hoys.”
WILLIAM MORRIS, ETC. : "Before you start,
Woodrow —before you start., call your
dog off and muzzle him.”
Pearce told a questioner in the Senate
last week that it was quite true —wounded
officers returned to Australia could only get
back by re-enlisting as privates; and he re-
peated that this rule was made at the
request of General Birdwood. This paper
never wished Birdwood any harm before;
but it now longs to hear that he has got a
"Blighty,” and has been informed that, if
be wants to return to the Front, he can only
do it by enlisting as a private. What is
sauce f*»r the goose is sauce for the other
Birdie.
A fragment from the Westralian Income-
tax Bill debate (in W.A. the income-tax ex-
emption for married men is now down to
£156 p.a.) :
Mr. Collier, leader of the Opposition, maintained
that no married man earning less than £2OO a year
was in a position to pay any taxation.
It is true, and it is also true that no married
man on less than £2OO a year is in a position
t 6 owe £670 of State public debt, much of
ir unprofitable, for himself and wife and
children. But he does it. largely
because he has been living in a fool's para-
dise. and never thought of any future. Now
lie has to put up with a bill which asks no
questions and has no bowels of compassion.
A great May Day celebration was held by
the Bolsheviks in Russia to “celebrate the
victory over capitalism,” and seeing that
hams have been costing £5O each in I’etro-
grad and eggs six bob, the victory seems
fairly complete.
Australian industries keep dropping for
lack of something which we should have
learned to make ourselves. The Sydney
match factory closed recently. The glass
trade is closing by instalments. The build-
WILL-O’-THE-WISP.
ing of new railway rolling-stock is almost
defunct. The shipbuilding trade died a
while ago, though some efforts are being
made at resurrection. It is civilisation that
is passing away for want of the abundant
local product that is most essential in a
long war—industrial self-reliance. If the
war lasts three years longer it may be time
for the Australian citizen to paint himself
brown and take to the woods with “Binghi”
on his visiting-card.
Nothing better illustrates the passing out
of the old military caste in Britain than the
quiet dropping of that dear old phrase, “of
no military importance,” as applied to air
raids. Hundreds of non-combatants had
been killed by bombs from the air before the
British official reports abandoned the theory
that such things were “of no military im-
portance.” Of course they w T ere German
attacks on the morale of the enemy; and
the morale of a people at war is a matter
of the very first military importance. But
it was a dreadful admission for the British
or any other military person to have to
make —that anything the mere civilian might
do or say or think could possibly be of any
military consequence.
During the first three weeks of May the
United States launched new wooden ships
at the rate of one per day. And Australia
hardly launches one wooden leg per week.
The timber of these breathless Yankee ships
isn’t too well seasoned, and even the keels
are sometimes only Oregon pine. But the
wages of the builders are enormous even if
their hours are long; and the pay of the
crew is at record rates, because sailors are
scarce; and the freights charged for the
run from Australia go as high as £6O per
ton for some lines," so the concern may cover
its cost and more in a single voyage. One
way and another we pay the freights and we
don't get the high-priced jobs. Something
like a large,, damp, dead, invisible octopus
is deposited on the chest of our shipbuilding
industry whenever it threatens to get a good
start and make 11s independent of the great
capitalist across the sea. And the business
is done so silently that we don’t even know
for certain who deposits the octopus.
If there should be no Melbourne Cup this
year, all by reason of the borrowing and
importing craze, a number of people will
realise that politics are not the dreary,
academic subject they supposed. And it
isn't impossible: the Chief Commissioner of
X. S. Wales says the abolition of all travel-
ling for pleasure may become necessary
through sheer lack of rolling stock. Hitherto
it has been customary to import the vehicles,
or else a lot of the materials to build the
vehicles, and the loan to pay for them.
Now these imports have all practically
stopped. As the old vehicles have been care-
fully nursed and repaired, the Ma State has
only had to knock off about a, quarter of the
mileage run, but already many passengers
who used to sit comfortably are standing by
reason of the importing habit, and haven’t
the least idea why they are standing. If
the war lasts a while longer, and the coun-
try doesn't get to work very hard, the last
surviving train may not be able to carry
enough passengers to make it worth while
having a Cup, for the Victorian railways are
very little better off than Ma’s.
It is a common saying that nothing is
gained by perpetually nagging about the
awful extravagances of Australia’s past—-
that the past is dead and should be buried ;
that old follies are as little to the point as
old ancestors. Which is true enough in a
way, but it isn’t
much of a way.
The skeleton of the
dead loan has to be
kept on a chair at
the dinner-table with
its bill in its hand
as a reminder, else
the old racket will
be kept up and new
skeletons will accu-
mulate. Darius
allegedly hired a
man to shout to
him every day at
diuner that he had
an unsettled ac-
count with the
Athenians; and it
probably did
Darius good, and
prevented him put-
ting on flesh. This
country wants an
enactment that a
warning against
borrowing should
be uttered before
service at church,
before the guests
sit down at public
dinners and at the
rising of the cur-
tain at theatres.
Australia has one
Past, and it can’t afford any more Pasts like
it. Too many Pasts lead to Sheol, which is
a dead-end full of imperishable rats.
Thus the Rev. W. P. Stewart while the
Bathurst War Service Committee was lately
discussing the proposed internment of a
local German :—■
He hated Germans, but he considered it was not
Christian spirit to penalise that family. A German
had placed a convincing argument before him re-
cently, when he reminded him that, if he waivnot
to be regarded as an Australian, his naturalisation
certificate might as well be regarded as a “scrap of
paper.”
No doubt. Suppose, though, that years back a
German got a naturalisation certificate with
the intention of being a good Australian, but
changed his mind as soon as the Kaiser
called? Or suppose a German got a certifi-
cate with the set object of hiding his in-
tended treachery? In these eases would the
“ticket” be a sacred document which we
ought to honor, or merely a bit of evidence
on which to build a charge of treason?
Australia couldn’t build steel ships,
Hughes said, because it didn’t have all the
proper and necessary materials. The
U.S.A. haven’t got all the proper and neces-
sary materials for building wooden ships,
either; nevertheless they are being launched
there at the rate, recently, of one a day.
For practical purposes Australia is the only
country in the world with the ideal material
for wooden ships’ keels; hut, though iron-
bark legs are being sent away as fast as
space can he found for them on America-
bound boats, Uncle Sam is making a do with
substitutes —even going to the length of
bracing big slabs of Oregon together for
the job. What Sam thinks it is worth to
get ships built quickly may be judged by the
fact that the ironbark logs are paid for
by the cubic foot —at a price which runs up
to £6O f.o.b. for a log. Sam knows he will
get it ail back from us on the ship’s first
round trip.
Juddspierre might be a good name for the
man who seems to have collared the N. S.
Wales Labor machine. Not that Judd has
the commanding presence of Robespierre, and
he isn't dead like the “sea-green Incorrupti-

ble,” but they were both survivals in a way
and culminations of a sort. The leaders of
the French Revolution fell one by one, the
more moderate men with constructive poli-
cies being shoved out, till Robespierre re-
mained. In like manner Judd remains after
better men have got the sack, and, though he

lutions, and Judds generally.
From the Gallery :
Willie Watt is showing impatience at the Opposi-
tion’s tendency to talk, talk, talk on the adjourn-
ment. But Willie well knows that half of grievance
time is now occupied by protests about the Censor-
ship ; and it is futile to merely scowl and promise
inquiry when assailed with evidence of the most
senseless suppressions. Scowls only instigate bitter-
ness and language, and the alleged inquiry usually
results in an apology without any promise of reform.
If that is all that Watt is going to do, the Opposition
oughtn’t to let Watt g'et home even for breakfast.
“0.L.”: Bert Cook, chief Parliamentary
reporter on the Melbourne Herald, has been
given the job of publicity officer in the
Prime Minister’s Department. The Federal
Government is a perfect whale on the prin-
ciple of preference to soldiers. It is pre-
pared to make three meals a day of it.
Dr. Maloney told the North Melbourne
branch of the A.L.P. that “lie knew that
bribery and corruption of the foulest kind
were taking place to-day in Australian poli-
tics, but he could not prove it in a court of
law.” As a remedy he advised the adoption
of the initiative, the referendum and the re-
call. But this paper recollects that
Malouey’s leader, in Oct., ’l4. promised a
Protective tariff and the initiative in the first
session of the new Parliament , and it has so
far discovered no evidence that either Dr.
Maloney or any other Labor member tried to
force his leader to keep his word. Labor
then had a strong majority in both Houses —
which was possibly why it wasn't keen on
passing measures that would take power
from the politicians and give it to their
employers, the people.
DECENTRALISATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Mr. Holman mentioned, too, that the National
party was particularly anxious that an effective sys-
tem of decentralisation should be carried into effect,
iff order to prevent congestion in the city. Mr.
Holman might have told his audience that' it was
the much-despised Labor party, of which he was then
a member, that was responsible for the only genuine
attempt to bring about that much-desired end.” ,
—-V.,S. IT. Labor-leader J. Storey.
Out on their claim! Would these decentralise'?
It is our job. We started long ago.
The crowded cities fostered in our eyes;
We saw the torrent life about them flow ;
We said within our hearts “Shall these things be?
Give us more ports that lead to open sea!"
Long did we toil to find the people room.
North, south and west, behold the thing we
wrought:
The wider harvests that we taught to bloom,
The crowded peoples out of bondage brought !
Imagination, striving, almost fails
To grasp the thing we did for New South Wales.
Observe those twenty cities on the coast,
The linking railways filling them with trade.
Where Sydney stood alone, their only boasr.
The single port a century had mude!
Never forget we opened ull their eyes
When thus we started to decentralise.
What’s that? You cannot find them on the map.
Sydney is still alone and growing fast.
Well, that's the folly of the other chap
Who stays in office now our chance is past.
But we began it; yes, indeed we did,
Although obscured, our triumph is not hid.
We did not build the cities? Well, not quite,
There were few votes around where they should be:
But I remember someone talked one night
Of other ports upon the outer sea.
That was the start. Some twenty years ago
1 heard his liquid Labor accents flow.
Talk’s cheap ? Make no mistake upon that score.
Talk is expensive, and expansive, too.
“Decentralise!” was a great cry of yore
As indicating what we meant to do.
If we forgot it when we came to power,
Why should the other fellow pluck our flower ?
But, really, when it comes to bedrock fact,
What is this talk of open ports about?
It makes good noise, but no one wants to act
In any sort of fashion that might flout
The Sydney crowd who, holding still the reins,
Sit tight and gather in their solid gains.
The public suffers. What is that to us ?
The State is losing force for lack of room.
Well, let it lose! Why should we make a fuss?
The smells of Ultimo are all in bloom—
Smells and a Labor vote. Still, we protest:
Decentralising, we have done the best.
X.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
DON'T TINKER
LET
MICK SIMMONS
DO THE JOB
PROPERLY
The Federal Cabinet last week cut down
the number of racing days in the Commonwealth
from 2419 to 1862. In N. S. Wales 716 days will
be devoted to horse during the next 12 months. As
this is at the rate of 14 days per week and a dozen
over there ought to be enough to go round. What
the Government is trying to get at in connection
with the business is a mystery. But apart from
this the reductions made in and around Sydney are
on the right lines, though the ukase might have
gone further and done better. The non-proprietary
chibs which race at ltandwick are very properly not
interfered with. Proprietary suburban clubs will have
27 tdays instead of 31, and the pony outfits have
32'instead of 48. There will be 18 days for trotting
instead of 20 in the Metropolitan area and 48 trots
in the country. One of these days somebody will
examine this racing business with a view to getting
down to the bedrock purpose for which racing goes
on and the justification many clubs have for exist-
ence. Meanwhile the best that, can be said of the
latest curtailment is that, being devoid of any
principle, it is mostly a sham, and that where it is
not this it merely represents a fussy application of
rule-of-thumb.
» * • •
The steeplechase meeting at the end of
this week will be the last A.J.C. gathering of the
racing year; and next season Ike will do no more busi-
ness there at place or concession prices. Next
season’s licenses will be issued only for straight-out
betting. The object is, of course, to divert money
to the totalisator. There can be no doubt that
elimination of the place fielder was long overdue.
The odds he gave were shockingly cramped, and as
in the main he did business with small, nervous
punters, he could play his game with impunity. Yet
this page doubts whether the place-bettor who patron-
ises the machine instead of Ike will have any more
small change at the end of the proceedings—at any
rate, so long as the tote distribution is on the 60,
20, 20 basis. Though a rough ’un may arrive and
give him a long price now and again, the general
result of punting on this plan must be ruinous.
The concession bet is, of course, place-betting in
another form. It was open to far less objection
than the other method, and being mainly carried on
by two or three bold fielders was to some extent a
protection against the other crowd, who had to com-
pete with it. Whether its prohibition will send
much money to the machine remains to be seen,
though the writer has his doubts. The change in
both cases will mainly be to the advantage of
totalisator proprietaries'. A straight-out tote and
HAROLD BORDER
N.S.W. Rugby
League star.
another with a single place dividend would be to the
advantage of the public ; and until the public gets
it, as this paper has all along pointed out, the tote
might as well be in the air.
* * * *
The four-year-old Charlemagne 11. mare
Miss Felicia is a smart sort, but her bacjters are
wondering how long the hoodoo has arranged to
stay. A couple of months ago they saw themselves
just snuffed out at Rosehill. Then they went for a
recovery in Tattersall’s Stakes at Randwick, but
didn’t even get a start owing to Miss F. unshipping
McLachlan and refusing to face the barrier. This
left them two down and one to play at Warwick
Farm, and they came at her again for the Maiden
with solid wads. Myles Connell got her through the
slips quickly enough, but half-way up the straight
both of them went within a whisker of being tossed
over the rails through another animal quickly cross-
ing in front. That left Miss Felicia three down.
P. O’Sullivan, who did the crossing, got six months’
holiday immediately afterwards, the horse he rode
got second money, and backers thereof got second
div.—all of which seems a cock-eyed arrangement
and very lenient punishment for doing a fearfully
dangerous thing. And Miss Felicia is still a maiden.
Nine sets of “tips” were published in Syd-
ney for the Warwick Farmland, on the whole, they
were not a bad lot qf tips either. But not one of
the perpetrators gave the three-year-old gelding
Colonel Knut a hope in the Maiden. Still, this didn’t
matter to the Colonel. He took charge of the ad-
vance inside the distance and hit the line half a
dozen lengths ahead of the nearest support. Where-
upon the fielders gave three cheers for what to most
of them was a skinner, and got ready to do business
on the next. The form shown by this nag was of
the kind which should induce the great community
of punters to have a word with themselves pri-
vately, with a view to discovering how many chances
they have. Colonel Knut had run three times pre-
viously during the month. He finished sixth at a
country meeting, was almost last at Rosehill, and
was actually last in Tattersall’s Stakes at Rand-
wick. Then came Warwick Farm. He is con-
spicuously good-looking, in spite of his numerous
failures.
* * * *
When Old King Coal parted with £640
seven years ago for a Positano colt he probably had
visions' of a Derby. He christened the animal Prince
Positano, and never saw his money again. After
running astern as a two-year-old the nag was given
several years’ holiday, and then tried on registered
and unregistered courses, on the flat and over hurdles.
He did no better. Last Saturday he was given a
trip in the hurdles at Warwick Farm and ran off
after going half a mile. But he may do something
yet—there are still a few cabs left to pull.
* * * *
The V.R.C. Birthday meeting, due at
Flemington next Saturday anti Monday, will have a
hurdle and steeplechase in each day’s programme,
and some of the best of the local jumpers may be
seen out. The talkcd-of Will Comfort is entered
for the hurdle race on the first day, whilst several
of the tried performers with National pretensions are
down for both events and will certainly be tested
in one or other of them. The jumpers have only
another six weeks in which to get fully wound up
for the big events at the Flemington mid-winter
meeting. If they are not pretty nearly fit for the
Birthday meeting their chances of doing a fast
three miles in early July won’t be worth considering.
In the sprints on the flat punters will probably lean
to the English horses, and back the winner if they
spot the right one. The dull going Ought to suit
some of the imported nags that were off-color during
the summer.
* * * *
There are not many of his breed racing, but
the parentage of Dr. James, winner of the steeple-
chase at Moonee Valley last week, is respectable
enough on paper for anything. He is by Renown
from a grand-daughter of Sunrise. Renown was a
top-class racehorse in Maoriland, the best begotten
by Dreadnought for the lately-dead J. D. Ormond.
At three years old Renown took half a dozen of the
best races over yonder, including a couple of Derbies
and two Guineas. He was ridden in all his races by
F. Davis, now the trainer of Desert Gold. If this
’chaser has anything like the courage of ljis sire he
will be often heard of.
The winning of the Gctodwood Handicap
(8.A.) by the local horse Pistblarie has revived the
question whether it ought not to be “Pistolaire.”
The A.J.C. somehow misprinted it that way when
the name was sent over to Sydney for registration
in 1915; and it sounds better French. However,
Whitington, secretary of the S.A.J.C., says there’s
no French about it. A glance at the breeding, Pistol-
Marie Hall, makes it clear how the last four letters
should run.
* * * *
lii the prayer that happens in a perfunc-
tory manner at both ends of every meeting of the
House of Reps., the Lord is requested to guide hon.
members in their “consultations.” The solemn Barn-
ford has asked that the word “deliberations” shall
be substituted, as the other one “has a flavor ini
Australia not' associated with prayer,” which fact*
Bamford seems to think may stop Providence doing
its best for members.
* * * *
Recently there was some fruitless discus-
sion in the Federal Parliament on the Government’s
hypocritical dealing with Tattersall in Tasmania. The
sweep business such as Tatt. conducts is illegalised
in all the larger States of the Commonwealth, and
the Federal Government loses a considerable amount
of postal revenue by putting the concern on the
prohibited list. Thousands of individual applica-
tions that would be mailed to an unprohibited Tatt.’s
are condensed into scores of letters from' lawless
unmolested agencies which forward many applica-
tions under one cover. From this point of view
alone the Postal Department’s action against the
lottery is a sham pretence of Moral Endeavor. But
the Government has now stultified its anti-gambling
action by proposing to tax the prizes obtained from
the lottery it has declared illegal. Political hypoc-
risy, as a rule, either makes arrogant pretence of
virtue or is apologetically shamefaced, but a tax on
the Tatt. prizes collected by a Government which
refuses postal countenance to the concern, and
threatens punishment to its agents, is hypocrisy cyni-
cal and shameless. The Government might as
honestly declare horse-racing illegal in order to
impose an extra-special tax on prize-winnings.
The question naturally arises: Why does the power
which decides to profit by a private gambling
business not wipe it out and establish a Commonwealth
Lottery in its place? The want that Tatt. supplies
and derives a large income from would be better
supplied by the Federation, now that the Govern-
ment has decided to turn this “gambling evil” to
financial account. No intelligent citizen commends
the principle of State lotteries, any more than
he advocates mining speculation for sneculation’s
sake. But a duly controlled and restricted vent
for the ineradicable gambling passion is good
enough for any Government to turn an honest two-
pence by. It is an utterly dishonest farthing that
Hughes and Co. are going to turn.
* * * *
“Mujik” : Has anyone ever reckoned that
the time was ripe to make a change in the system
under which colors are worn by jockeys in Australia?
It is a confusing system, anyway. When compara-
tively few owners were racing there was no doubt a
certain convenience in allowing each one to select
a livery and stick to it. With multitudinous owners
in the game there is nothing to be said for the
system, which results in a few men monopolising
the most distinctive millinery and the others having
to adopt all sorts of queer combinations—having even
to get down to the armband, which at a dis-
tance or in a crowded finish is about as distinctive
as a postage-stamp on the animal’s tail. The whole
business should be up-ended and the colors to be
worn in every race allotted by the Club in the same
way as the weight or the place at the barrier. There
are six striking colors—white, black, red, blue,
yellow and green. Without going into any of the
variations it is possible by using these half-dozen
straight out or in combinations of contrasting hoops
and stripes for jackets, and by fixing the incidental
caps in sharp contrast, to make provision for the
largest fields capable of being started at Randwick
or Flemington. The colors could be allotted by
ballot. Owners would lose nothing. The public
would gain immensely by the greater ease with
which the race could be followed. Some of the
colors now sported—weird yells in gamboge, orange,
lilac, puce and mauve —are horribly confusing even
when inspected through a powerful field-glass. Most
people watch the riot of color in complete ignorance
of what is happening. If there is any objection to
simplifying the spectacle I want to know what it is.
* * * *
Sydney baseball has a whirlwind in it in
a hefty nine from St. George. This season they
have made mincemeat of Petersham (last year’s
premiers), scoring 20 runs to 6, and on Saturday
they showed some little men of Nippon a point or
two —11 runs to seven. The nine look more than
likely to get away with the premiership.
* * * *
The casualties at Sydney Hippodrome
where Dave Smith controls the barrage have been
numerous during the week, but recoveries have aver-
aged 100 per cent. Operating on Fred Fitzsimmons
the other evening Sid Godfrey gave a tasty demon-
stration of how to administer a substitute for chloro-
form, which in the event of supplies running short
should be handy to dentists, beauty specialists and
others. The demonstration was limited to 10 sessions,
but Godfrey had finished in the fourth. He carries
too much wallop for Fitz. An important item on
the menu for Monday was a collision between Harry
Stone and Harry Green. The local product sprang
into, the fray with great gladness and two willing
hands. But Stone is a wily old bird, and, stemming
back the devastation, totted up points which gave
him the verdict. If they renew hostilities often
enough one of them will get hurt.
* * * *
There was enough ginger in Sydney League
football on Saturday to provide heavy casualties. In
the conflict between Annandale and N. Sydney five
Annandalcs had to be sent to dock for repairs. In
the majority of other matches the clearing stations
had a busy day. Clearly tne League will have to
use its heaviest boot if the game is to be kept above
the level of a free fight. The main buzz of the
afternoon was between South Sydney and Balmain,
and over it 12,000 barraclcers howled with glee.
The Southrons won by 13 points to 2. As it was
Balmain's second defeat in two years and only the
fifth calamity in 39 matches during four years, the
episode was something to take notice of.
* * * *
Wally Messenger (Eastern Suburbs) is
keeping the family name to the front in Sydney
League football. This season he has scored all nine
goals for the club in three matches. On Saturday
lie had four goals on the score-sheet against Glebe
before he left the field for repairs.
* * * *
R. Spears, a racing bikist from N. S.
Wales, is beginning to make the followers of the
decayed sport in U.S.A. watch his movements with
interest. The other day at Newark, N.J., he won
two out of the three open events, decisively licking
Spencer, the Canadian crack and present holder of
the American Championship.
* * * *
“Wart”: Caught this eel at Longford
(Tas.) in 1908. Length, sft.; girth, 21in; weight,
341 b. You can have a look at him any time you
like when you’re in Launceston Museum.
THE PUNTING SHELLBACK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His room, is a dazzling peep-show, a sight for the
gods to see,
Plastered with sporting pictures, old prints from
the Referee.
Pasted in bunk and on bulkhead, jockeys and nags
without end,
Outshining the brush-and-comb bag, the gift of a
lady friend.
First seeing the light at Hobart, at fourteen going
to sea,
Master or mate at the whim of\ Fate, and now he is
fifty-three !
Grizzled and grey, yet holding no brief for the word
“regret,"
Smiling, he’ll say in a casual way: “I’ll laugh at
the bookies yet.”
Only last night, as we paced the bridge, I said to
the second mate
“You’ve followed the sea for a long time now —and
what do you think of Fate ?”
“Huh! Fate,” he answered, “gets blamed by
fools who’ve fell in the mire and stuck,
Rotten with funk, and lacking the spunk to struggle
and try their luck !
And ‘luck’ is the grandest word we’ve got, a word
with a hopeful lilt,
A word full true, as the Binghi knew when his first
dug-out he built.
Yes! luck's all right, if you give it a chance, by
keeping full head of steam.
For the bloke that ‘greets’ would flatten the sheets
with the wind abaft the beam.
“I’ve followed the gee-gees constant, as long as I’ve
followed the sea,
And I’m certain to make a big pot soon ; then it’s
‘go on the land’ for me;
And when that happens, in sealskin gear my missus
. I’ll sure array,
And Freddy, my youngest boy, I’ll send to college
at Jervis Bay.
I’ve only two; but the oldest lad—a brainy young
kid is Jim—-
I’Vn thinking somehow I hardly know just yet what
to do with him.
Hey! Watch your steering there, Svensen, lad!
don’t think you’re driving a plough!
You’re making a wake like a shickered snake, and —
what was I saying just now?
“Oh, Jim? Yes, Jimmy, 1 want him to graft where
brains have a chance to shine.
I'm anxious to .make him a bookie, or a nob in
the racing line.
With Navy and Turf represented, and living ashore
at my ease,
I’ll be content to cast off the lines whenever the
Lord decrees.
I stood once to win ten thousand, and not a bean
off did I lay.
A leg-in I’d got. I wanted the lot, so trusted to
luck all the way.
I’d Merriwee backed with Voyou—the Derby and
Melbourne Cup.
The double looked sweet; but Voyou was beat,
because the wrong jockey was up !
“The sea is a rotten calling. Discomfort and paltry
screw—
The owners they collar, the divvies, and it’s them
run the horses, too ;
In touch with the turf and its glamor, while we are
running their ships—
I’ve sweated for B —n and for Y —11; but neither
gave me any tips.
But I tell you my time is coming”... .He paused—
and I gave him the course,
And left him there in the cool night air to worship
his one god: Horse !
An optimist down to his toenails, he’d laugh in
the face of Fate —
A real good sort and a genuine sport, this punter,
our second mate!
His room is a dazzling peep-show. His life it is
one big bet.
He laid long odds on a sailor’s life, and the favorite’s
running yet.
Hopeful and still mojre hopeful, and generous to a
fault,
l'ou shellbacks who read these verses know the true
old Hobart salt.
Just peep in his room as you’re passing and glance
at the littered floor
Where Arrow and Tout lie scattered, with records of
races of yore.
Let’s hope that he makes his fortune — he’d punt with
his latest breath,
And “do” his shirt on the final spurt with the black
bookmaker Death !

EIGHTY-FOBRTH ANNUAL REPORT of the Directors of the Bank of Australasia,
With the Accounts for the Half-vear to 15th October, 1917.
Presented to the Proprietors at the Annual General Meeting, held on Thursday, ith April, 1918.
REPORT.
The Directors submit to the Proprietors the Balance-Sheet as at 15th October last, with the
Profit Account for the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, tor
British and Australasian rates and taxes, and after making provision for bad and doubtful debts ana
other contingencies, the net profit for the half-year amounted to £200,440 13s. 6d.
To this sum must be added £122,868 13s. 4d. brought forward from the previous half-year, making
a total of £323,309 6s. 10d., out of which the Directors have declared a dividend for the half-year
at the rate of 14 per cent, per annum, or £2 16s. per share, and a Bonus at the rate of 3 per cent,
per annum, or 12s. per share, together £3 Bs. per share, free of income tax. .
The Dividend and Bonus will absorb .£170,000, and the Directors have further appropriated
£30,000 to the Reserve Fund. There will then remain £123,309 6s. lOd. to be carried forward to the
mXt and Bonus will be payable in London and in Australasia oil the sth April.
The Directors retiring by rotation are: Horace Peel, (Esq., Tom Richard Johnson, Esq., and the
Rt. Hon. Viscount Midleton, K.P., who, being eligible, offer themselves for re-election.
The Proprietors will be asked to approve the election, as a Director, of Mr. Harold Nelson, in
place of Sir E. Montague Nelson, K.C.M.G., resigned.
The Auditors, Messrs. Welton and Bond, have merged their business with that of Messrs. Deloitte,
Plender, Griffiths and Co., and the latter firm offer themselves for election as Auditors.
Since the date of the last report the agencies at Coburg (Melbourne) and Kiewa, Victoria, and
the branch at Rosedale, Victoria, have been closed.
4 Threadneedle Street, London, 25th March, 1918. T. R. JOHNSON, Chairman.

We have examined the cash and securities in London, and the London books, and have verified the
transfers from the several branches in the Corpmonwealth and New Zealand, and we beg to report that,
in our opinion, the foregoing is a full and fair balance-sheet of the Bank, and that it exhibits a true

at market price or under.

There is nothing exciting about this little
shipping co.. whose fleet serves the fertile
but drowsy South Coast. However, it has
been doing a little better of late. The profits
in the table are shown after providing for
depreciation, and whether that allowance
has been more than ample The Bulletin
has no means of ascertaining. In addition
to what has been written off property the
co. has been building up a “Repairs, public
risk and contingency account,” which has
risen like this: 1916, £3500; 1917, £0000;
ICIB, £ll,OOO. Possibly some of that £7500
earmarked in two years is not a set-off
against current wear and tear, in which case
the profits are better than the table shows.
The balance-sheet figures have been moving
much more satisfactorily during the last
few years:—
1905. 1915. 1918.
Liabilities £1,165 £11,413 £7,610
Ship property .. . . 40,393 78,950 54,950
Other property .. .. 705 1,056 1,400
Public securities, cash, etc. 42,235 1,873 29,152
Book debts 3,628 6,756 9,548
Other assets.. .. . . 1,961 4,799 5,542
At March, 1905, the co. had a cash surplus
of £44,098 over liabilities. In 1915 liabilities
exceeded liquid assets by £2784, the co. hav-
ing meantime almost doubled the book value
of its ship property. Nevertheless, with this
augmented fleet earnings were very little
more than they had been 10 years previously.
In the past three years the cash position has
improved by £33,800, and apparently the
little Ilillmeads, which had been purchased
second-hand .10 years ago, is the only vessel
disposed of. Any further comparison is
dependent on the extent to which shipping
property has depreciated.
Cronulla Hotel, Ltd. (N.S.W.) ;

1917.. 625 5 „ „ 738 834
1918.. 969 5 „ „ 740 229 1063
This little co., like the Hotel Pacific at
Manly, went into the pub business when
everything in the garden was blooming. The
seaside resort a score or so miles south of
Sydney was expanding rapidly and the holi-
day-maker with money to splash was becom-
ing more numerous every season. But the
co. had bad luck from the beginning. Early
in 1913 a cyclone hit the premises, necessi-
tating an outlay of £9OO. Next year the war
began; the following year bar-trading was
restricted by six-o’clock legislation; and all
the time the prices of foodstuffs and liquors
have been soaring. Capital is £14,791, there
is a mortgage of £6OOO. and £603 is owing
to sundry creditors. Of the assets, £19,112
represents property and goodwill. How much
of the total is goodwill The Bulletin does
not know; and certainly the intangible asset
isn’t worth much under present conditions.
Furniture, plant and odds and ends are
included at £2682; there is £1067 of stock
and £335 of cash. The present, therefore, is
not very cheerful. And the future is quite
uncertain. Profits are dependent on so many
things that it is no use even attempting to
guess.

This little co. first switched on the current
in the. nor’-western N. S. Wales town in
1911, and though even now there are only
252 consumers it has paid divs. from the
jump. Last year the Moree summer was
made more tolerable by the installation of
a cold-storage plant, and that meant in-
creased earnings. The profits and reserves
above, by the way, include additions to
depreciation and maintenance accounts, now
totalling £2853. What is a fair thing to
allow for future replacements The Bulletin
does not know, but the directors state that
“ample provision has been made for excep-
tional expenditure.” Capital is £13.000, the
co. owes £1960, and there are liquid assets
totalling £4025.
* * * *
The Kaiser, with the help of the Free-
traders. gave S. Australia’s best revenue-
producing railway line a severe set back on
the declaration of war in 1914. For 1913,
which was the best year in the history of
Broken Hill as far as output of ore is con-
cerned. 856,000 tons were despatched. In
1914 the figures dropped to 674,000 tons, and
have been steadily falling ever since; 1915,
512.000 tons; 1916, 455,000 tons; last year,
355.000 tons. The greatest quantity ever
forwarded was in June. 1914 —over 24,000
tons. Other contributing causes for the
decline in 1916 and 1917 were strikes of a
month and six weeks respectively; but the
pinch being felt now is the dwindling amount
of shipping that is available. Thousands
of tons of ore are on wharves and paddocked
at Port Pirie, some of it there ever since
1914 —mostly parcels intended for the place
where nearly all the Broken Hill ores went
before the mad dog got off the chain.

Chaplain-colonel Larry Rentoul, of Vic-
toria, is taking an earnest and active part in
the preparations for the Waacs’ official
d#but, and perhaps may himself yet be seen
driving a tramcar in order to release some
willing and able, but family-hampered, citi-
zen desirous of getting to the Front.
“Plain Australian’s” suggestion :
The proposal for a “Ryan Thousand” Suggests a
plan to encourage recruiting. Let us give to these thou-
sands, not merely the name of their politician, but
the actual carcase of him, to take away and do what
they like with. Private individuals give native
bears, and bulldogs, and monkeys, and wallabies, and
iguanas, and other varied creatures to departing
soldiers, without bothering as to whether they are
ever brought back. Let Australia rise to the occasion
and give its politicians. They certainly eat more,
are less picturesque and more noisy, but these draw-
backs could doubtless be overcome by a resourceful
am l determined soldiery. If the first one was used
up, more could be sent. The prospect of really own-
ing a share in a politician would attract both sides
of opinion, and also a lot of people who haven’t
any political opinions but only sensitive ears. The
educational effect on those politicians who came back
would be incalculable, and if they didn’t come back
they could be spared in a noble cause. When in
the trenches they could be turned on to addressing
the Hun, and the army could rush the listening
enemy as soon as he got trying to follow the argu-
ments. The character of the Kaiser could be repre-
sented to the German Army in the same way as that
of a political opponent is dealt with, and the Boche
exhorted to strike —in the best Australian sense—
for real freedom. Whilst the politicians were away
Australia would have an opportunity of getting to
business—building ships, cleaning up the six sets of
State borrowers, organising taxation and expenditure
on a reasonable basis, starting some home-grown
industries, and otherwise getting a real strangle-hold
on German influences. The price is little enough to
pay.

IMPORTANT.
Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and ■ water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, craßseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free, 1/3. Free sample posted.

should read the British Austral-

vited to visit the British Australasian
Reading Room at the Offices, 115 High Ilol*

home papers, meet their friends, use the
telephone, and obtain information without
charge.

FIFTY-SECOND ANNUAL REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS
TO THE SHAREHOLDERS.

Since the last Report the agencies at Hyde Park and Willowie have been closed.
At the last meeting of Shareholders the hope was expressed that your Directors would be able to
submit to you the position of the Provident Fund, with a view to providing greater benefits to our
staff. The Directors have carefully considered the matter, and will submit proposals for your considera-
tion at a Special General Meeting to be held on Ist May.
During the past year 18 members of the bank’s staff have enlisted, making a total of 105, of whom,
we regret to say, 11 have been killed, six have been discharged as no longer fit for service five have
resigned from the Bank, leaving 83 now serving their country.
The General Annual Meeting will be held in the Board-room at the head office of the Bank King
William Street, Adelaide, on Wednesday, Ist May, 1918, at 12 noon, at which the Shareholders will be
called upon to elect two Directors in place of Howard Watson Lloyd, Esq., and James Richard Fowler
Esq., who retire by rotation, and, being eligible, offer themselves for re-election. ’
The Shareholders will have to elect two Auditors for the ensuing year, and to fix the remuneration of
the retiring Auditors.

A good deal of revenue might be raised if
the Government would tax the income of the
leading villain in the drama and the picture-
show. The ill-gotten affluence of that
bounder is disgusting.
“Fax”: Margot Lester, the handsome
pupil of Florrie Green, who danced with
grace at Melbourne King’s matinee last week,
is a daughter of Edwin Lester, the character
actor. There should be a bright professional
career ahead of Miss Margot. Mrs. Green
speaks of her as one of the brainiest and
most promising of the long procession of
Melbourne girls who have graduated for the
stage at her academy. The young dancer
has a theatrical pedigree, anyhow. Her
father is son of the late Edwin Kelly, an
old-time scholarly actor who was great in
certain parts.
“Jean Sibi”: Singer Philip Newbury,
"The Scarlet Trouba-
dours,” at Sydney
Playhouse.
Courtney Ford lays
the odds.
resurrected for a
few nights at Mel-
bourne Bijou, is a
souvenir of the past
in manner and de-
livery. The little
foot drawn back,
the podgy hand
over the heart, the
lips parted in the
loveliest pianissi-
mos of pleading—
all are there to re-
mind one that there
really was a time
when vocal gallan-
tries were select,
though ragtime
makes it seem so
very long ago.
Newbury’s voice is,
little impaired in
its higher reaches,
whilst his facial
resemblance to
Walter Kirby
grows mor„e alarra-
ins, though he is still two chins short.
Died of wounds in France, John Horace
Ormiston, late of the Mines Department,
Sydney. The dead soldier’s mother was con-
nected with the Sydney stage in years gone
by; she was a sister of the better-known
Kitty Corcoran, who became the wife of the
wealthy Jack Smith, politician and racing
man.
“Pick”: Circus-proprietor Jimmy Ashton,
recently found drowned at Dubbo (N.S.NV.).
was a real Australian in putting a square
deal before everything. One wild night in
the ’seventies, at Bibbenluke (N.S.W.), the
tent was blown to ribbons and a very sparse
audience had to be sent home. The show
was billed to play at Nimitybelle the follow-
ing night, and for the next three nights at
Cooma. Having fulfilled those engagements
Ashton travelled back over 58 miles and gave
a free performance at one-horse Bibbenluke.
He had heard in Cooma that Bibbenluke
reckoned it hadn’t been treated fairly.
When the mail left London Ethel Irving
was booked to appear in “The Prime Minis-
ter,” a new play by ’All Caine.
“75” : English theatrical papers speak of
one Joy Wattle, “the Australian girl,” and
mention that she has booked return dates
until 1921. Anybody ever heard of this
blossom ?
“Mrs. Warren’s Profession,” the first pro-
duction of which in VYark resulted in police-
court proceedings and the ultimate discom-
fiture of the authorities, has just been
revived there, with some of the original cast,
and has caused scarcely a ripple of protest.
Apparently the Yankee critics were too busy
condemning a new piece, “The Squab Farm.”
The girl who was almost a star was mad-
der than Hamlet about it. “Call that a
paper!” she sneered. “They won’t even give
me four lines when I’ve been out of the show
for a week with a cold. Yet they give a
stinking boa-constrictor a two-column block
and a write-up!” Moral: Don’t be an act-
ress ; be a boa-constrictor.
“Stargazer”: Demonstrations against a
play, a set of players, or aji individual actor
are rare occurrences in Australia. But
actors and managements haven’t the same
“The Million Dollar
Girl,” at Melbourne
Tivoli.
Lord Help-us, of
Nieman and Kennedy.
compunction. When

The Deputy Federal Commissioner of
Taxation writes regarding the Amusement
tax:—
Departmental tickets must be used for admission
to all entertainments at prices exceeding 6d. except
in the following cases:—
(a) Cases where the proprietor of the entertain-
tainment enters into a substantial bond ;
(b) Cases where exemption is granted in view of
the entertainment being held for educational, philan-
thropic, religious or charitable purposes.
Departmental tickets are issued to proprietors of
entertainments only on written application to . the
postmasters, and they must forward the tickets to
this office within 24 hours after the close of each
entertainment. The Regulations under the Act give
full particulars as to the requirements in this regard,
and the system in vogue provides a check against
irregularities. There is also an Inspectorial Staff
on the look-out for evasions.
Proprietors under bond are not required to issue
Departmental tickets, but are permitted to remit the
tax collected at the end of each week. These pro-
prietors are required to furnish an annual statement
certified to by a public accountant, and their books
are always liable to investigation, the same as those
of other proprietors.
Where a bond is accepted, a certificate is issued to
the proprietor, and a copy of this certificate must
be exhibited at each entrance to the place of enter-
tainment.
This is in reply to various country con-
tributors who have been unable to see how
the Government was protecting itself against
tax-dodgers.
Killed in France, Lance Sheil, an English
mummer who was first seen here in “The
Scarlet Pimpernel” and remained to marry
an Australian girl and appear in sundry
shows, including “On Our Selection” and
“While the Billy Boils.” He also “produced”
Beaumont Smith’s film version of “The Way-
backs.” Sheil enlisted in Australia and was
running shows behind the lines for some
time before he was sent into the trenches.
“H. 0.”; There is a Sydney man who has
made money out of the film censorship.
When a film is banned the gaudy posters,
daybills, etc., with which it had been in-
tended to point its lofty moral are kept in
bond and in due course must be destroyed.
This is not as easy
as it sounds, as the
stuff can’t conveni-
ently be hanged by
the neck or as-
phyxiated, and even
to cart it away
and burn it means
undue expense.
Many tons had ac-
cumulated when a
man offered to buy
it at a nominal
price and destroy
it. The offer was
accepted, and the
purchaser reduced
the highly colored
bills to confetti.

“Norbell”: Good old Grattan Riggs used
to perpetrate a Sinn Fein bellow-drama 25
years ago. “Shin Fane” was its phoneti-
cally-spelt label. I wonder how a revival
would go?
One of the victims of a recent London
air-raid was Mrs. Lena Guilbert Ford, who
wrote the words of the widely-chorused
“Keep the Home Fires Burning.”
“Motley”: Apropos late circus-man Ash-
ton, whose tragic exit is recorded. The first
noteworthy Australian circus was Maleom's,
in Sydney, and the first Ashton was the
earliest responsible man on the road. Hayes
was an early Melbourne manager. Then
came Noble, at the north-east top of Bourke-
street, before Parliament House was begun.
His circus was afterwards boarded up for
concerts as the Salle de Valentino. Rowe
came from San Francisco, and opened on
the corner of Lonsdale and Stephen streets,
where Coppin’s Olympic afterwards stood.
Burton’s National Circus began in 1851.
“0.K.”: Cannot’s imitation of Talleur
Andrews singing an impassioned love-song
—“You that I-er-woor-ship so”—is the best,
bit of musical mimicry staged here since
Albert Whelan imitated Henry Bracy in
“Take a Pair of Sparkling Eyes.” Cannot’s
throaty effort comes easier because he bur-
lesques the Andrews manner. Whelan was
true to the impassive original in all re-
spects, and his reproduction of Bracy’s faded
tenor warble would have deceived Henry’s
own dog.
“Fax”: The pioneer musical comedies—
“A Gaiety Girl,” “In Town,”-etc. —had the
scenes laid always in England and mostly
in London. The late George Edwardes, who
discovered them, kept them there. They
had three acts, as a rule. After a while the
three were reduced to two, one of them out-
side Britain. Nowadays there are oftener
three than two, and scenery and costumes
make amends for poverty of dialogue.
“Katinka,” which is coming to Melbourne
Majesty’s next week, has three acts and
scenes at Yalta (on the Black Sea), old
Stamboul and a Turkish cafd in Paris. In
fact, “Katinka” isn’t a real blood relation
of “Our Miss Gibbs” or “The Girl from
Kay’s.” She’s a foreign connection by mar-
riage, and has even been referred to as a
comic-opera.
“B. J.” : When one comes to think of it, it
was rather clever of Shakspeare to write
“Hamlet” more or less off his own bat, see-
ing that 16 (sixteen) human intellects were
employed to concoct “The Million Dollar
Girl.” All of the distinguished authors get
tiiexr names on the Tivoli programme. W : hat
I regard as unfair is that the names of the
ushers are omitted. The XYI authors
merely manufactured the revue, whereas the
ushers, with their unbounded enthusiasm and
discriminating applause, made it.
“Chevron”: George D. Treloar, son of a
Ballarat chemist whom Alfred Dampier
called to Melbourne to play Hamlet, has
found greater fame under the star-shell
than ever the footlights could have re-
flected. His amateur days were followed by
an association with Julius Knight and Oscar
Asche. Then he became Private Treloar
of the Artists’ Rifles, and later a captain in
the Coldstream Guards. Trench-mortar
officer on the Somme, he was in despatches
for gallantry at Martinpuich, Les Boeufs
and Courcelette, the last-named action being
the first occasion on which the whole Guards
Division charged as regiments in line. He
now holds both D.S.O. and M.C. Educated
at St. Pat’s College, he was a chorister at
St. Peter’s Church in his native town. His
mother, also his aunt and uncle—Lucy and
Sydney Devine—were thespians, too.
From London:—
At last a worth-while musical frivolity that is
not of Austrian origin. “The Lilac Domino” is its
name and it comes from U.S.A. The “book” is
commonplace and most of the dialogue tripey, but
the critics agree that the music, by Charles Cuvil-
lier, is equal to anything of the kind' heard here for
years. Some extra numbers, which are well spoken
of, were composed by Howard Carr (who married
Trixie Tracy, once a Melbourne chatterer for The
Bulletin), and he also conducts in a way that has
earned him bouquets from several papers. The
Pall Mall Gazette says: “lie has proved himself
one of the very best of our theatrical conductors, to
say nothing of his talent as a composer.”
THE EXORCISM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Hand in hand we sit alone,
Breast to breast we oit-times lie,
Like a Darby and a Joan—
Care and I,
Care and I.” — Bulletin Bard.
There’s one defence for stricken men
From subtle Care, and it is laughter.
Put on thy mask of Mourns then ;
Gibe all the day; the long night after
Pursue her with thy scoffing guile! *
Though often it may sorely grieve thee,
Deceive thyself a little. Smile!
’Tis better far than all the while
Have her deceive thee.
Deride her smoke-grey eyes, her face
Of ivory, her pensive meekness.
Let her not know thy feeble case,
Thou knowest now her fatal weakness.
Accept her, and besetting thee
Her face, as in a magic prism,
Will look from every plane, more dree
Each day ; but scoff, and she will flee
Thy exorcism. .
Oh, hush! Thy mistress is untrue.
A wanton she more lewd and foxy
Than Thais, Pliryne—all the crew ;
Than Jezebel or Samson’s doxy.
She pillows ’neath her breast thy head,
But when thou turnest to thy labors
Kuns slily to another’s bed.
She is the cast-off, be it said,
Of half thy neighbors!
victoria.
EDDYSON.

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly St lections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
There are plenty of reasons why grey hair
is not desirable, and plenty of reasons why
hair dyes should not be used. But, on the
other hand, there is no reason why you should
have grey hair if you do not want it. To turn
the hair back to a natural color is really a
very simple matter. One has’only*to get from
the chemist two ounces of concentrate of tam-
malite and mix it with three ounces of bay
rum. Apply to the hair with a small sponge
for a few nights and the greyness will gradu-
ally disappear. This liquid is not sticky or
greasy, and does not injure (lie hair in any
way. It has been used for generations with
most satisfactory results by those who have
known the formula.
Blackheads Fly Away.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into gen-
eral use in the boudoir. It is very simple,
harmless and pleasant. Drop a stymol tablet
obtained at the chemist’s, in a tumbler full of
hot water. After the effervescence Ims sub-
sided bathe the face in the liquid, using a
small sponge or soft cloth. In a few minutes
dry the face and the offensive blackheads will
come right off on the towel. Also the large
oily pores immediately close up and efface
themselves naturally. The greasiness dis-
appears and the skin is left smooth, soft and
cool. This simple treatment is then repeated
a few times at intervals of four or five days
to ensure the permanence of the result.
Emergency Complexions.
Even a complexion which is hopelessly dull
or greasy can be made beautiful in a moment
by a perfectly harmless home recipe. If you
have no cleminite in the house, get about an
ounce from your chemist, and add only
enough water to dissolve it. The process de-
fies detection, and the result is an immediate
appearance of velvety, youthful “bloom”
which every woman desires. The effect will
last all day under the most trying conditions,
indoors or out. and renders powdering quite
unnecessary. This simple substance is also
very good for the skin, and no possible harm
can come from its regular use.
Effectually Killing Superfluous Hair.
Many women know how to remove ugly
growths of superfluous hair temporarily, but
few know how to remove it permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered phemlnol may be
used. Get about an ounce from your chemist
and apply a little directly to the objection-
able hair. The purpose of the recommended
treatment is not merely to remove the super-
fluous hair instantly, but also to kill the hair
roots completely in a comparatively short
time.
How Nature Makes New Complexions.
It is well known that the human skin is
constantly undergoing a tearing down and
building up process. With advancing years or
waning vitality this tissue-change lags: the
lifeless, soiled surface skin stays on so long
that its owner gets a “poor complexion.”
Commonsense tells us this dead skin cannot
be enlivened or beautified by any cosmetic,
lotion or powder. The natural thing to do is
to actually remove the offensive skin—remove
the bad complexion. It has been found that
ordinary mercolized wax completely absorbs
the devitalised skin, in minute particles, so
gently, gradually, as to cause no inconveni-
ence. Mercolized wax, which any chemist can
supply, is put on at night like cold cream,
and washed off in the morning. If vou would
have a brilliantly beautiful and youthful com-
plexion, just use this simple treatment.
How to Have Thick and Pretty Hair.
Soaps and artificial shampoos ruin many
beautiful heads of hair. Few people know
that a teaspoonful of good stallax dissolved
in a cup of hot water has a natural aflinity
for the hair and makes the most delightful
shampoo imaginable. It leaves the hair bril-
liant, soft and wavy, cleanses the scalp com-
pletely, and greatly stimulates the hair-
growth. The only drawback is that stallax
seems rather expensive. It comes to the
chemist only in sealed quarter-pound pack-
ages. which retail at about four shillings.
However, as this is sufficient for 25 or 30
shampoos, it really works out very cheaply
in the end. [4

“Sow-and-Pigs”: When the big U-boat
wants to have a chat with its friends it gets
out two balloons, inflates them and sends
them aloft for 1000 ft. or more on a wire
paid out by a drum driven by an electric-
motor. One of the best American wireless
engineers thinks the sea-hogs can thus throw
a message 2000 miles, and, with ideal condi-
tions, 3000 or 4000 miles.
If the war could be won by phrases the
whole thing might be left to Lloyd George.
Recently before the National Council of
Free Churches he was referring to the offer
of the Kaiser to put Germany at the head of
a League of Nations to insure the peace of
the world. “There,” said he, “you have a
dagger wrapped in the Sermon on the
Mount!”
Charged at Collingwood (Vic.) Court
lately with failing to register as an alien, a
man whose father had fought against the
■Germans long ago pleaded that he was “only
a German by accident,” his mother being on
a visit to friends in Prussia when he was
born. It recalls the plaintive song of the
unlucky man, “I was born on a Friday when
my mother wasn’t at home.”
This is another view of the Ilun for the
benefit of the peace negotiators. Bul-
garia sold its soul, which isn’t large,
and its body, which isn’t washed so often as
it might be, and the reward is coming
quickly. It has become a rather somnolent
partner, being exhausted by three wars in
quick succession, so the Hun has asked for
prompt payment of the £50,000,000 which
lie allegedly advanced to finance Bulgaria’s
end of the trouble.
Although it is generally believed that
one Australian Brigade has won “the
King’s Colors,” the Defence Department
doesn’t know anything about it. Neither
the London Gazette nor Birdwood’s
orders, it says, have contained any
mention of the honor having been
conferred. Returned men are, however,
wearing the purple patch on the back of
their tunics, and those who are still occupied
on the other side have written to relatives
announcing that the Colors have been con-
ferred upon the Brigade for having fought
back to back at Bullecourt. “There are no
such thing as King’s Colors,” say the
authorities, though special privileges are
granted from time to time to regiments which
have won particular distinction.
Another fund with too little coming in
and too much going out! This one was
created in Kalgoorlie to give assistance to
workers incapacitated by lung troubles and
other mine ailments. To date nearly £30,000
has been disbursed in direct relief, but owing
to the number of subscribers having become
considerably less and the number of bene-
ficiaries considerably more, the fund is
exhausted and the distributors are faced with
a deficit of £2OOO. The mine-owners and
miners are willing to increases their subs,
provided the Government, which is the other
contributing party, agrees to do likewise;
but in view of the rotten financial condition
or the State the Mines Minister pleads his
inability to do anything at present. Mean-
time those in receipt of relief will have to
take what they can get.
“Double Entry”: Billjim home and look-
ing for a job deserves all the help we can
give him; but some Red Tape mandarins
seem determined to offer him all the discour-
agement they can supply. Two vetenftis
recently applied for humble positions worth
£3 apiece in the clerical division of the
Federal Service. Instead of being given
pens and desks after a five-minute test, they
were set an examination. After they had
puzzled over it they took the papers to a
Sydney actuary, who agreed with a member
of the examining committee of the Austral-
asian Corporation of Public Accountants that
any man who could puzzle the answers out
would be entitled to call himself an account-
ant, and that to ask a humble £3-a-week
bookkeeper such things was preposterous.
What boiled owl of the Federal Service is
responsible for this stupidity?
“T.D.C.”: Hyett, gen. sec. of the Vic.
Railway Unions, insists that his crowd don’t
want to confiscate war loans, but only in-
terest. What’s the difference? Hyett has
£5O in the Savings Bank, where it earns him
3| per cent. He takes it out and puts it
into a war loan. Suppose it there produces
nothing. If Hyett tries to sell it he will find
nothing doing. If the bond has a currency
of 20 years he will get now just as much as
will, with interest, produce £5O 20 years
hence. In other words, if you confiscate the
interest, you confiscate half the principal
every 20 years or so. Hyett should do some
more explaining.
“X.Y.” on the migration of a clan: —
As a member of the elan, I tell you the true story
of the Maedonells who landed at Port Albert (Vic.)
in 1842. The clan Glengarry suffered very small
loss in the Battle of Culloden, the chief withdrawing
his force after the defeat without loss of officers.
The Young Pretender, with that fateful talent of the
Stuarts, had offended Glengarry on the eve of the
battle by assigning to him a subordinate place in
the order of battle, posting him in the rear or on
an unexposed flank. As the Glengarry clan ha 3
always been among the storm troops of the Scottish
armies, the effect on the mind of “the best fighting
man on earth” may be imagined. The father of
the man who landed at Port Albert saw that the old
home of his clan was doomed, and he made efforts
to establish his people in other climes. Many had
already settled in Canada before his death, though
he could still put 500 men into the field at three
days’ notice. His patrimony was heavily in debt,
most of such debt having arisen from the circum-
stance that he had to keep a large proportion of his
clansmen on a war footing during the Napoleonic
wars, and his men gave a good account of themselves
at Quatre Bras and Waterloo. When he was pre-
paring to remove his people to Australia and Maori-
land he met his death by accident. His son and
heir took up the scheme of removing his people to
Australia, and, with that object in view, came to
Victoria to spy out the ground. Having decided
upon the land he returned lo Scotland, to organise
the removal of the clan; but on his return home
he found that his wife had proved unfaithful, and
this misfortune broke his heart and he returned to
these latitudes to die in Maoriland. With his death
the direct line to the Chieftainship of Glengarry and
the Lordship of the Isles became extinct.

BE PREPARED.
Like many other dangerous maladies diarrhoea often comes on unexpectedly and frequently
before the patient is aware of it some serious disorder has developed. During the warm
weather attacks of this kind are very frequent and often so swift in their results that life is in danger before the physician
can be summoned.
Those who have the interests of their family at heart keep a bottle of Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy on
hand for immediate use in cases of this kind. It is without doubt the best medicine prepared for diarrhoea, dysentery and
other disorders of the bowels. If given at the first unusual looseness of the bowels the attack may be avoided but even in
advanced stages it is a quick and most effective remedy.
Never leave home without a bottle of Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy in your luggage. The excitement
incidental to travelling and change of water and food often bring on diarrhoea, colic and pains in the stomach for which
speedy relief must be obtained and Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy can always be relied upon to do this.

Have Nerve, Not Nerves.
In these war times many people are living
in an unnatural condition of nervous excite-
ment. Unless something is done to correct it
the inevitable result will he a nervous break-
down, an exhaustion of nervous energy that
may take a very serious and unforeseen turn.
Neurasthenia is one such result, with its
accompaniment of worry, irritation, oversen-
sitiveness and severe headache. It means
that the patient has lost the power to recover
and store up energy. The Condition will
grow worse if not corrected.
The treatment is one of nutrition of the
nerve cells, requiring a non-alcoholic tonic.
As the nerves get their nourishment from the
blood the treatment must be directed towards
building up the blood. Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills act directly on the blood, and with pro-
per regulation of the diet have proved of the
greatest benefit in many cases of neuras-
thenia. A tendency to anaemia, or bloodless-
ness, shown by most neurasthenic patients, is
also corrected by these tonic pills.
Sufferers from nervous disorders who have
been taking treatment without benefit should
invstigate the tonic method. Write to-day

THE SLACKER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Of all the vast army of slackers,
Among the most noisy and smug—-
King-pin of the stay-at-home Jackas—
Is Montague Marmaduke Bugg.
He s married and well over forty,
He motors and races and “goffs,”
Affects an apparel that’s sporty,
And at all young shirkers he scoffs.
He oft at the Billions Club lunches
And wags a true patriot’s chin;
All know when rich viands he munches
The war he is helping to win.
He purchases coffee and ices
For girls owned by men who have gone;
Some flapper to tea he entices,
And chortles lie’s “carrying on.”
Most futile and feeble of friskers,
Past wars found him here in his place;
His one badge of courage the whiskers
Of grey that he wears on his face.
His feet may be colder than zero,
But age gives him pluck that’s sublime,
For Montague Bugg has a hero
Become by effluxion of time!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.
Once-famous Victorian Canon Nash is
now pastor of Prahran Congregational
Church.
The late Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat,
left one son, who dutifully followed in dad’s
priestly footsteps. The Rev. Harry is chap-
lain with the British in S’Africa.
Australian labor has a sort of representa-
tive in the House of Lords —-the Earl of
Hardwicke, who once worked as a miner on
TVestralian goldfields. He now has a billet
in the office of the Minister of Labor, llis
wife was a Maoriland girl—a Russell, of
Auckland.
A Maorilander was marked for distinc-
tion recently when R. G. Robinson, of the
Forestry Department, was elected an hon.
member of the Royal Scottish Arboricultural
Society. This is as far up the tree as any
overseas forester can climb. From Maori-
land Robinson swaps timber-talk with three
continents.
“Bert R.”: Dropped into a stationer’s
shop in Perth for a book of W.A. views to
send to friends in England, but did not buy.
I couldn’t; for inght on the title page
appeared the ridiculous legend: “Printed in
England.” I refuse to advertise my coun-
try’s laziness by posting such things ovei’-
sea.
Boracie acid, so much of which is used
by the retail butcher as a “sweetener” for
his sausages, has had a bad advt. as an
article of human diet. A Melbourne woman
took a quarter-pound in one lot the other
day, and the undertaker then attended to
the matter. But the Law still allows a free
use of the adulterant on the national dinner-
table. •
“Fi-ank Bentley” : “Copabella” (8.2/5/’18)
refers to the Shire of Upper Yarra’s appeal
for an independent tribunal to inquire into
loss4s-in-transit, etc., on Vic. railways. The
request for co-operation was made to 100
shires, and of these all but five have sailed in
to help. As the matter of victimisation
was mentioned it is noteworthy that after
the circular had gone abroad the very first
consignment for the shii’e which originated
the movement was a pump, carried at
owner’s risk. It was broken on arrival at
its destination. Of course it was only a
coincidence.
“Copabella”: These effete English union-
ists don’t know the beginnings of “class
consciousness.” The following is from the
rules of the Carpet-Weavers’ Association
(Kidderminster, Eng.) :
That each firm having members in this Associa-
tion be allowed one representative to attend meet-
ings, with the right to vote on all questions.
Poor backboneless beggars, it looks as though
they actually want to live in peace with the
boss! Imagine the Melbourne wharfies in-
viting Appleton to their meetings, or the
A.W.U. giving Edmund JoWett a voice in a
strike debate! No wonder the British
Laborites persist in wanting to fight
Prussianism, pay their debts, and do other
out-of-date things.
“Viking” : How splendidly we waste ! Our
environs are littered with tins, our pai’ks with
paper. Our back yards are repositories for
woollen and cotton rags and leather “scrap.”
Our factories and workshops are cumbered
with heaps of steel, iron and (to a lesser
extent) other metals. Nor does the crime
stop at “scrap” heans. The sugar-mills
throw :.way potential millions in molasses,
which could be transformed into treacle,
syrup, rum, methylated spirits or alcohol for
traction purposes. Our woolscours yearly
waste hundreds of thousands sterling of lano-
line. And that’s only the beginning of the
catalogue. There are huge fortunes awaiting
the men who will secure options over Aus-
tralia’s “rubbish.”
In the days of the first Revolution the
Cordoliers Club was a political power in
Paris. It began with a sane, comprehensive
reform programme, like the Australian
Labor party, but it drifted into I.W.W.
ways, also like the Australian Labor party.
Then Camille Desmoulins founded the Old
Cokdeliek, a paper which appeared semi-
occasionally and oftener when possible and
ran to eight or nine issues. It was a pas-
sionate appeal for a return to the old pro-
gramme. Desmoulins was expelled and
beheaded. Our own W. M. Hughes isn’t so
picturesque a figure as Desmoulins, and he
doesn’t write as he did, and he doesn’t seem
to care very much for the old programme,
and he hasn’t been beheaded as yet, but
otherwise there is some sort of a parallel.
“A.J.G.”: The object of the raid was to
secure prisoners, and, if possible, identify
the units opposing us. One Fritz stood
throwing bombs until our boys got close,
then cleared off down the trench with a long
Bananalander after him. They hadn’t gone
far before the .Hun stopped, threw up his
hands and yelled: “Mercy, cobber!” Billjim
went straight ahead. A sergeant shouted:
“Here, what did you do that for? He had
surrendered, hadn’t he?” “That’s all right,”
replied the bloke from Dawson Creek. “But
I wasn’t going to let a cow like that
call me cobber.”
