The Mountain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE said: “We were born ’neath the
Mountain,
My playmate and I
The stern and magnificent Mountain
That, towering on high,
Looks godlike ; a god on whose shoulders
Reposes the sky.
“When the dawn lit the brow of the Moun-
tain
We ran to behold
Its mantle of glamor and beauty,
Its roses and goiu,
AVhat time the fine mists of the morning
Gleamed spectral and cold.
“But the beauty that dwelt on the Mountain
Was mingled with awe.
In the moods that it donned through the
seasons
No softness we saw,
But the terrors of vengeance and justice-
implacable law.
“When we played at the foot of the Moun-
tain
We could not be gay,
For it seemed that the Mountain was
present,
Stern-browed at our play,
And banished the light and the laughter
Of childhood away.
“And the Mountain grew taller and vaster
When night settled down,
And stars came to sit on its shoulders
Arid rest on its crown ;
And it seemed ever more in the darkness
To mei-4tce and frown.
“We had ever great dread of the Mountain,
lts grandeur and gloom ;
For it seemed like the home of the shadows,
The birthplace of Doom,
And the fear of it entered our doorways
And shadowed each room.
“There was hate in the heart of the Moun-
tain
When, shrouded and cowled
In the garments of storm, in black anger
Above us it scowled,
Red lightnings around it, and thunders
That grumbled and growled.
“And as ever we looked on the Mountain
We said with a thrill,
That the Mountain sent Death down among
us
To silence and still;
For we felt that Death came from its
shadows,
Since Death was so chill.
“But we grew ripe for love ’neath the
Mountain,
My playmate and I,
And all in a moment rose-splendor
Fell down from the sky,
And the earth gave out scents, and on velvet
The winds whispered by.
“Yet we keep our love hid from the Moun-
tain,
As one keeps a dove
From the beak and the claws of an eagle
That hovers above;
For we know it is stern and stone-hearted,
And loves not our love.
“For the Mountain is mighty and god-like,
And massive and tall,
And great are its lights and its shadows,
And wondrous its thrall,
And we are so little and human,
And Love is so small.
“Yet we know that the Mountain shall
crumble
And perish away—
To. a mound of red earth in a desert
’Twill dwindle some day—
While Love shall endure through the ages,
Forever and aye.”
N.S.W.
RODIERIC QUINN.
The Unwritten Law.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If you say you’ll wait, wait. This is the law
of the hush.
“TPVON’T suppose you’ll find a big mob;
[I it’s not the time of day. You ought
to manage to make the Gohanna
Hole by dark. I’ll he there waiting for you.’’
Jim Marsland nodded; and, heading his
mare in the direction of No. 1 Mill, he rode
off.
The boss, Harry Gilmore, had told
Marsland to get what sheep he could find at
the windmill that watered No. 6 paddock
and to bring them down and join him at the
big watering-place known as Gohanna Hole.
The scrub all the way to the mill and
beyond it was varied in kind but uniform in
density. Ironbark, sandalwood, pine, coola-
bah, wattle and many other varieties were
as thick as they could stand.
Jim’s four dogs, a credit to the kelpie
breed, all except one poor specimen of black
and tan, followed their master, eager for
work. They were to get their fill very
shortly.
Marsland hummed - contentedly as he
jogged along, the rein over his arm, cutting
up tobacco and putting a little in the heel
of the old pipe, as he used to say.
He was getting near the mill now, and
something caused him to draw rein and
listen. “Sounds like there’s a bit o’ mus-
terin’ goin’ on,” he muttered to himself. He
lit his pipe and broke into an easy canter.
“Get behind, Skinny.” The owner of the
name slunk back —she had bounded forward
when her master increased his pace. The
bleating of a large mob of sheep was plainly
audible.
Emerging from the thick timber at last,
Marsland came to the vicinity of the mill.
For half a mile around the scrub had been
cut down to allow of the fan-wheel receiving
the full advantage of any breeze.
Round went the great wheel; but Mars-
L.nd knew exactly why 12,000 sheep were
congregated here at this time of day. Not
a drop of water came from the pipe jutting
out over the large corrugated-iron tank.
ihe bushman crossed his arms over the
pommel of his saddle and rested his forehead
them. It was roasting hot.
“Here’s a go,” he muttered. “Every hoof
in the paddock, I’ll swear, an’ one man to
drove em!”
He §ent Skinny round the left wing. and.
dismounting, led his horse round the right
ot tho nock to tlie mill.' ’Way bnck!” ho
called to Skinny, who obeyed, keeping wide.
Ihe sheep commenced to string out slowly
into the scrub. Marsland sat on his haunches
calling his dogs behind.
The mill had ceased to turn and every-
thing was still. A young wether advanced
to the little party, bleating and indignantlv
stamping his feet. The horse hung his head,
eyes half closed, switching lazily at the
flies with his long tail. Presently a roaring
sound came from the trees in the scrub. A
whirling pillar of dust and leaves circled
toward them across the clearing; then,
changing its course, it turned into a forest
of dead pine, leaving a path as it went.
Two things Aiarsland might do: go into
No. 10 and chance seeing the boss and other
musterers and get a hand. oi\undertake the
task before him. He decided instantly—he
had to get those sheep out.
The tail had not yet commenced to move
while the lead very slowly drew out, follow-
ing down the fences. The bushman looked
around him. Everything whs very dry.
Many sheep had to be left, being already
too weak to keep on their feet. These
Marsland had to kill. It was better so than
leave the poor wretches to the mercy of the
crow, eventually to perish in all the agony
in which these black devils would surely
leave them. Several even now were a cruel
sight, blood streaming from gaping eye-
sockets.
Hard work it is droving sheep in the great
heat of summer, and in any case the mob is
allowed to spell during the drowsy hours of
midday. Scarcely, indeed, “allowed” ; really
the sheep decide this matter themselves, and
miy effort of man or dog to move them is
in vain. This was a mob of ewes and lambs,
the worst of sheep to handle.
An hour had passed, and still occasionally
the clank of the broken mill could be faintly
heard in the heated stillness. They had not
made more than half a mile, though the lead
was a good mile and a half in advance.
The symptom which Jim had dreaded now
showed itself. Little groups of two or three
tarried by shady bushes until they were
urged on. It was impossible to force the
tail, where the weakest plodded on, heads
down and mouths open. Now the whole
mob commenced to bleat, and little groups
lay down in every available shade. Jim
used every effort in his power. He was
hoarse from shouting; be whistled and
clapped to keep them moving; but the end
had come. The mob commenced to surge
round in a ring. ,
The big bushman knew that any effort
now was useless. He let them ring, and
soon 12,000 weary and exhausted mothers
and lambs sank gasping to the ground.
The drover, too, lay down. Having un-
saddled and hobbled his horse and watered
the dogs from his neck bag, he drank ahd
stretched himself out in the shade of a green
wilga, and slept, leaving the dogs to watch.
Long before it was time to make a move
he awoke, and, there being sufficient water
for tea and another drink for the dogs, he
lit a fire and boiled the billy.
The sun was still striking down unmerci-
fully as they commenced to string off once
more in the direction of Gohanna Hole.
It is often necessary when droving ewes
and lambs to put an end to the weak young
that are unable to keep up with the flock.
It happened often this day. One by one
they fell exhausted. It was only one sudden
blow, which was not felt, and all was over;
but the big bushman had a heart as big as
his body, and it hurt him. Yet what else
was left to do? To save the mob he mtist
get on as fast as possible. Leave the weak
to the crow? Marsland preferred the lesser
of two evils.
Three times he attempted to give one of
the little stragglers a drink. Dismounting
and letting fall his reins, he picked up the
almost-spent form and tried to seize a ewe,
but failed, with the result that the rest,
frightened, charged and jambed themselves
into the centre of the flock. The next time
he caught a ewe but missed the lamb, and
ended up by saving the little creature a
drink of water from the dinted felt hat.
Little things like this had won for Mars-
land the love of all who knew him.
As the day grew cooler he pushed on
with renewed effort. He was determined to
make the south-west corner by night, so that
by running a wire or two he might make use
of it for a sheep break, picketing the dogs
across the corner. There would be no moon,
he knew, so that it would be impossible to
push on without a stop ; in any case his long
experience told him that in their present
condition they would never reach the water
without a rest, though he knew that that rest
must account for a number of lives by morn-
ing. But he must take his chance.
Had it not been for his dogs, the task
so far successfully carried out must have
proved impossible. They seemed to see their
master’s plight and did their best to help
him—all but one. the black-and-tan. He
followed behind, tongue dragging and refus-
ing to work. Marsland looked back at him
in disgust as the dog dim into the cooler
earth under a bush, spreading out his limbs
so that his body rested on the cold ground.
Jim whistled—not a move; neither did the
dog prick his ears in response. A new hand
would have been deceived into believing the
dog exhausted. Marsland scowled at him.
“Your tonsrue’s as red’s a strawberry,” he
muttered : “you’re no more knocked up than
I am.” Presently the mare started as a kan-
garoo-rat leapt up from her feet. In a
moment a flash of black and tan shot through
the scrub and a dog barked and yelped in nur-
suit. As he stooped under a low limb Mars-
land broke off a heavy waddy. Had the dog

Smarting, Soreness and Painful
Cracks Soothed and Healed.
What irritation arises from raw,
chapped hands! If the skin is at
all sensitive, some peonle suffer
more or less in this way all the
year round; but with the damp,
cold and frost of winter, the hands
and wrists become a positive tor-
ture. First, the skin loses its pleasant
softness, then there is a disagreeable
roughness and redness, followed by
cracking and bleeding, particularly in
the region of the knuckles and wrists.
This distressing condition is made worse
by drying the hands or face carelessly after
washing on a raw, cold day. Neglecting chaps
or any cold sores may lead to painful' swelling
and inflammation. There Is a danger of grit
and dirt getting into the cracks of the skin,
and setting up positive torture.
Tlie trouble is utterly beyond the reach of
toilet creams and cheap salves, which possess

THE thing that the people should have
been allowed to realise from the outset
is that WE MAY GET A LICKING. We
still may get a licking. Are you
let the Ravisher of Belgium tan without
lifting a hand ?
returned before his boss’s anger had sub-
sided there is no saying what might have
happened.
The corner now lay about two miles ahead.
Marsland rode wide round the flock and
busied himself running through the wires to
block the lead. It was now sunset. He
would make the corner with the tail nicely
by dark.
When the mob was settled he hobbled his
horse and lit a fire. Again putting a little
in the heel of the old pipe he lit up. There
was one comfort at least.
It had crossed his mind to ride over to
Gohanna Hole and inform the boss of the
broken mill and get assistance. But even
with four dogs to keep guard and an im-
provised break, it was too dangerous. Mad
for want of water, the flock would put for-
ward every endeavor to return to their
habitual watering-place. Marsland had seen
sheep get out of a I>reak before. If he were
to ride to Gohanna Hole and during his
absence the flock got out, it would mean the
loss of 12,000 sheep; to save them would
be impossible. Without anything to eat or
drink, and a lump in his throat, he paced
up and down in order to keep awake.
Then there was a murmur among the
flock. Two dogs pricked their ears, sat up,
and sniffed the air. II« wondered if it
might be the boss. “No, the boss will wait
at Gohanna,” lie muttered.
“More trouble!” he thought to himself.
The howl.pf a dingo broke on his ears.
A thought struck Jim, and, walking across,
he loosed Jack, the blaek-and-tan.
“You’ve been good for nothing—we’ll give
you a try at this.”
Holding him by the scruff of the neck he
led him a little way way from the flock and
listened. A rustle sounded near the fence.
“ ’Way back !” ordered Jim. Releasing his
hold, the dog bounded into the scrub.
Not a sound followed for a long while.
Then a dog barked and yelped away through
the tall oak.
In half an hour Jack returned without a
mark, but puffing hard. “Useless to the end,”
Jim said, slipping the chain over his head.
It was a tired man who pulled out the
wires next morning. The mob strung out
along the fence through the opening as he
walked back to the tail.
Sixteen ewes were left behind and 37
lambs. Of the ewes nine were dead. Three
were able when assisted'" to their feet to fol-
low on. The other four met the inevitable.
Marsland carried one lamb with him.
Had not God been unmerciful! A cloudy
day or a shower of rain to cool the air would
have helped, but with all its awful heat the
sun baked down upon them all day. The
massacre among lambs and even ewes now
became constant. It was impossible to even
use a dog at all. Marsland walked; his
horse followed behind. He picked up one
ewe after another and put them on their
feet. He could not speak; his throat was
sore and parched, his tongue so swollen that
he nearly choked, his eyes half-blinded with
dust. At 10.30, as on the previous day, they
“ralng” and camped. He would not reach the
hole before six o’clock: could he hold out?
If any man could, it was Marsland.
He dreaded the start, knowing so well
what it meant, and he had had his fill of
killing. But it had to be done. So he poked
along in the direction of the water, losing
another 27 sheep and 45 lambs.
As he trudged behind, his eyes abnormally
large, Marsland strained his ears for a sound.
At last it came —the sound that goes up
from a mob of perishing sheep on smelling
water. Springing upon his horse. Marsland
galloped to steady the racing lead. Making
full use of his dogs, and galloping up and
down, he held them in check along a line
nearly a mile long.
He had kept up this frantic work until he
feared he had left it too late and was going
mad. Turning his mare, a lather of sweat,
he galloped to Gohanna Hole.
“Hold them back, for God’s sake, hold
them back !” he shouted, and falling from his
horse rolled to the edge and drank.
The boss, who was clearing the banks of
10,000 sheep to make room for the new-
comers, rode up to Jim, now prostrate on
the bank.
“You’ve had a rough spin, Jim.” he said.
“Twelve thousand ! Mad ! Keep them
back!” gasped Jim.
“Ted is holding them,” replied Gilmore—
“you galloped past him.”
There was a silence, then Jim spoke again.
“You waited, Boss!”
“You are twenty-six hours late, my boy,”
he returned. “But had it been fifty-six I
would have waited.”
This is the law of the bush.
SELWIN KEMPSEY.
With the Whalers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A JET of spray thrown funnel-wise'
from the oily stretch of sea. A broad
grey back broke water; with deep,
roaring snorts the rorqual filled his lungs
before he descended to the feeding-grounds.
The waves had not yet subsided. _ Foam-
flecked ripples eddied and surged in con-
fusion.
Across the sea went a lusty hail, rousing
listless whaling-hands in the for’ard galley.
“There she blows!”
They tumbled up in eager haste. _ Some-
where on that expanse of sea, within a
radius of tw r o or three miles, a whale had
been sighted, and the crew had marked him
down as theirs. Already the bo’sun had told
the cook that the rorqual -would go fifty feet,
while the cook was sure he was a blue-
noser. Nay, more than sure —lie was ready
to bet on it.
Only the look-out remained silent as he
scanned the water in every direction. The
whale had sounded half a mile off the vewnlS
bows. Shortly he would reappear, buV)jT.t>
what point the look-out could not tell. ' A
whale manoeuvres under water, frequently
doubling back on his course.
This one submerged for ten minutes, and
when his fins broke water the vessel was a
mile to leeward. , ,
“Port your helm! Gunners, stand by!
A bright, scarlet gun had been uncovered
in the bows and was run out and charged.
To the shell a ponderous harpoon had been
attached, the haft connected with a strong
hempen rope coiled for’ard.
The little vessel crept nearer, buffeting
through the swell. There was something
sinister about her approach.
In the crow’s-nest Scotty, the look-out,
cursed volubly as he noticed that the distance
between pursuer and pursued was not lessen-
ing. The w T hale was sheering off—making
out to sea. Threshing the water he raced
away, crossing in front of the vessel. He
was evidently an outlaw, vanquished in a
conflict with another of his kind and ban-
ished from the school. Thenceforth his life
had been one of storm and trouble; the life
in which the passion for fight had been
engendered. To the inexperienced his fury
was appalling. Twice he leaped, his huge
body cleaving the water and descending with
a vast upheaval of spray.
But the little ship crept on like a blood-
hound stalking the quarry.
Scotty leaned forward in the crow’s-nest
and bellowed into the mouthpiece: “South
by east a point. Keep her nose inshore.”
The vessel was running parallel with the
breakers, the whale between her and shore.
Now it was a trial of speed. The whale
still held the lead, although by no great
margin. Often he sounded and doubled back
on his course, misleading his pursuers.
The sun’s rays were slanting down behind
the bleak head of Cape Green, firing the
waves with crimson blushes. The race was
almost run.
As the tired monster relaxed his efforts
and awaited his enemy’s approach the look-
out issued a welcome command.
“Heel her over. Furl fore and aft sails!”
The clipper, now under bare poles, glided
slowly nearer.
The blue-nose awaited his enemy in seem-
ing derision. He would not flee inshore. As
an outlaw he had faced in triumph dangers
great as this. His tail flailed the water
angrily; then, as the gunner sprang his
weapon, the rorqual lowered his head and
prepared to sound. The huge flukes disap-
peared, and with a crash the weapon spoke.
“Got him! Got him!” came from every
mouth.
A cable had been attached to the harpoon-
line, and as the wounded creature submerged
it ran out slowly, checked by the galley
winch. One hundred fathoms ran by. Deep
down the whale was writhing and struggling.
His breath spent, he rose to the surface to
blow a crimson spray. Exulting, they
hoisted sail and crept nearer, coiling in the
slackened rope. It was all over. The
whale rolled on his side, flicking his tail in
impotent fury.
Not twenty yards separated them. Fifteen
yards and then ten. The steersman asked if
lunch was ready. For’ard the gunner, an
experienced whaler, reloaded his weapon in
haste. Too many times had he been in at
the death.
It all happened as he had expected. With
a final plunge the whale broke the harpoon-
rope, and then, wheeling, hurled his huge
bulk at the whaler. A gasp of alarm was
drowned by the sharp crack of the detonator.
A shower of crimson spray; a cascade of
water that seethed and eddied for a moment.
Then silence.
So they brought in their catch to the
whaling-station.
E. W. DEBENHAM.
Luna Di Miella.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BIG yellow moon that hears the old
confession.
Burning but patient swain who waits
alone;
Waits as a lover who has not possession,
Burning as coal and patient as a stone,
Great golden moon, moon of the lover wait-
ing,
Brimful of honey that shall sweeten mating.
Light that sings alto at the throne of love
(No nr'ddle range, content and close to
earth),
Tonic to dominant the song to prove,
And give to lyric love a new rebirth;
No middle range in this—no sweets that
cloy—
But high and higher, tiptoe up to joy.
Level dark brows, as she of Sicily,
Velveted eyes and delicate, proud nose;
Her face, of Tuscan flowers the memory;
Her mouth, a full voluptuous Roman rose;
And night she carries with her, scented rare,
In that fine shroud, the curtain of her hair.
Dryad and woman she, honey and wine,
Clinging as virgin, bold as any boy;
Desire of her is the desire divine
That makes love’s distaff spin its hour of joy ;
Full of strong wine of love, beyond all
reason,
Through golden hours of love’s short summer
season.
All walls yield unto steel or gold, or love
That robs not any and is fair exchange;
O honey planet that dost sweetly move,
Bring joy to lovers all within thy range!
For love is urgent as the flowers in Spring,
And, ere we know it, summer will take wing.
Wine, bread, a friend, an enemy, a kiss
Are all the gifts that life has in his giving;
Are all that life can furnish; all life is.
To die of love’s a pleasant end of living—
To die while death can come to find us
strong,
After love’s silence "and love’s undersong.
O yellow moon, deep mirrored in the river,
Be thou the moon of honey, for our loving;
The curving stream in thy full light aquiver
With futile passion for the beam that’s
moving:
Moon that drips honey, as her lips fall sweet-
ness,
tWane not, O honey month, ere our com-
pleteness !
O moon of honey! Roses bring, and wine!
Garlands to loop the temple and the bed!
Let not death be until with love we dine,
Nor funeral lilies, linen shroud, nor lead.:
Let there be trumpets, for the bell strikes
soon
To wane thy light, O golden, honeyed moon!

sheep-yards is, in my opinion,' a far better method than doing the work at
the woolshed, as the saving for the lambing ewes in driving alone is very
considerable.”
